Loan and PrQjeci Summay
Boffower        :  Republic of Indonesia
1,oan Amount       US$ 125.0 million equivalent
Twenty years, including five years of grace, at the Bank's standard variable
interest rate.
project Description:  The main project objective is to achieve potential benefits from irrigated
agricultural development by completing and fully developing ongoing small
and mnedium scale irrigation schemnes in 13 provinces on the Outer Islands of
Indonesia. A related objective is to assist development of institutional capacity
to enable improved coordination of irrigation with agriculture and other related
services. To meet the above objectives, the proposed project would comprise:
(a) survey, investigations and design; (b) completion of irrigation works on
some 130 schemes serving about 273,000 ha, includirg construction of minor
canal and drainage systems and preparation for operation and maintenance
(PROM); (c) land clearance and development of about 40,000 ha; (d)
agricultural development activities including seed multiplication programs,
trials for problem soils and training of farmer groups; (e) land titling and
certification; (f) environmental protection including forest surveys and erosion
control; (g) settlement; and (h) coordination and management of irrigated
agricultural development.
Benefits and Risks:  The project should generate substantiai incremental benefits from the low cost
mninor works and land development required to complete schemes. The
benefits derive from increased crop production, improved performance and
sustainability of irrigation schemes and better coordination and improved
management. The primary benefit would be increased agricultural (rice)
production and the main beneficiaries would be farmers with land within the
design command area of schemes, but which has yet to be served by irrigation
and fully developed. Major quantifiable benefits at full development would be
incremental annual production of about 1,100,000 tons of paddy. About
127,000 rural families would benefit directly from the new areas brought
under irrigation with some improvements for an additional 100,000 families
presently receiving an irrigation service. The project would settle some 1400
families on schemes in Sulawesi and Kalimantan. The project should result in
significant indirect benefits in more isolated regions and generate important
socio-economic benefits. It would also alleviate oroverty as about 30 percent
of the beneficiaries are recent transmigrants or landless local settlers. The main
project risks, generally known from 1-ssons learnt under previous operations,
relate to instfi itional coordination, quality control of planning, design and
implementation, whether all farmers will fully utilize irrigated and developed
land, and whether agricultural extension and the development follow-up would
be satisfactory. These risks are acceptable given the Government's recognition
and commitment to achieving full benefits from irrigated agricultural
development and the willingness to implement necessary policy actions.

INDONESIA
PROVINCIAL IRRIGATED AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
I. PROJECT AND SECTOR BACKGROUND
Background
1.01    The large expansion of irrigation infrastructure in Indonesia over the last two decades has
made a major contribution to bringing Indonesia to self-sufficiency in rice. However, most of this
success has been on Java with a long established tradition of irrigated agriculture. It has not been
matched by a similar performance on the Outer Islands. Furthermore the expansion in infrastructure
has only recently been accompaniea by a country-wide effort to maintain and operate irrigation
schemes efficiently. On the Outer Islands, small and medium scale schemes remain incomplete and
agricultural development has lagged because: (a) financing and implementation have been piecemeal
and uncoordinated; (b) the construction stage was generally entered without adequate design and
planning; and (c) there has been little incendve for the implementing agencies involved to focus on
achieving potential benefits rather than continuing expenditure programs.
1.02    Recognizing the lack of returns to the large sunk investments in irrigated agriculture, the
Government of Indonesia (GOI) has requested Bank assistance for a Provincial Irrigated Agriculture
Development Project (PLADP) to: (a) finance a five year program of completion and full development
of selected on-going irrigadon schemes on the Outer Islands; and (b) implement and consolidate
institutional arrangements at central and provincial levels to enable full potential benefits to be achieved
from irrigated agricult.. I development in a timely manner.
Agricultural Sector
1.03    Agriculture remains thc key sector of the Indonesian economy, contributing about 21 percent
of GDP (in constant terms), providing employment to some 55 percent of the labor force and
generating about 56 percent of the non-oil exports. Food crops constitute the largest subsector,
contributing 52 percent of the agricultural GDP. Rice, the staple food and dominant crop in
Indonesia, accounts for about 30 percent of the agricultural GDP. Growth in agricultural production
has re-attained the level of about 4.0 percent achieved during the 1975-85 decade, after a decline of
between 2.0 and 2.5 percent in 1986 and 1987. A growth of 5.5 percent was achieved for rice
production in 1987 as a result of good rice prices and rainfall, and increases of 3.0 and 2.3 percent in
area planted and yields respectively. The area increase was partly due to some 45,000 ha of previously
designated sugarcane area reverting to rice and to farmers responding to higher rice prices by shifting
out of soybeans and comn.
1.04    Rice was the most important crop in the sector growth over the last two decades.
Consequently, the expansion in irrigated command area has made a major contribution to taking the
economy over the threshold of food security. Paddy production of some 41.6 million tons in 1987 is
more than double the 1970 level, with the increase coming mainly from higher irrigated crop yields.
Paddy yields in many areas of Java with full and reliable irrigation now average 4.5 to 5.0 ton/ha and
are amongst the highest in Asia. Paddy yields are lower on the Outer Islands, but are still in the order
of 3.5 to 4.0 ton/ha where full and reliable irrigation is available. Although higher yields account for
about 75 percent of the rice production increases, the harvested area has also increased by some
30 percent since 1970. National yields for irrigated paddy average about 4.2 ton/ha compared to an
average of about 1.7 ton/ha under rainfed conditions. Although precision is impossible, according to
recent Government studies, irrigation investments have contributed some 20 to 30 percent of the
growth in rice production since 1970. This has happened in combination with other inputs, such as
improved variedes, increased ferdlizer use and extension services on the existing cropped area.



- 2 -
1.05    Agriculture continues to be the main source of income of the majority of the rural population
in Indonesia who comprises about 75 percent of the total population. After a decade and a half of
impressive performance, (some 4.0 percent growth per annum), the sector is now adjusting to lower
prices for commodities, resource constraints on public sector support, reduced scope 'or expansion of
migation infrastructure, and the possibility that rice may no longer be the engine for growth in the
sector. The major issue in agriculture is how to sustain growth, employmtnt, income and exports in
the sector on an efficient basis. For agriculture to continue to play a major role in growth, employment
and poverty alleviation, the best prospect will be through greater economic efficiency, intensificadon,
and a more diverse pattern of production. This would entail a changing role for the Government,
geared towards providing support services and intervening less in pricing, marketing and trade.
Irrigation Subsecto
1.06    Surfac Esiadon. The total irrigated aea in Indonesia is estimated at about 5.5 million
hectares with about 1.1 million ha of village-operated irrigation area and some 4.4 million ha of public
sector irrigation. The designated area under public irrigation includes some 2.3 million ha of technical
irrigated area, 1.3 million ha of semi-technical area, and 0.8 million ha of simple irrigated amea 1/.
However, since most irrigation schemes are run-of-ihe-river without storage, in any particular year,
the actual service area may be less. Thus, at present, the percentage of irrigated land classified as
technical exceeds 50 percent of all public irrigated land.
1.07    Goundwater. The estimated potential for groundwater development in Indonesia, presently
estimated at about 175,000 ha, is limited by the lack of suitable aquifers. Up to a decade ago, there
was little private development except through hand-dug and manually operated wells and mninor
development on sugar estates. As of 1986, only about 25,000 ha have been developed with about
15,000 ha by public sector and 10,000 ha by privately financed shallow we'ls in East and Central
Java. Groundwater technology has been extended to the Outer Islands only on a very limited basis.
1.08    Over the last two decades, irrigation expenditures have amounted to some Rp 13 trillion
(1988 prices), equivalent to over US$ 7.0 billion, of which about one-half comprised external
assistance from bilateral and multilateral sources. In addition to the Bank, with loans of some US$ 1.6
billion for 24 operations (para 2.04), several other external donor agencies are engaged in supporting
irrigation development in Indonesia. These include ADB and bilaterals (USAID, OECF, Australia,
CIDA, Netherlands, Italy). The coordination between these agencies, whether it entails parallel
financing/cofinancing, or support for future strategies, has been generally good.
Isiutina Franmework
1.09    The Ministries of Public Works (MPW) and Agriculture (MOA) are the two major institutions
involved in irrigated agriculture development. Within MPW, the Directorate General of Water
Resources Development (DGWRD) is responsible through the two Directorates (DOI - and II) for the
planning, design and construction of irrigation schemes. Within the Provincial Public Works (DPU),
the provincial water resource services (PRIS) are responsible for construcdon, and operation and
maintenance of the public irrigation schemes. The chief of PRIS is administratively responsible to the
Provincial Governor, through the chief of DPU. However, for all technical and personnel matters, the
PRIS chief is under the jurisdiction of the DGWRD. Each PRIS, at its provincial headquarters,
includes sections for planning, design, construction and O&M. Within the MOA, the Directorate
General of Food Crop Agriculture (DGFCA), through the Directorate of Agricultural Area
Development (DAAD) has responsibility for land clearance and development with the Directorate of
Food Crop Agricultural Extension (DFCAE) responsible for agricultural extension and other inputs.
1/  Technical areas have full Government control to tertiary canal level; sei-technical areas have
permanent canals with Govemment control to main canal level; and simple irrigated schemes have
few permanent structures and are usually farner managed.



The Provincial Agricultural Services (PRAS or DIPERTA) have responsibility for the various
agricultural activities at provincial level through subservice u. its reflecting national level organization.
1.10    Other institutions at the national level include: (a) BAPPENAS (through its Agriculture and
Irrigation Bureau) with responsibility for planning, budget coordination and monitoring; (b) BP'N, the
National Land Board, which is responsible for land titling and certification through central staff based
in the provinces; (c) the Ministry of Transmigraton (MOT) with provincial staff responsible for
settlement of transmigrants; and (d) the Ministry of Forestry (MOF) with provincial staff responsible
under the proposed project for forestry aspects of irrigation development and environmental protection
of natural resources.
1.11    At the provincial level, the BAPPEDA. which is responsible for planning, coordinating and
monitoring, reports to he Governor through the Chairman of BAPPEDA, as do the other provincial
staff through the chiefs of the respective provincial agencies. The BAPPEDA is structured along
sectoral lines with the Agriculture and Physical Infrastructure divisions sharing responsibility for
inigated agriculture. An important function of BAPPEDA is the planning and budget coordination of
the various line agencies involved in an inter-agency development such as irrigated agriculture.
Challenges Facing the nrrigation Subsector
1.12    Notwithstanding Indonesia's achievements in developing its food crop subsector, its present
position is tcnuous, particulariy with regards to rice self sufficiency and the growing population. The
easy opportunities for irrigated agriculture have been exploited. The challenges stem from .imited
expansion potential for irrigation, which is now primarily on the Outer Islands. In addition, there are
emerging competing demands on water resources, primarily on Java, where most of the effective
irrigated agriculture is located. Another challenge is the need for an effective interface and coordination
between the institutions responsible for agriculture and irrigation infrastructure.
1.13    Limits on Expansion of Irrigation Network. The costs of irrigation investment have
increased steadily as the portfolio of less expensive projects has oeen depleted and potential areas for
new construction have shifted primarily to off-Java locations. Bank commodity price projections
indicate declining real rice prices and increasing fertilizer prices beyond the year 2000. This,
accompanied by levelling otr in yields and no major breakthrough in rice research on the horizon,
could make the achievement of rice self-sufficiency an objective which will be difficult to sustain.
Furthermore, it is not known for certain what expansion in irrigated area is needed to maintain growth
in rice production in line with potential domestic demand. Some estimates indicate that an irrigation
development program which wvould create new service areas of some 25-35,000 ha per annum would
suffice to maintain balanced growth in rice production (ADB, 1987). However, there are other
estimates from Government agencies, which indicate that as high as 100,000 ha per annum of new
area should be brought under irrigation.
1.14    Complefing Existing Irrigation Schemes. Various studies have also shown that a further
300,000-400,000 ha could be irrigated from existing incomplete irrigation schemes, if there is
adequate and coordinated land and tertiary development (para 5.15). This opportunity to expand the
irrigated area should have high priority because it would be relatively low in cost, takes advantage of
large sunk invest. ,ents in existing irrigation infrastructure, and can be implemented quickly. Thus,
perhaps the mo-. important challenge in the irrigation subsector, particularly on the Outer Islands, is to
strengthen the interface and coordination between irrigation and agriculture. This would also help to
strengthen the institutional capacity to cffectively plan, execute, manage and then operate and maintain
new irri,ated command area.
1.15    Competing Demands on Water Resources. Water requirements are not only for irrigated rice
and nLn-rice crops. It is also required for: (a) aquaculture in fresh and brackish water ponds, rivers
and lakes; (b) municipal water supply for drinking and urban, industrial and commercial uses;
(c) flushing and low flow maintenance; (d) hydro power generation; and (e) inland navigation and
recreational use. Except for hydro power generation and recreation, all other uses of water are



-4.
out-of-stream uses. The need is becoming acute, particularly in Java, for protecdon and conservation
of catchment areas, increased attention to water quality, and improved management ol r.ver basin water
resources and their allocation. This wili become increasingly important given the growing shortages of
dry season water supply, which can adversely affect productionl of the second season paddy crop.
I,. BANK INVOLVEMENT AND LESSONS LEARNED
Bank Involvement in the Sector
2.01    Experience with irrigation development, not only in Indonesia but in many other countries as
well, demonstrates that the subs^ctor is facing a number of technical, financial, institutional and human
resource challenges. The Annual Review of Evaluation Results for 1989 2/ suggested that successful
irrigation projects have yielded important long-term benefits, particularly in supplementing national
food supplies and raising income, which were also the major thrusts of Indonesian Government
investment programs for irrigation, due to cridcal shortages and large imports of rice in the 1970's.
The review also found that the overall operational perfornance of irrigation systems has been poorer
than anticipated at the time of project appraisal and completion. The physical infrastructure has often
deteriorated leading in some cases to a long term decline in production Fnd low efficiency in water use.
Inadequate O&M funding and institudonal and organizational problems have contributed to this
shortfall in performance. The review suggested that the Borrowers concerned have often disengaged
too early from projects, leaving irrigation and drainage systems incomplete and land development
unfinished. For maiy projects, complementary services (e.g. extension, research, marketing) and
beneficiary participation, associated with the productivity of irrigation development, have been
inadequate or seriously delayed.
2.02    These shortcomings are also present in Indonesia. Many problems, in particular technical
problems, are project specific. However, the solution to some of the causes of poor performance are
generic subsector issues extending beyond the boundaries of individual projects, relating to public
finance, human resource policy and institutional organization and development. The major issues at
present facing the irrigation subsector in Indor.esia, and the lessonL learnt under Bank projects, are
described below. It is, however, worth noting that irrigation development has played a critical role in
Indonesia achieving rice self-sufficiency, and rice has provided a major impetus to growth in the
agriculture sector.
Issues and Lessons Learnt in Irrigation Development in Indonesia
2.03    Irrigation has absorbed a major part of Bank lending for Indonesian agriculture over the past
two decades with loans/credits of some US$ 1.6 billion for 24 operations (Table 2.1). During the
decade of the 1970s, the Bank supported: (a) rehabilitation/modernmzation of irrigation and drainage
system, construction of new irnigatiout and drainage systems, a multi-purpose dam, and construction of
tertiaries; (b) institutional strengthening and staff development, although with limited impact; and (c)
policies aimed at increasing generation of land/property tax revenues, through land reclassification and
valuation. More recently, especially under the Irrigation SubsecLor Project (ISSP I), Loan 2880-IND,
the increased focus of Bank lending has been on improved O&M of existing systems; suppoit for
policies entailing turnover of small public schemes to farmers' groups; and the introduction of an
irrigation service fee. These policies are aimed at increasing efficiency and sustainability of
investments by reducing dependence on central government budgets for O&M funding, imnproving
resource allocation and increasing beneficiary participation.
21   Annual Review of Evaluation Results for 1989, Agriculture and Rural Development Sector
Working Paper, OED, August 29, 1990.

2.05    Maior Lessons in Indonesian Irrigation. These lessons fall into four broad categories -
technical, institutional and human resource, financial and environmental - and include, inter alia:
(a) quality control; (b) Government's lack of priority for non-construction and O&M activities;
(c) institutional and human resource constraints; (d) coordination of the irrigation-agriculture
programs; (e) monitoring and evaluation of irrigadon system performance and the impact of irrigation
on agricultural production; (f) local funding constraints, use of available funds and cost recovery
mechanisms; (g) beneficiary participation in formalized water users organizations; and (h) environ-
mental impact.
2.06    Technical Constraints. Insufficient physical resources data (hydrology and soils), at the
evaluation of the technical feasibility of many irrigation projects, has resulted in flaws in planning and
design. These combined with deficiencies in construction have caused huge periodic reconstruction
expenditures that are often treated as O&M. Priorities have not been geared to improving operations,
but on the degree of physical disrepair. Maintenance activities are generally uncoordinated and often
do not relate to efficiency or operational objectives. An underlying problem is that many schemes,
particularly on the Outer Islands, have never been fully completed and thus the irrigation systems are
generally inadequate to achieve full benefits of irrigation. Because there is little accountability for how
or whether existing schemes function as planned and designed, inefficient irrigation management has
developed. Government and the Bank are addressing the O&M related issues, including
institutionalizing processes whereby plans for O&M are in place, operational procedures are simplified
and schemes are verified and certified that they operate as designed, before they are handed over to
provincial O&M authorities. For future projects, whose preparation is supported by the Bank, a
concerted effort is in place to ensure adequate physical resource data.
2.07    Institutional and Human Resource Constraints. The developmental impact of the irrigation
subsector depends on effective coordination and cooperation between the Ministries of Public Works,
Agriculture, Home Affairs, Transmigration and the Provincial authorities. On the Outer Islands, in
particular, coordination is not effective. Each agency follows its own budget provision (which for
development activities are directed by parent agencies in Jakarta), program and priorities and functions
as if each project activity was self-contained. In addition, no agency involved in irrigated agricultural
development undertakes a regular evaluation of either its own performance or its contribution to the
overall benefits from irrigated agriculture.
2.08    The planned central approach, followed until recently, of most ministries/agencies involved
in irrigation development tends to be non-participatory. As a result, an inability to provide adequate
staff incentives, limited career prospects and insufficient resources contribute to the staffing problems.
These are compounded by unclear accountability and Government's heavy dependence on consultants
who substitute for shortages of technical skills within the public services. The impact of training,
supported by various donors, is also unclear. In this respect, the Bank is supporting improved
monitoring and coordination of consultancies by Govemment and the assignment of suitable
government staff to work with consultants to facilitate transfer of knowledge.
2.09    It is generally not possible to evaluate with any certainty the impact of projects or institutional
management because of lack of reliable data, reporting and monitoring. Most progress reporting of
either schemes or individual contracts relates to monitoring budget allocations and expenditures with
little useful data on performance of investments. As a consequence, there is a lack of diagnostic
analysis on performance of both irrigation service and the related agricultural impact.
2.10    There has been inadequate participation in the irrigation subsector by the private sector,
including the local community. While informal farmer groups are found in irrigated areas in all
provinces, more formalized organizations are required. An adequate legal basis for WUA
organizations, and effective catalysts in getting such organizations started and functioning are needed.
On the Outer Islands, delays in land and agricultural development of many schemes has also
contributed to non-participation of farmer beneficiaries. In this respect, under the ISSP (Loan 2880-
IND), the Bank is supporting the establishment and strengthening of WUAs so that they can operate,
maintain, manage and own small schemes previously under public sector management. In addition,



-7 -
legal and administrative procedures needed to facilitate the turnover of small public schemes to farmer
groups.
2.11    Financial Constraints. Insufficient and unpreaictable local funding for key activities has been
an important constraint, particularly for land acquisition, settlement and agricultural development,
which are not usually supported by donors under irrigation projects. The generic funding problems
have consisted of two parts, namely availability of funds and the budgetary process. In general, few
schemes have full and secure financing commitments when they are started.
2.12    Until recently, the cost recovery mechanism was through the land tax (IPEDA/PBB), of
which only a small percentage was allocated for O&M of irrigation and drainage systems. There have
been some initiatives towards collecting irrigation service fees in a few areas. More recently,
Government changed its position and the service fee is being collected in pilot schemes under the
ISSP I (Loan No. 2880-IND). This is expected to reduce the dependence of provincial and local
governments on financial transfers from the central government. The fee will be linked to actual
financial requirements for O&M in specific irrigation schemes and water users encouraged to take
greater responsibility for their systems. The Bank is strongly supporting these recent efforts by
Government.
2.13    Environmental Constraints. There has been limited consideration of the environmental
impact of irrigated agricultural development, primarily because irrigation projects have been generally
planned, designed and implemented as civil works rather than developmental operations. As a result,
nominal attention have been given to the effect of irrigation development on the surrounding area, or to
the long term environmental impact. Consequently, the need is becoming acute, paricularly in Java, to
protect and conserve natural resources and catchment areas. In addition, the growing competition for
water between the various users has increased the problems associated with water quality and
managerment of river basin water resources, including groundwater, and their allocation. Finally,
involuntary resettlement ac.ivities are not always planned and implemented with a thorough analysis of
viability, sustainability, and sensitivity to the needs of the population adversely affected. The
proposed project has been designed to help Govemment meet these challenges.
2L.14    Determinants of Sustainabilitv. Resolution of the above issues and lessons learnt are pre-
requisites for sustainable development in the irrigation subsector in Indonesia. As expressed in OED's
Annual Review for 1989, the achievement of sustainability requires attaining a balance across all forms
of capital participating in irrigation development. Based on the lessons learnt and the underlying
causes of the problems, the Government, with the Bank's participation, has identified a strategy to deal
with these issues.
Proposed Strategv and Rationale for Future Bank Involvement
2.15    Government Objectives and Strategy. Government's objectives for rice-based farming
systems in irrigated lowlands are to: maintain food security, facilitate crop diverstification during the
second crop season, alleviate poverty and support regional development. Government's 1987 Policy
Statement for the Irrigation Subsector defined these irrigation policy objectives and laid a basis for
irrigation subsector priorities. These are: (a) introduce appropriate O&M practices/procedures so that
irrigation systems can be sustained in the future; (b) increase local revenue generation and cost
recovery from beneficiaries; (c) turn over (privatize) smaller public irrigation schemes, which are now
operated by Government; (d) rationalize planning, programming and budgetary procedures; and (e)
complete viable high-priority projects, particularly those with large sunk investments. In carrying out
the latter, Government has more recently also expressed its intention of focusing increasingly on the
completion and integration of agricultural development activities along with the physical infrastructure
so as to realize fully and more quickly the potential agricultural benefits.
2.16    These initiatives enabled Govemment to undertake appropriate action programs and
expenditures throughout the subsector, and for the Bank to support a program of essential reforms. A
first phase Irrigation Subsector Project (ISSP, Loan 2880-IND, US$ 234 nillion) became effective in



-8-
January 1988 and is being implemented over three years. The project represents a shift from area
specific or provincial operations to a national focus, and from construction of new irrigation systems to
improved operation and maintenance of existing projects. It has set in motion a process of
improvement in O&M funding, rationalization of budgeting/programming procedures and investment
strategies, improved cost recovery, and institutional strengthening. More recently, in response to the
lessons learnt, primarily from the Second Provincial Irrigation Development Project (SPIDP, Loan
2375-IND, PCR No. 8336, dated January 26, 1990), Government has focused on completion and full
irrigated agricultural development of ongoing schemes, especially in the Outer Islands. The objective
is to realize more quickly than in the past, potential benefits from sunk investments in river diversion
structures and main canal systems. The Government concern with incomplete irrigation development
is reflected by: (a) the priority given to the proposed project; (b) a basic policy change to make land
development a budget item to catch up with the backlog of undeveloped land within irrigation
command areas; and (c) actions to develop and implement arrangements and procedures for
coordination/management at the national and provincial levels.
2.17    Bank Strategy. The Bank fully supports Government's objectives in the agriculture sector of
a sustained growth and reduction in rural poverty, increased productive employment opportunities in
rural areas and a more balanced regional distribution of growth. Irrigated agriculture will continue to
be important to achieve these objectives, although the emphasis will continue to shift towards
achieving growth through greater efficiency in water use, and more diverse patterns of production.
Even in the rice-based farming systems in irrigated lowlands, there is a scope for crop diversification
in the second crop season; in the third season, crops are already diversified. This will become
increasingly important to meet the future consumption requirements and to maintain food security.
2.18    The Bank strategy is to support Government's strong focus on: (a) ensuring efficiency and
sustainability of past irrigation investments, particularly in Java, where the expansion potential is
somewhat limited; (b) improving management and allocation of water in view of competing demands
on water resources, already emerging in Java; and (c) assessing the potential for and supporting the
expansion of irrigated areas, particularly in the Outer Islands, as needed to sustain growth in
production of food grains required to meet the growing and changing consumption demand. The Bank
would focus most in areas of institutional structure, organizational strengthening and human resource
development, particularly in the context of multipurpose management of water on Java, and in the
Outer Islands where the provincial capacities are limited. Private sector involvement in irrigation
would be fostered through privatization of small public irrigation schemes and private development of
groundwater resources in areas with shallow aquifers. Specific, area-targeted interventions would be
supported to promote objectives of regional equity and poverty alleviation.
2.19    The Bank strategy would be pursued through a coordinated lending program with each
operation focussed on important and complementary objectives.
(a)  The proposed Provincial Irrigated Agriculture Development Project would support the
integrated development and completion of schemes and institutional mechanisms and
procedures for irrigated agriculture in the Outer Islands, with emphasis on planning and
design of irrigation works, land development and related agricultural aspects.
(b)  A proposed follow-on project to the ISSP would focus, primarily in Java, on efficiency
and sustainability of existing infrastructure through support for O&M, cost recovery
and strengthening of O&M-related institutions and on improved management and
allocation of water both at the scheme level and in a river basin context. Inter-sectoral
water allocations and related institutional aspects are also being investigated under
various studies supported by Urban Development Projects as well as those being
carried out by the Bank.
(c)  Two operations under preparation (integrated swamps, and groundwater development)
would exploit resources in less developed regions, promoting specifically the objectives
of regional equity and poverty alleviation.



-9.s
(d) A proposed sector review would also benefit the longer-term investment strategy, so as
to decide if any major expansion in irrigated area or additional reservoirs are needed in
future, in addition to the consolidation and completion of existing investments.
2.20    Rationale for Bank Involvement. The Government requested Bank assistance to address the
issues related to incomnplete development from small and medium scale irrigation schemes and obtain
the full potential benefits from schemes. A second Government objective is to develop and implement
effective coordination'/management procedures for irrigated agricultural development at the provincial
and national levels. Biased on experience under past operations, the Bank is well placed to assist
Government in shifting the current Indonesian focus from construction programs to programs that
would obtain full benefits from irrigation works in a timely manner.
2.21    Under the proposed project, the Bank would encourage Govemment to give priority to
completion of on-going schemes rather than investments in new schemes, or existing schemes that
require major reconstruction or rehabilitation. The project would also require that the planning and
design of all schemes be criticaliy reviewed, in particular, the adequacy of the data base (hydrology
and soils) on which the schemes have been formulated. With Bank encouragement and support, the
Government is likely to give more priority to sensitive .ssues such as: (a) inter-agency coordination;
(b) local funding and budgetary process; (c) preparation for O&M until schemes are fully completed
and handed over to the provinces; and (d) procedures to test and certify that schemes are fully
operational. Specifically, the Bank role is required to shift the current institutional focus from
expenditure/construction programs to attaining agricultural benefits from irrigated agriculture and to
establish procedures to achieve above issues. This would be in tandem with Bank's support under
parallel operations to address policy and other institutional reforms.

Ill. THE PROJIEC
A. Proiect Obiectives and Strategy
3.01    Priect Objectives. The main project objective is to complete and fully develop ongoing
small and medium scale irrigation schemes on the Outer Islands of Indonesia, to achieve the potential
benefits from irrigated agricultural development. On-going schemes are those where the diversion
structure and the main distribution system have been completed, but are not ready for hand over for
efficient operation and mnaintenance (EOM). A related objective is to assist development of institutional
capacity to enable improved coordination of irrigation with agriculture and other related services.
3.02.       Strategv. The project would be structured as a five year operation with the first year
covering schemes presently well defined and categorized on technical and economic grounds. In the
remaining years, the project would support similar on-going schemes and activities to be defined and
agreed at annual implementation reviews scheduled to fit in with the Government's budgetary process
(para 5.19).
3.03.   EMi;ct Scope. Some 170 candidate schemes in 13 provinces proposed by Government 2V
were screened and categorized to confirm whether they matched the project objectives (para 3.01).
Following the screening process (summarized in para 3.19 and detailed in Annex 5), some
131 schemes were included under the project as shown on Maps IBRD 22361 and 22362. The
selected schemes were then categorized into two main types - 'go-ahead' and 'deferred' - to reflect the
status of preparation, technical and economic viab.1ity and risks. In this process, physical, technical
and economic determinants/constraints for each scheme were evaluated with special attention to the
quality of the original planning and design. Thirty-four 'go ahead' schemes, commanding about
23,300 ha, are ready to proceed immediately in the first year of the project period (Annex 1, Table 1).
A further 95 schemes, potentially serving about 249,500 ha, have been selected as 'd,ferred' schemes,
subject to further review of physical, technical and/or economic viability (Annex 1, Table 2).
B. Erolect Components
3.04.   The project would finance all activities until schemes can be verified and certified as fully
functional, namely: (a) survey, investigations and design; (b) completion of irrigation works on some
130 schemes serving about 273,000 ha, including construction of minor canal and drainage systems
and preparation for operation and maintenance (PROM); (c) land clearance and development of about
40,000 ha; (d) agricultural development activities including seed multiplication programs, trials for
problem soils and training of farmer groups; (e) land titling and certification; (f) environmental
protection including forest surveys and erosion control; (g) settlement; and (h) coordination and
management of irrigated agricultural developmnent.
C. Detailed Features
3.05    Surveys. Investigations and Design (SID). The SI) services would relate to irrigation and
land deve!opment works necessary to fully complete a scheme (para 5.15). The objective of the SID
activities would be to evaluate and confirm the command area and scope of each scheme such that it is
technically and economically viable. The activity relates primarily to deferred schemes where there is
uncertainty on: (a) adequacy of water availability and quality compared to the proposed command area;
(b) land capability (soils and topography); (c) land ownership and other land use issues; and (d) the
scheme scope where proposed expenditures on works (such as canal lining, drainage and flood
protection works) cannot be justified on the information available. The breakdown of SID services
and estimated costs by schemes are given in Annex 3, Tables 1 to 4.
2/   Primarily incomplete schemes financed under the SPIDP (Loan No. 2375-IND) and the
ongoing ISSP (Loan No. 2880-IND). The project provinces are: Aceh, Riau, Bengkulu,
Lampung, West and South Sumatra, West, Central and South Kalimantan, and North, Central
and South and South-East Sulawesi.



- 1 1 -
3.06    Completion of Irrigation Works. The existing irrigated area of all project schemes is about
114,000 ha (some 42 percent of the total command area of about 273,000 ha). For most project
schemes, the river diversion structure and most of the main canal distribution system are essentially
complete (Annex 5). The project would support the completion of the main canal system of each
scheme so a timely, reliable and equitab!- irrigation service can be provided to all parts of the
designated scheme command area. Minor irrigation works (commanding about 166,000 ha) would be
completed together with minor remedial and/or rehabilitadon works where schemes have deteriorated
over an extended construction period. In addition, drainage and flood control works would be
required for some schemes. The completion of irrigation civil works would include construction of
civil works and preparation for O&M. The breakdown of irrigation works by schemes is given in
Annex 3, Tables 1 and 2 for 'go-ahead' and 'deferred' schemes respectively.
3.07    The construction of civil works would include, inter alia completion of minor irrigation canal
and drainage systems (including some secondary canals and tertiaries), minor control and measuring
structures, maintenance and access roads, buildings, and completion or rninor rehabilitation of existing
infrastructure (weirs and main canals) so that a scheme can function as designed. Provision has also
been made to test three communication links between Jakarta and selected schemes using Very Small
Aperture Terminal (VSAT) technology (Annex 5). Land acquisition for irrigation works has been
included in the project cost estimates, but would not be financed under the Loan. With respect to
completion of irrigation works, DGWRD/PRIS would: (a) collect hydrological and climatic records on
a continuous basis for all schemes; (b) where land needs clearing, or steep slopes need levelling,
ensure that construction of tertiary canals be phased to follow land clearance and development; and
(c) defer works on schemes with soil problems (acid podsols and deep peats) until the agricultural
potential is confimed, based on actual experimental d;ta (para 3.12).
3.08    At present, there is no formal provision for operation and maintenance of schemes that are
under construction. Consequently, when a scheme remains incomplete for an extended period of time,
it often deteriorates into a rehabilitation project before the scheme starts to function. To avoid this
problem and to prepare for routine O&M (under 'recurrent' budget provisions) on the completion of a
scheme, the project provides specific support for O&M on completed parts of schemes during the
construction phase. Preparation for O&M (PROM) would constitute an essential part of the
construction process and thus be funded under 'non-recurrent' budget provisions. The PROM
component would support routinc O&M costs of those parts of a command area as they are completed
and also include for the overall scheme, inter alia, preparation of operational plans (POM) and O&M
manuals, some staff training, and general start-up activities for O&M including provision for facilities,
vehicles and equipment. Thus, when a scheme is designated complete, it would be ready to be tested
as functional before it is handed over to authorities responsible for 'recurrent' efficient O&M (EOM).
An essential process in accepting scheme completion would be to verify and certify that the scheme can
function adequately by physically testing the operation principles and irrigation performance (para
5.16).
3.09    Land Clearance and Development (LC&D). Prior to 1988, land clearance and development
was the responsibility of farmers and was based on an ineffective and inadequate credit system. It
took on average some 5 to 7 years from initial survey to full development for a typical land holding.
These delays were due to cumbersome and uncoordinated land titling programs, lack of funding,
complicated bureaucratic procedures, and for some schemes, slow settlement. For most schemes, land
developmnent seriously lagged civil works programs. Recognizing these problems, the Govemment
changed its policy in 1988 to undertake LC&D works at public expense through the Ministry of
Agriculture. Under the current five year plan (Repelita V), the Government plans to fully develop
some 375,000 ha of uncleared land within existing irrigated command areas.
3.10    Under the project, about 27,000 ha would be cleared and 40,000 ha developed for rice
cultivation. The LC&D component, described in Annex 6, would include: (a) survey, investigation
and design; (b) land clearance, including de-stumping; and (c) land development or leveling to form
sawah (fields suitable for rice cultivation). The scope and estimated costs are given in Annex 3,



- 12-
Tables 3 and 4. The LC&D programs would only be implemented in parts of scheme command areas:
(a) with suitable soils and assured water supplies; (b) where costs of LC&D works are justified on
economic grounds; and (c) when LC&D is wanted by the farmers. Land with slopes of greater than
8 percent would not be developed unless justified on technical grounds and only after a cost/benefit
analysis is undertaken to co-nfirm that the LC&D is economically feasible. Land under nature reserves,
protected or primary forest within the designated command area of schemes would be excluded and the
command area of a scheme adjusted accordingly. The LC&D program would be implemented as
quickLy as possible, since developed land can be used for dry land farming if there is some lag in
providing an irrigation service. It would also be implemented so as to avoid environmental damage.
In particular, the top soil would be saved and replaced after land levelling (Annex 6). To maintain
quality control, any ongoing LC&D programs funded from other sources would be completed and
reviewed for quality (para 5.20) before being considered for Bank support under the project. Thus all
schemes with ongoing LC&D programs have been included under the 'deferred' category (para 3.20).
3.11    Agricultural Development. The project would support several other agricultural development
activities to assure achievement of predicted benefits as described in Annex 8. Such activities would
be scheme specific and include, inter alia: support of facilities and equipment for agricultural extension
services (mainly for remote and isolated schemes), seed multiplication programs, trials for problem
soils and training of farmers groups in agricultural development activities. The scope and estimated
costs are given in Annex 3, Tables 5 and 6. The project would provide an irrigation agronomist under
the project consultant services (para 3.18) to assist provincial authorities in the preparation of
agricultural development plans. More general technical assistance for agricultural research and
extension would be forthcoming from the extensive technical assistance already available to
DGFCA/DAAD from other financing sources/donors.
3.12    Several schemes in Sumatra provinces (in particular, Riau) have problem soils which require
a program of trials to establish technically sound and economically viable crop production technologies
that are manageable by farmers/settlers. The Central Research Institute for Food Crops (CRIFC) in
Bogor has already established a range of recommendations for irrigated rice production on acid
podsolic and shallow peat/muck soils. However, these recommendations need to be locally verified
before they can be applied by extension agents to the local farning community. Once a specific soil
use recommendation has been made for a scheme, the recommendations would be incorporated in the
scheme planning and design (para 3.21). The CRIFC would make research staff available to formulate
and undertake appropriate trials on those schemes deferred for soiVtechnical reasons for the provincial
agricultural services (PRAS) involved, through MOA/DGFCA. A provisional sum has been included
to cover the cost of crop/soil trials.
3.13    Land Titling and Certification (LT&C). An essential requirement to plan irrigated agricultural
development is to establish land title within the scheme command area (Annex 7). Land titling and
certification is necessary to enable farmers to more easily obtain credit and also to provide the basis for
future taxes on the land owners. The poor progress to date on LT&C has been because the responsible
agency (BPN) lacked funding and has not been involved enough in the scheme implementation process
to coordinate the LT&C work with irrigation development. The project would therefore support all
LT&C activities to enable timely issuance of land title for all scheme command areas under the project.
These activities would vary for individual schemes, as detailed in Annex 3, Tables 7 and 8, but would
include cadastral and land use surveys, certification, equipment and administration. Most activities
would be undertaken departmentally and BPN would generally perform its duties when the land is
sufficiently cleared to enable the necessary surveys.
3.14    Environmental Protection. A detailed environmental review has been undertaken of all
schemes proposed for inclusion under the project (paras 3.23 to 3.25). To implement the
recommendations of this review, the project would support a series of activities, primarily to establish
a data base on which to plan a long term environmenmal action program. These activities would
include: (a) a forest inventory survey prior to land clearance; (b) independent supervision of logging
during land clearance; (c) a forest boundary survey to protect forest areas surrounding irrigation
commdnd areas; (d) survey of land use in the catchment areas of project schemes, particularly on



- 13-
adjacent forest lands; and (e) planning and design of soil erosion control works and activities (as
required). The scope and estimated costs of environmental protection works by scheme and provinces
are given in Annex 3, Tables 9 and 10. Results of the above activities would be monitored and
evaluated through the project period and, if necessary, support would be provided under the project for
essential preventive works by making minor revisions in scope from other components during the
annual review process. Forest clearing on project schemes would not be undertaken without approval
of the provincial forestry services.
3.15    Settlement. Reqiirements for settlement under the project would be limited to some
1400 families on five schemes in the provinces of Central and South East Sulawesi and West and
Central Kalimantan. All schemes with potential settlement requirements are in the 'deferred' category
and thus all settlement proposals would be evaluated under the status reports and action plans to be
approved by the Bank prior to scheme implementation. Such studies would include surveys of
existing population and use of land and water resources and of the target population group to be
settled. The settlement objective for each scheme would be to ensure that sufficiently experienced and
qualified farmers are available to cultivate the full command area developed under the project. Not all
settlement would be implemented under the Ministry of Transmigration (MOT). Some schemes are
expected to attract spontaneous migrants without incurring the high costs associated with Government
sponsored se -U;tnent. The project financing would only be made available for completing settlement
on schemes which are not already attracting spontaneous settlers. When Government sponsored
settlement is necessary, Government would provide the farrn and homestead holdings and the project
would support costs of surveys, land consolidation, land clearing and construction of housing, access
roads and public infrastructure, and transportation of settlers and initial agricultural development. The
MOT would undertake settlement on schemes under the project only after: (a) a detailed review of the
socio-economics of settlement is undertaken, including the prospects for spontaneous transmigration;
(b) the technical and economic viability has been confirmed; and (c) areas designated for settlement
have suitable soils and reliable irrigation supplies.
3.16    Institutional Support. The organizational structure of the project is described in para 5.01.
Most project activities are simple and straighEforward (i.e. routine data collection, planning and design,
supervision of construction and O&M). The activities continue the ongoing programs being
implemented by existing technical agencies and can be undertaken without technical assistance. If
Government decides to use consultants for routine tasks, costs would not be directly financed as
technical assistance from the Loan proceeds but funded by Government. All SID work would be
undertaken either departmentally or through local consultants (paras 3.05 and 3.10).
3.17  There are, however, a range of activities where institutional performance has been weak,
primarily because technical agencies have focused on construction programs and not on technical
performance of completed works. The process of coordinating project activities at national and
provincial levels has already identified areas where institutional support is required. The project
includes facilities, services, vehicles and equipment, and limited project specific staff training in each
province so that project provincial coordination (para 5.03) is effective. The items financed and
estimated costs are detailed in Annex 3, Table 13. In this respect, the project would support overseas
training in project and diagnostic analysis for three officials (one each from BAPPEDA, PRIS and
PRAS) from each province. General training and technical institutional strengthening are covered
under many other projects supported by the Bank and other donors and thus is not included under this
project.
3.18    Consultant Services. A major shortcoming during project preparation has been the limited
capability of Ministries/agencies involved in irrigated agricultural development to coordinate their
activities. Considerable progress has been made in the setting up of national and provincial
coordination arrangements and identifying procedures (para 5.02). However, the preparation has
depended on consultants to advise on technical issues and to analyze provincial preparation proposals
for consistency and viability. Although the project preparation has been a useful learning process,
much still has to be done, in particular, to process and evaluate revised proposals for 'deferred'
schemes and to monitor project progress. Thus, during the implementation phase, national level



- 14-
coordination to be carried out by BAPPENAS through the project national coordination working group
(PNCWG) would be assisted by the project consultant services. Selection of these consultants, with
extensive experience of irrigated agricultural development in all of the project provinces, would be
critical to project success. The project consultant services would be administered through DGWRD,
and consist of foreign and local consultants. Its main tasks would be to assist in the: (a) supervision
of coordinated project imnplementation for both irrigation and agricultural activities; (b) review of
scheme preparation and analysis; and (c) the verification and certification process to confirm that
schemes are complete and ready to be handed over for O&M. Terms of reference for the consultants
are given in Attachment 1 of Annex 4. The project consultant services would consist of three
international consultants (specialized in general irrigation development, irrigated agriculture and agro-
economics) together with national consultant staff to assist and monitor the project programs (Annex 3.
Table 14). An assurance was obtained that, by August 15, 1991, the Government would employ
consultants adrministered by DGWRD to advise with project implementation and supervision under
terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank.
D. Status of Preparation and Implementation Schedule
3.19    Status of Preparation. The project has been prepared by the line agencies at national and
provincial levels with assistance of consultants and the economic analysis of candidate schemes.
Candidate schemes have been screened and categorized to reduce the project risk (Annex 5). In this
process, proposals for schemes previously financed under SPIDP (Loan 2373-IND) were compared
with Status Reports and Action Plans under SPIDP and justification sought for any additional works
(in particular, area expansion or canal lining). For schemes financed under ISSP (Loan 2880-IND),
the preparatory review reports (1988), ISSP General Coordination quarterly reports, and Construction
Supervision reports were used to compare the project provincial proposals to avoid double financing.
3.20    For the 34 'go-ahead' schemes, contract documentation is under preparation and works can
commence, on or before April 1991. For the 95 'deferred' schemes, there is sufficient information to
prepare provisional proposals, but insufficient to define the present status and formulate a reliable
action plan. The deferred schemes have been subdivided into Sub-categories HA, IIB and IIC
(Annex 1, Table 2). The 43 schemes in the Category IIB would need special attention since, as
presented at appraisal, they have basic issues related to availability of physical resources and technical
viability. In addition, some 25 schemes under Sub-category IIB have economic rates of return (ERRs)
of less than 12 percent for the investments proposed under the project, excluding sunk investments to
date. These schemes would need special review to explain this lack of economic viability since the
schemes are designated as substantially complete. In addition, six 'deferred' schemes with serious
problems have been placed in Sub-category IIC. These schemes have been previously financed by the
Bank, but since Bank approval, substantial changes have been made to scope and estimated costs. The
schemes, as now presented, may not be technically viable and more information would be required to
confirm the planning and design. Some 10 percent of total base costs of all schemes have been added
to complete and develop about 45,000 ha under these Category IIC schemes and any additional
schemes that Government would propose for inclusion under the project.
3.21    For all 'deferred' schemes, technical status reports and action plans, based on adequate
surveys, investigations and design would be prepared satisfactory to the Bank. Such action plans
would include, inter alia,: (a) review of the resources available and their allocation to the scheme under
study, (b) agricultural and operational plans, (c) works and activities to be implemented with detailed
cost estimates, (d) institutional requirements, including Water Users' Associations (WUA. ), to
support the implementation, and the other support necessary from agriculture and other agencies; and
(e) an economic and environmental (if required) analysis. Schemes presently being financed by other
donors would be deferred until the other donors have completed their programs and the status of such
schemes can be reviewed (para 5.20). The selection process would be used through project
implementation to evaluate any additional schemes for which Government may seek support under the
project. The procedures and criteria to be used in this process are detailed in Annex 5. An assurance
was obtained that Government would screen, select and commission schemes in accordance with
procedures agreed with the Bank. In this respect, all agencies involved in the preparation for



- 15-
'aeferred' schemes would take into account: (a) the recommendations related to technical, economic
and environmentl viability in the project preparation documents; and (b) the status reports and action
plans prepared for schemes financed under the SPIDP and ISSP.
3.22    Implementation Schedule. The project implementation schedule by province and component
is given in Annex 3 (expressed in termns of annual expenditures). Deferred schemes have been
provisionally scheduled to reflect the timing and amount of SID work to be undertaken prior to
clearance for implementation. The implementation schedule would be updated and confirmed each
year (about May/June) at a joint Government/Bank Annual Implementation Review (para 5.19) so that
budget provisions and other resources can be made available in the following year. The review
process would decide what 'deferred' schemes are ready for implementation. For each scheme, the
phasing of the various programs would be reviewed to ensure that individual schemes are completed
and fully developed as soon as possible. An assurance was oftained from Government that the project
would be implemented in conformity with technical, financial, administrative, agricultural and
environmental practices, standards and criteria agreed with the Bank, and provincial work programs to
be agreed annually with the Bank, by June 30 each year.
E. Environmental Aspects
3.23    The environmental aspects of the project are summarized in Annex 9 and detailed in a full
report available in the Project File. The individual schemes pose minimal environmental risk since the
project supports only minor modifications of existing schemes where the principal impact has long
been registered. The risks of water-related diseases (malaria and typhoid) are small and the drainage
facilities where required would tend to reduce areas of stagnant water which are potential breeding
grounds for mosquitoes. However, there may be potential conflicts between development of some
small and medium scale schemes and protection and the maintenance of the surrounding national forest
estate. None of the schemes for implementation in the first year are assessed as being of sufficient
significance to justify detailed environmental analysis. However, four 'go-ahead' schemes (Air
Benkenang, Andowengga, Air Nasal Kiri and Air Kedurang) as proposed, include small areas
designated as nature reserves and Protection Forest (Hutan Lindung) land. These areas would be
excluded from the scheme commands.
3.24    Of the 'deferred' schemes under the project, Sadang, Maloso and Kaleana Kanan/Kiri
schemes in South Sulawesi, Air Seluma scheme in Bengkulu, Sitiung II scheme in West Sumatra,
Way Umpu scheme in Lampung, Gumbasa scheme in South Sulawesi and Merowi scheme in West
Kalimantan would require a detailed environmental study (SEL). The Sausu Upstream and Lambunu
schemes in Central Sulawesi, Air Lais scheme in Bengkulu and Padang Sappa scheme in South
Sulawesi would require a preliminary environmental assessment (PEL). Such assessments would be
prepared by DGWRD/PRIS. Government would complete environmental impact studies prior to
submission to the Bank for re-evaluation of the deferred status of these schemes (para 3.25). With
respect to the Sadang and Maloso Irrigation schemes, special attention would be given to flood control
and drainage impact and the interface between irrigation and the important coastal fishery operations.
3.25    For all project schemes, a comprehensive environmental planning approach would be
adopted. In this respect, the Ministry of Forestry (including soil conservation and forest management
specialists) would be added to the project coordination working groups at national and provincial levels
(para 5.03). The main responsibility of these specialists would be to monitor environmental impact
through the project period. They would ensure that: (a) no land clearance would take place in
designated 'Protection' or 'Production' Forest; (b) where approved clearing of medium or heavy forest
is to take place, a forest inventory would be undertaken prior to clearing; (c) arrangements made to
harvest and sell any merchantable timber prior to or during general clearing operations; (d) land within
the water catchment area of a scheme would be reclassified and gazetted, as required, as 'Protection'
Forest; and (e) effective soil erosion control procedures would be incorporated into civil works
contracts. In this respect, the project would support costs of environmental surveys, including
catchment surveys, and any corrective actions to be agreed with the Bank at each annual project
review. Assurances were obtained that Govemment would: (i) undertake detailed environmental



- 16-
assessments on deferred scheme satisfactory to the Bank as part of the project screening and selecdon
process; and (ii) cause MOF/DGWRD to classify catchment areas of schemes as Protection Forests.
With respect to item (ii) of the above assurances, DGWRD would undertake either detailed
environmental studies (SELs) or preliminary environmental assessments (PELs), depending on the
command area of the scheme to be assessed (para 3.24).
F. Role of Women
3.26    Provision is made under the project in the design of irrigation works to take into account the
role of women. For example, foot access structures would be provided to minimize the journey from
village to fields and washing steps are constructed where appropriate. It is also recognized that the role
of women is important in irrigated agriculture, particularly in rice transplanting, crop harvesting and
drying operations. Water collection for household use is also undertaken by women. The household
uses of irrigation water would be taken into account when the design of deferred schemes are finalized,
in particular, to reduce the burden of this task. The interest of women would be represented at the
scheme verification and certification process (para 5.16) when schemes are formally transferred from
the construction stage to routine O&M. In particular, for PROM acdvities (para 3.08) where small
schemes may be turned over to WUAs, an acdve participation of women would be supported. Data
collection and analysis for purposes of monitoring and evaluation of project impact would break down
by gender employment benefits that are generated by the project (para 5.17). Information would
include different employment opdons for men and women, wage rates and training requirements. The
participation of women in WUAs would also be monitored and evaluated. Any lessons learnt would
be incorporated in the detailed design of those schemes deferred to the later years of the project.
IV. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
Project Costs
4.01    Total cost of the project, including physical contingencies and expected price increases, is
estimated at about Rp 397.4 billion (US$ 215.4 million), with a foreign exchange component of some
US$ 89.2 million, or about 41 percent. The cost estimate is based on September 1990 prices and
includes taxes and duties computed at about Rp 24.5 billion (US$ 13.3 million). Civil works
estimates are derived from costs of similar schemes already under execution and, in particular, the
costs of completion of ongoing schemes under the Bank supported ISSP (Loan 2880-IND). The cost
of civil works for deferred schemes is based on the anticipated scope of works identified at appraisal.
Costs of land clearance and developmnent and land titling and certification have been based on standard
unit rates for each activity and applied to each individual scheme. Cost estimates for equipment and
vehicles are based on price quotations from suppliers, cross-checked with prices of comparable units
recently purchased or currently being procured by various agencies. Consultant staff costs are based
on experience with recent consultancy contracts in Indonesia. Costs have been included for the
refinancing of the Project Preparation Facility (PPF) and also for consultant services for project 'start-
up' activities through to loan effectiveness (US$ 1.25 million). A provision, equivalent to 10 percent
of the estimated costs of all deferred schemes, has been included in the base cost to cover the
'deferred' schemes under Sub-category IIC (Annex 2, Table 11) and any additional schemes agreed by
the Bank for inclusion under the project. Physical contingencies for possible design variations in
'deferred' schemes have been assessed at an average of 10 percent of base costs of all works to
complete and develop schemes and equipment and vehicles. Price contingency estimates assume
increases in local currency costs of 6.5 percent in 1991 and 6.0 percent per annum thereafter (based on
expected domestic inflation), and in foreign exchange costs of 3.6 percent per annum in 1991 and
thereafter. Project cost estimates are summarized in Table 4.1 below and detailed by province and
components in Annex 3.

4.02      The proposed Bank loan of US$ 125.0 million equivalent would finance 100 percent of
foreign exchange costs and about 28 percent of local currency costs of the project. This represents
about 62.5 percent of total project costs (US$ 199.4 million), exclusive of taxes and duties and the
cost of vehicles (reserved procurement). A provision for retroactive financing of US$ 2.5 million has
been included to cover eligible expenditures, including consultant services. This would finance start
up activities, particularly surveys, investigations and designs of schemes to be included in the
years 2 and 3. Government would finance the remaining project cost of US$ 90.4 million from its
development budget (or a net contribution of US$ 77.1 million after taxes and duties). The project
financing plan is summarized in Table 4.2 below, and the annual phasing of Government contributions
(by project element) is given in Annex 2, Table 15. In this respect, the Government would allocate
adequate funds, facilities, services and resources to implement and efficiently operate and maintain all
infrastructure and public facilities constructed under the project, and ensure tircly release of funds.

ZE.:urement
4.03      The procurement under the- nroject would be undertaken by the respective implementing
agencies at nati t ial and provinciZl levels. The procurement of surve. invesiigation and design (SID)



- 18-
services would not be suitable for international competitive bidding (ICB) because the activities are
scheme specific both in location and scheduling. They would be partly undertaken by force account
(US$ 1.8 million) and partly through survey contractors under local competitive bidding (LCB)
procedures acceptable to the Bank (US$ 2.7 million). Most of the irrigafion civil works
(US$ 106.6 million) for completion of ongoing projects would generally be small in scale, dispersed
over 13 provinces. Works, such as tertiary canal -onstruction, would require close interaction with the
local community, both as beneficiaries and as laborers. They are thus unlikely to attract foreign
competition, and would be largely tendered on the basis of LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank.
Civil works packages larger than US$ 3.0 million would be procured through ICB. Such packages
are not expected to exceed more than four in number, and total about US$ 14.5 million in value. The
ICB contracts would only occur on 'deferred' schemes, with none in the first year of the project. This
would be the mros: cwon,mical and efficient procurement method in Indonesia for civil works on
remote schemes that cannot be fully defined in advance and for activi;des that need to be carried out
with least disruption to ongoing operations.
4.04    The land clearance and development works (US$ 30.7 million) are at present undertaken
through a large number of small contracts to enable local provincial contractors to tender. This has
proved to be difficult to supervise with the limited resources available and has often led to poor quality
implementation. The LC&D works would therefore be implemented, wherever possible, under as
large as possible contracts in each provinces, subject to size of the provincial work programs and rate
at which 'deferred' schemes are cleared for implementation. Because of contract size and the scattered
location of the schemes, the LC&D) works would not attract international bids and would be therefore
be procured under LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank. For similar reasons, agricultural
development works and services (US$ 1.9 million) would not attract intemational bids and would be
procured under LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank (US$ 1.0 million) or undertaken by force
account (US$ 0.9 million). Land titling and certification works and services (US$ 12.2 million)
vwould also not attract international bids and would be procured under LCB procedures acceptable to
the Bank (US$ 9.8 million) or undertaken by force account (US$ 2.4 million). Settlement works
(US$ 8.6 million) consists of many discrete activities specific to individual schemes that cannot be
packa6W into large contracts and would be procured under LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank
(US$ 7.4 million) or undertaken by force account (US$ 1.2 million).
4.05    Institutional strengthening services (US$ 2.7 million) would be carried out by the BAPPEDA
in the provincial governments using force account. Consultancy services (US$ 10.1 million) would be
procured under Bank Guidelines for the Use of Consultants.
4.06    Most equipment and materials (US$ 1.7 million), including computers, survey and office
equipment and other materials, would be required in small batches by different agencies. They would
generally not be suitable for ICB and would be procured under LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank
(US$ 1.3 million) or international and local shopping (US$ 0.4 million). Nonetheless, packages
valued at more than US$ 200,000 would be procured through ICB procedures. In bid comparison,
local manufacturers would be allowed a 15 percent margin of preference, or the applicable customs
duty, which ever is lower. Government regulations, prohibiting importation of vehicles, make ICB
for procurement of vehicles impossible. Vehicles (US$ 2.7 million) must therefore be classified as
reserved procurement and would not be financed under the loan. However, an understanding was
reached that vehicles required for the project would be provided, or purchased from Government
budgetary sources, under a schedule agreed with the Bank.
4.07    Review of Procurement. For works contracts, the Bank would review: (a) all ICB contracts
prior to tendering and award; and (ii) LCB contracts over US$ 1.0 million, namely about 23 works
contracts (with an aggregate estimated value of US$ 41.0 million) covering about 26 percent of the
value of all works. The remaining works contracts (in aggregate about US$ 90.2 million) would be
reviewed by the Bank on a post award sample basis. The Bank would also conduct a prior review of
all contracts valued at US$ 200,006 or more for equipment and materials (with an aggregate value of
US$ 0.6 million) and for SID services (with an aggregate value of US$ 0.7 million). Contracts for
small equipment and materials and SID services not exceeding US$ 200,000 (with aggregate values of



-19-
US$ 0.7 million and US$ 2.0 million respectively) would be controlled by Govemment procurement
units, and reviewed on a random basis after award. All international consultant invitation docurnents,
proposed awards and contracts would be reviewed by the Bank.
