Disbursements
4.09      Implementation Period. Disbursements under the Loan are expected to be completed by
December 31, 1996, about five years after Boared approval. While the disbursement profile for past
investment projects in the irrigation subsector in Indonesia has stretched over about 8 years, it is not
applicable to this project, which constitutes a five-year time slice of an investment program of schemes
presently under construction and most of which are substantially complete. The schedule of
disbursements is given in Annex 1, Table 3 and the detailed implementation schedules of the project
components (Annex 3) indicate that this disbursement schedule is feasible. The target areas to be
covered under each component and in each province are feasible related to the provincial institutional
capacity.



-20 -
4.10    Loan Allocation. The proposed allocation of the loan and percentage of expenditures to be
financed are given in Annex 1, Table 4 and would be as follows: (a) 60 percent of expenditures on
irrigation civil works (with an allocation of US$ 55.5 million); (b) 80 percent of expenditures on land
clearance and development (US$ 23.3 million) and settlement (US$ 6.5 million); (c) 91 percent of
expenditures on survey, investigations and design (US$ 4.0 million), agricultural development
(US$ 1.7 millioni) and land tiding and certification (US$ 10.5 million); (d) 1(X) percent of expenditures
on environmental protection (US$ 0.6 million); (e) 1(X) percent of eligible toreign expenditures for
directly imported equipment, 95 percent of local expenditures (ex-factory prices) and 65 percent of
locally procured items (US$ 1.5 million); (f) 100 percent of expenditures on consultant services
(US$ 9.7 million) and on institutional support (US$ 2.5 million); and (g) unallocated (US$ 7.95
million). In addition, US$ 1.25 million would be allocated for refinancing of the Project Preparation
Facility (PPF).
4.11    An assurance was obtained that as a condition of disbursement for schemes in the deferred
category, including any additional schemes financed under the project, except for the SID component,
prior approval by the Bank to the scheme design, scope and estimated costs, and confirmation by the
Bank that all agencies involved have cleared the scheme for implementation under the project. In
addition, assurances were obtained that as a condition of continuing disbursement: (a) for all schemes,
except for the SI) component, in each of the project provinces, Government would make a budget
allocation to implement the agreed annual provincial work program for each such province; and (b) for
irrigation works in each of the project provinces, Government would make annual budgetary
allocations (and furnish evidence that funds were expended in the previous year) for the fiscal year
1992/93, and each subsequent fiscal year thereafter until project completion, which at least maintain the
fiscal year 1990/91 level of total nation-wide irrigation O&M financing under the Central Government
budget in real terms and, if required, provide any incremental funding need for efficient O&M. In this
respect, Government has already furnished the Bank with evidence that: (a) the budget requests for
fiscal year 1991/92 provide for the agreed annual work programs; and (b) it has maintained the fiscal
year 1986/87 budget level for O&M in real terms for the entire irrigated area in Indonesia, as agreed by
Government under the ongoing ISSP (Loan 2880-IND).
4.12    Disbursements would be made against contracts valued at less than US$ 200,000, except for
works executed on force account basis which would be disbursed against statements of expenditure
(SOEs). Bank loan disbursements would be for works (including SID activities related to works),
materials and equipment. Documentation for SOE expenditures would not be submitted for review,
but would be retained by the project implementation units and made available for inspection by the
Baink during the course of project supervision. Total disbursements against SOEs is expected to
amount to about US$ 75.0 million, or about 60 percent of the Bank loan amount.
4.1' 3    Special Account. Government would establish a Special Account in Bank Indonesia to be
maintained by DG Budget and implement the new procedures for faster processing of disbursements
and withdrawals satisfactorily tested under previous Bank operations. The Special Account would be
used for all eligible payments made against all disbursement categories. The Bank would make an
initial deposit equivalent to an estimated four months' disbursement (US$ 7.0 million) into the Special
Account, which would be replenished quarterly, or when payments reach one-half of the initial
deposit. A monthly statement on the Special Account would be submitted by DG Budget to the Bank.
Accounts and Audits
4.14    For all Government agencies, each accounting section is headed by a qualified accountant
who reports monthly on budget and financial status. Internal audit sections are responsible for visiting
project sites quarterly and examining local accounting records. Accounting procedures currently in use
are adequate to support statement of expenditures (SOE) applications. Final accounts, including
consolidated receipts and expenditure statements, are prepared at year end that meet Bank
requirements. In this respect, accounting procedures presently utilized by provincial governments are
expected to change during the project period with the improvements being introduced by Govemment
under the Accountancy Development Project (Loan No. 2940-IND). These include, inr ali



- 21 -
introduction of a uniform chart of accounts, double-entry accounting and balance sheet accounting.
This should facilitate improved management reporting on project activities. With respect to auditing,
project accounts are subject to two audits: (a) by the Inspector General of the concerned ministry; and
(b) by the Financial and Development Supervisory Board (BPKP). The work of both auditors has
been found to be generally satisfactory on Bank-assisted irrigation projects.
4.15   Assurances were obtained that Government would cause each project executing agency at
both national and provincial levels to: (i) establish and maintain adequate and separate accounts from
the beginning of project implementation; and ii) maintain such accounts in accordance with the sound
and generally recognized accounting principles and practices satisfactory to the Bank, so as to enable
these agencies to provide annual financial statements to reflect the financial performance and status of
project components. In addition, assurances were obtained that Government would:
(a)  keep all project related contracts, documents related to the SOEs, and orders and
receipts for at least one year after the Bank has received the audit report for the fiscal
year in which the last withdrawal from the loan account was made;
(b)  maintain the Special Account, including records and documentation of receipts and
witndrawals, as deemed satisfactory by the Bank;
(c)  complete consolidated annual financial statements of the project for audit within six
months of the close of each Government fiscal year for independent audit; and
(d)  undertake independent audits by BPKP of each year's project consolidated accounts,
including the audits of SOEs and the Special Account, and submit such audit reports to
the Bank within nine months of the close of each Government fiscal year.
4.16    The project accounting and audit arrangements would be complex given the number of
executing agencies and provinces under the project. Programming and the budgeting/accounting
advice from project consultancy services (para 3.18) would assist in establishing, the introduction and
monitoring the project accounting systems. These systems would include, int aia, financial
statements and consolidated income statements in accordance with the Government regulations and
procedures.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Background
5.01    The organizational structure for the implementation of the project is already in place. Each
project executing agency would be responsible for survey and investigations, planning and design,
implementation, and coordinating its own work program. The coordination activities, including
planning and integration of budget proposals at national and provincial levels, would be undertaken by
BAPPENAS (the National Planning Agency) and the BAPPEDA (the provincial planning bodies)
respectively. The technical agencies involved in irrigated agricultural development are: (a) Ministry of
Public Works (MPW) and the provincial water resource services (PRIS) for construction and O&M of
irrigation works; (b) Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) and the provincial agricultural services (PRAS)
for land and agricultural development; (c) BPN for land titling and certification; and (d) Ministry of
Transmigration for settlement. The Ministry of Forestry would be responsible for environmental
aspects related to natural resources protection.
5.02    They each have the potential capability to successfully implement their own work programs
or activities. However, since each agency is primarily concerned with its own activity, there is limited
interest in the overall performance of an irrigation scheme. Consequently, the work programs of the
agencies have continued on separate tracks with no real accountability for performance of the separate
components or the overall project. Arrangements set up under project preparation at the national a.id



- 22 -
provincial levels have demonstrated important steps towards an integrated and coordinated approach to
irrigated agricultural development (para 3.19). However, project preparation has shown that the
coordination necessary between irrigadon and agriculture development cannot be made the
responsibility of one of the technical ministries or agencies. Thus, at nadonal level BAPPENAS has
undertaken coordination of an inter-agency project national coordination working group (PNCWG)
chaired by BAPPENAS including the project ministries/agencies with a coordinating secretary from
DGWRD assisted by consultant services. These arrangements would continue under project
implementation, also assisted by consultant services (para 3.18). The main coordinating function at
national level would be to provide technical assistance and guidance and generally review and
coordinate provincial programs to ensure uniformity of planning and design standards, criteria and cost
estimates. In addition, any major policy issues (general and technical) that may arise would be
addressed as part of the overall project supervision. An important function would also be to undertake
project analysis and some diagnostic analysis. Most provinces have little or no capability in these tasks
which are essential if unjustified expenditure programs are to be avoided and priority given to
achieving benefits from investrnents as quickly as possible. Coordination of irrigated agricultural
development has started to work at the national level and a basic objective would be to support this
process through the project period (para 5.09).
5.03    At the provincial level, the BAPPEDA have undertaken the coordination of project activities
in each of the project provinces respectively. Inter-agency project provincial coordination working
groups have been established, chaired by BAPPEDA, and comprised of representatives from the
technical line agencies involved in the project (public works, agriculture, BPN, transmigration and in
some provinces, forestry). This provincial coordination process has started to work and in most
provinces there is a growing realization of present shortcomings and the substantial additional costs
and loss of benefits through piecemeal irrigated agricultural development. Under the project, the
provinces would be responsible for detailed planning, evaluation and analysis of work programs, the
budgetary process related to project schemes, reviews, appraisals and implementation (by each
technical agency), and reporting and monitoring. The project provincial coordination working groups
(PPCWG) would continue through the implementation phase (para 5.09). They would review
candidate schemes, agree to coordinated work proposals and programs for selected schemes, and
identify provincial policy issues associated with scheme compledon.
B. Project Organization
5.04    Irrigation. The Ministry of Public Works (MPW) through the Directorate of Water
Resources Development (DGWRD) and the PRIS in each province, would be responsible for any
surveys, investigations and designs required, completion of irrigadon civil works and for the PROM
component. For these activities, the concemed units within DGWRD would be responsible for project
planning and programming, standardizing O&M procedures, review of ongoing schemes, and pre-
qualification, selection and supervision of the work of local consultants for preparadon of designs and
tender documents. DGWRD/PRIS responsibilities would include supervision of project
implementation, and monitoring of their procurement actions. The designated staff from the main
executing bodies within DGWRD (DOI-I & II and DPP) would inform the PNCWG of the progress
made in project implementation and problems, if any, that need to be resolved. The DOI-I would be
responsible (at naional level) for the completion of ongoing projects and would provide technical
guidance and administrative support to the PRIS for the implementation of EOM of irrigation areas
(through the parallel Bank supported ISSP). The DOI-I would also be responsible for training related
to irrigation systems and on-farm water management for the middle and lower level admninistrative
officials, staff of agricultural and irrigadon services and (as applicable) the WUAs. It would also
provide the main interface with MOA through the Directorate of Food Crops Agriculture (DGFCA).
The DGWRD would be responsible for administering the project consultant services to assist in project
implementation and supervision (pam 3.18) and also for quality control related to data collection,
planning and design and project programming. At provincial level, the PRIS in each province would
be responsible for implementation and for transfer for small schemes to WUAs.



- 23 -
5.05    Land and Agricultural Development. The Ministry of Agriculture (MOA), through the
Directorate of Agriculture Area Development (DAAD) within the Directorate General of Food Crop
Agriculture (DGFCA) would be responsible for the land and agricultural development (LC&D)
component. The implementation would be the responsibility of the PRAS in each province. The main
role of DAAD at national level would be assist the PRAS to strengthen their capability related to
preparation of specifications, contracting procedures and the effective use of staffing and general
administration. Special attention would be given to quality control of surveys, investigations and
designs, provincial planning and programming, supervision, monitoring and evaluation of preparation
and implementation of ongoing work programs. The DGFCA, through PRAS, would be responsible
for the agricultural development component (para 3.1 1) and assist the provincial authorities in the
preparation of agricultural development plans for each scheme. The Directorate of Planning and
Programming (DPP) in DGFCA would be responsible for monitoring overall project performance with
respect to irrigated agricultural impact. In addition, the Ministry of Home Affairs (MHA) in
coordination with DGFCA through the PRAS, would have overall responsibility for organizing,
reorganizing and developing WUAs and would undertake surveys, and conduct an inventory and
evaluation of WUAs for all project schemes. To coordinate agricultural activities at provincial level,
each PRAS would coordinate the provincial agricuiltural program through a unit comprising the
provincial Heads of Agricultural Area Development (AAD), Planning and Programming (P&P) and
Production Development Subservice (PDS). The Head of the AAD would chair the unit since the AAD
would be responsible for the major activity in each PRAS. An assurance was obtained at negotiations
that Government would cause MOA/DGFCA to provide appropriate staffing, facilities, services and
the other resources required to carry out effective agricultural extension and other agricultural services,
satisfactory to the Bank, throughout all the proposed project districts.
5.06    Settlement. The Ministry of Transmigration (MOT) at the national level and through its
provincial offices would be responsible for settlement component. Where spontaneous settlement does
not meet scheme requirements, settlement under the project would follow well established procedures
that have been tested extensively through the project provinces under Governments transmigration
programs supported by the Bank. There would be no need for institutional strengthening and training
under the project. T>1 main objectives would be to ensure that settlement is integrated into the
coordinated work r , grams, and there are sufficient experienced farmers to fully cultivate the
designated irrigation command areas of each scheme. In addition, MOT would monitor and improve
the welfare of existing transmigrants already located on project schemes.
5.07    Land Titling and Certification. The National Land Agency, or Badan Pertanahan Nasional
(BPN), would be responsible for the land titling and certification component. BPN is a revenue
generating agency and is supposed to support itself from charges to its clients. Routine BPN activities
include land registration, cadastral surveys, and land titling and certification. Land consolidation also
forms part of BPN's mandate. Individual land titles are issued to local farmers and transmigrants
except in those provinces where land is held in clian ownership, when the clan is issued the tidtle. The
timely issue of land title is important, because it provides the basis for future land taxation and also
enables farmers to more easily obtain agricultural producdon credit. BPN's poor performance to date,
with serious delays in issuing land certificates, has been due to insufficient funding and coordination
with the other agencies involved in irrigation development. Lack of funding has resulted in the need
for institutional strengthening, additional staffing, equipment and transportation. Once the flow of
funds is assured under the project, it is expected that BPN would be sufficiently strengthened to
continue its activities without further financial support. Project support for BPN is seen as a way of
breaking the cycle of funding constraints resulting from the inability of BPN to generate income from
incomplete ongoing irrigation schemes. It would also help to alleviate problems related to
farmers/owners unwilling to activelv support irrigation development, particularly in remote and
isolated areas where land ownership is often in dispute. Schemes with substantial areas with land
ownership in dispute have been deferred until the disputes are resolved. BPN would give priority to
these schemes.



- 24 -
5.08    Environmental Protection. The Ministry of Forestry (MOF) through the Directorate of
Reforestation and Land Rehabilitation (DGRLR) would have a minor, but important role, in
undertaking surveys and inventory of forested land within and surrounding command areas of all
schemes under the project. The DGRLR staff would also advise on soil erosion control and upstream
catchment protection on selected schemes to reduce the risk of negative environmental impact. The
project provides support to cover the costs of forest inventory and resurvey of forest boundaries and
associated overhead costs (para 3.14). In addition, forestry staff would participate in to the national
and provincial coordination working groups to ensure that MOFs interests are taken into account in
further management and implementation of project schemes.
5.09    Coordinalion. For project implementation, the national (BAPPENAS) and provincial
(BAPPEDA) coordination arrangements used during project preparation would be maintained and
strengthened. A project objective would be for each province to develop the capability to undertake
technical analysis and cost/benefit evaluations for schemes, (in particular, deferred schemes), included
under the project. The procedures for coordination of project implementation would be based on those
used during project preparation. These arrangements would be standardized and strengthened as
described in Annex 5. The project includes the administrative costs for the provincial working groups,
vehicles, equipment, and training for BAPPEDA, PRIS and PRAS staff in project analysis. Some
BAPPEDA would benefit from the project consultant services (para 3.18) to help in the setting up and
start up of implementation coordination. In addition, the BAPPEDA may be able to draw on technical
assistance of other donors (i.e. ADB, USAID, CIDA, etc) with ongoing programs in some of the
provinces.
5.10    At the district level, coordination and overseeing of project implementation would be under
the district BAPPEDA, channelled through the Irrigation Commnittee, which is represented by Cabang
Dinas irrigation, Kabupaten agricultural service and other related services, and headed by the local
Bupati. The Bupati, as chairman of this committee, would obtain technical guidance from the
provincial level project implementation agencies (PRIS, PRAS, etc.) through the district services of the
concerned agencies. Thus, an assurance was obtained that Government would cause BAPPENAS and
each provincial BAPPEDA to undertake project coordination at national and provincial levels
respectively, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank. In addition, to ensure that the effectiveness of
project coordination can be monitored, assurances were also obtained that Government would submit
to the Bank for its review and comments, for each province under the project: (i) not later than June 30
each year, coordinated work programs and cost estimates for the following fiscal year; and (ii), by
September 30 each yer:r, coordinated budget proposals for the agreed provincial work programs. A
budget allocation to implement the agreed work program would be a condition of continuing loan
disbursement for irrigation work programs in a province (para 4.11).
5.11   Farmer Participation. The project would benefit from an active and strong beneficiary
involvement in the planning, implementation and the O&M of completed schemes. In this respect,
under project implementation procedures (Annex 5), the MHA/DGWRD/PRIS/PRAS would: (a) set
up water user associations (WUAs) on all schemes under the PROM component; and (b) invite elected
representatives of the WUAs to participate in the verification process when a scheme is formally
certified as complete. Special attention would be given to the 49 schemes commanding less than
500 ha that may be candidates for turning over to farmer management.
C. Project Management
5.12    The main challenge to project management involved in irrigated agricultural development
relates to overcoming the lack of inter-agency coordination and cooperation and evaluation of project
impact. The following activities, described below, need to be put in place, or substantially
strengthened: (a) the quality of planning and design with special attention to data collection and project
and diagnostic analysis; (b) priority to completion of ongoing schemes; (c) timely provision of local
funding and coordination of budgetary provisions; (d) monitoring and evaluation and reporting; and
(e) reduction of unproductive technical assistance and training.



- 25 -
5.13    Scheme Pregaration. Analysis and Documentation. An important activity to be supported
under the project would be data collection and presentation. As evidenced by findings of the technical
audit on 84 SPIDP schemes 8/, present practice is to generally enter the planning and design and
construction stages of irrigation projects with inadequate physical resource data (water availability, soil
surveys and topography). Hydrologic and climatic data collection in all project provinces are given
low priority and are subject to unpredictable budget allocadons. Preparation reports for mos project
schemes qualify their recommendations by stating that the data base was inadequate, and then resort to
using empirical formulae for planning and design. The assumptions in the empirical formnulae are
generally not confurmed by subsequent collection of data as a routine activity 2/. In addition, river flow
data is seldom collected and analyzed on a regular basis for completed schemes under EOM. Because
of an inadequate data base, many substantially complete schemes proposed for the project have had to
be deferred, pending confirmation of reliable water resources (and suitable soils). To address this
problem, hydrologic and climatic data collection would be routinely collected on all project schemes.
An assurance was obtained that Government would cause DGWRD/PRIS to collect and analyze
regular and sufficient hydrologic and climatic data to provide a continuous and reliable basis for
assessing water availability and allocadon for each scheme under the project.
5.14    To ensure that collected data can be located, the geographic information systems (GIS) which
are being established in each province, would be used as the foundation for data monitoring and
evaluation of project schemes. Relevant data would be assembled and collated onto the base maps,
using the computer equipment recently installed in most BAPPEDA. Appropriate training would be
given to BAPPEDA and technical agency staff in the GIS process and procedures as they relate to the
project. The terdary block would become the basic planning unit. Physical infornation (soils and land
use) and economic parameters (costs and returns) would be assembled by tertiary block, and entered in
the GIS for analysis and presentation. For 'deferred' schemes, this information would then be used in
the preparation (or confirnation of existing) status reports and action plans. For this purpose, simple
and standardized formats would be adopted to elicit inter-agency coordination. The project consultants
(para 3.18) would assist the project agencies in this process (Annex 4).
5.15    Scheme Completion. A major shortcoming with irrigation development on the Outer Islands
is that the 'completion' status of an irrigation scheme is not clearly defined before construction is
started, often on the basis of partial funding. Consequently, substandal expenditures are made, yet
few schemes are functional before funding is exhausted. For many schemes supported under the
SPIDP (Loan 2375-IND) and the ongoing ISSP (Loan 2880-IND), the scope of works (and thus
proposed expenditures under the projects) were substantially greater than those put forward when the
schemes were approved for Bank financing. Expensive items, such as canal lining, have often been
added without technical justification, while essential works, such as completion of tertiaries that would
generate benefits, are deferred. Consequently, many schemes are left incomplete for extended periods
and deteriorate before the scheme, or in some cases, even part of a scheme, is functional. To avoid
these shortcomings, assurances were obtained that Government would implement all works required to
fully complete and develop each scheme under the project, as defined and agreed when the scheme is
approved by the Bank, before any additional development to a scheme or any expansion to the scheme
command area is undertaken.
I/   PCR, Second Provincial Irrigation Development Project, Report No. 8336, January 26, 1990.
2/  An example of this shortcoming is the flood control and drainage proposal for the Sadang
scheme in South Sulawesi where an expensive feasibility study was undertaken a decade ago
without an adequate data base. As a consequence, a US$ 30.0 million component proposed
for financing under the project was excluded.



- 26-
5.16    Verification and Cenification of Scheme Completion. An important step in irrigation
development is the transfer of a scheme from the implementation to the O&M stage. At present, the
transfer process and procedures are unsatisfactory and incomplete schemes are often prematurely
handed over for O&M. To address this problem, when a scheme is considered complete by the
implementing agencies, there would be a formal test to verify the satisfactory functioning of the
completed irrigation system and to confirm that the command area is fully developed (Annex 5). The
test would be undertaken by experienced staff from DGWRD/MOA together with representatives from
BAPPEDA, PRIS, PRAS, other provincial agencies involved in scheme implementation. The farmers
to be served, through their WUA, would also participate in this process. The verification process
would consist of a technical audit to confirm completion of all civil works and land clearance and
development, as agreed when the scheme was approved for implementation. Completion of the
preparation for O&M (PROM) activities would also be verified. The scheme would then be tested to
confirm that it can function as designed through the full operational range of discharges (i.e. the
scheme can deliver the design irrigadon service) and that the operadng principles and plan are practical.
If the scheme cannot function as designed, remedial works would be undertaken and the scheme re-
tested after such works are completed. The project consultants (para 3.18) would assist in this process
as required. Once the various representatives involved in the verification process agree that a scheme
is ready to transfer from the construction phase to routine O&M, a "Certificate of Completion" would
be issued and schemes formally handed over for O&M. An assurance was obtained that Government
would cause DGWRD/PRIS to certify and verify that such schemes are complete and can function as
designed before handing over project schemes to the O&M authorities within each PRIS or to the
farmers' groups, as appropriate.
5.17    Monitoring and Evaluation. Monitoring and evaluatdon (M&E) has been poor on previous
Bank supported projects and it is often not possible to define with certainty the physical status of
schemes designated as complete. M&E would be given special attention under the project, with each
project agency responsible for monitoring and evaluating physical and financial progress of their own
programs. Implementation performnance on individual components would be collated at the provincial
and central level through the respective coordination working groups. Socio-economic impacts, crop
incomes and margins, crop production and support services and water management practices would be
monitored by the Directorates of Planning and Programming in DGWRD and MOA, based on methods
and criteria previously agreed with the Bank. The M&E of water management practices would be
undertaken through the ISSP (Loan 2880-IND) and the proposed follow-up project. These M&E
activities would cover criteria such as water use efficiencies and understanding by field staff of routine
O&M procedures, such as operation of gates, water measurement and maintenance of canal systems.
The VSAT technology (para 3.07) to be tested under the project, should help to improve the efficiency
of routine data transfer. There is also an urgent need to strengthen the M&E of technical performance
of irrigation schemes, such as the percentage of water demand that was met, fluctuations in water use
efficiencies and the effectiveness of farmers' contributions (in cash or labor) to tertiary system
management. An assurance was obtained that Government would cause all project ag ncies to monitor
and evaluate project activities and performance according to standards agreed with the Bank.
5.18    Progress Review and Reporting. Progress reports would be prepared by each province,
collated at the national level using the project coordination arrangemkcnts and submitted to the Bank on
a biannual basis. Each report would include a brief description of the main events during the reporting
period including construction/activity progress; expenditures incurred; project staffing status; tender
and award of contracts; functional irrigation area developed; actual areas irrigated; and incremental
agricultural production under the project. In this respect, the progress reports would summarize
performance indicators of: (a) physical and financial progress of project implementation compared to
agreed targets for all components; (b) operation and maintenance; (c) environmental impact; (d) project
technical and economic impact through indicators such as system efficiency, cropping intensity, crop
yields and net farm income; and (e) institutional capability, including staff training and strengthening of
WUAs. Thus Government would provide the Bank with half yearly progress reports, to a format
satisfactory to the Bank, on June 30 and December 31 each year after the start of the project together
with a Project Completion Report submitted no later than six months after project completion.



- 27 -
5.19    Annual Implementation Review. There would be at an annual implementation review of the
project about May/June each year so that findings can be reflected in the Government budgetary
process. The review would take place concurrent with a regular supervision mission. The annual
implementation review arrangement would give flexibility throughout the project period to, inter alia:
(a) evaluate on a regular and formal basis what deferred schemes are ready for implementation; (b)
amend individual provincial programs to reflect performance during the previous phase; (c) amend
work programs between agencies to ensure priority for essential components; (d) consider any other
'additional' scheme not reviewed at appraisal which would meet the project concept and objectives; and
(e) incorporate any lessons learnt in the course of the previous year. The first annual implementation
review would take place in about June/July 1991 to review the previous year performance and agree
the work program for budgeting the following year (FY 1992/93). The work program for FY 1991/92
was reviewed and agreed at negotiations. The project consultants (para 3.18) would assist in the
preparation and collafion of information for the annual implementation review and the responsible
agencies would ensure that the necessary performance data are available.
5.20    Multiple Financing. Many schemes proposed by the Government have ongoing programs
for civil works, tertiaries and land clearance and development financed by other donors (i.e. ADB and
OECF). All such schemes have been deferred until the ongoing programs financed from other external
sources are completed in order to: (a) determine scheme status on completion of other financed
programs, particularly with respect to land clearance and development and confirm what has to be done
under the project; (b) enable work programming and scheduling to be fully coordinated; (c) standardize
specifications and technical criteria; (d) avoid double financing; and (e) evaluate the impact of works
financed by other donors on scheme technical viability and potential benefits. The project consultants
would assist in this process (para 3.18).
5.21    Budget Procedures. Present procedures for channeling fL. .ds for development of irrigated
agriculture need to be strengthened. Funds are allocated by individual agency which makes inter-
agency coordination difficult to achieve. Since the budgetary procedure cannot be changed for a
specific Bank-financed project, the project procedure to review and agree on annual work programs
and budget provisions (para 5.10) would be used to address the shortcomings inherent in existing
budget procedures. Thus, the BAPPEDA of each province would prepare an annual coordinated work
program and budget proposal for each provincial scheme using the project coordination process. At
the national level, these provincial programs would be collated into coordinated project proposal for
BAPPENAS review and processing. The BAPPENAS review of the project and provincial proposals
would ensure that the work programs are coordinated and funded.
VI. PRODUCTION. MARKETING. FARM INCOMES AND COST RECOVERY
6.01    Agricultural Production. The project consists of manv discrete schemes spread over
13 provinces with substantial differences in climatic conditions, water availability, soils and farmer
capability. Since the project would complete existing irrigation schemes, there would be some increase
in agricultural production in existing irrigated areas with the main increase coming from the new areas
brought under command. The reliability of irrigation services, availability of developed land and land
titling (to provide collateral for credit) would contribute to increased production. The estimates of
agricultural production reflects the best average estimate of project impact on agricultural production
for the islands of Sumatra, Sulawesi and Kalimantan and are detailed in Annex 2. An impact analysis
has been made for each candidate scheme using conservative estimates of incremental production based
on crop intensity and yield data provided by the Ministry of Agriculture from limited data collected on
similar irrigated areas in each province.
6.02    Cropping Intensities. Land use, cropping patterns and intensities, crop yields and production
for the 'present' situation and the 'future without' and 'future with' project situations have been
estimated for each scheme included under the project. Data for three typical schemes in Sumatra,
Sulawesi and Kalimantan are given in Annex 1, Tables 8, 9 and 10 respectively. Present cropping
patterns for areas presently being irrigated vary greatly by scheme, depending on the status of



- 28 -
completion and the reliability of the -xisti.ag irrigation service. The cropping intensities are expected to
average about 150 to 175 percent over the total project command area. For some completed schemes,
the cropping pattem would include minor increases in secondary foods crops, but the main impact
would be an increase in rice cropping intensity to some 175 to 200 percent at full development
(depending on scheme location and province) through the project area. In existing irrigated areas of
schemes, increased cropping intensities would be possible because of increased water availability for
dry season cropping and supplementary irrigation to guarantee wet season crops. In newly irrigated
areas of completed schemes brought under cultivation, there would be a transfer from rainfed to full
irrigated agriculture resulting in an increase in crop production during both the dry and wet seasons.
6.03      rpX Yields. Crop production intensification methods, with use of HYVs and fertilizer
inputs, are well established in the project provinces. Farm surveys indicate that improved technology
has been adopted on more than 90 percent of the area planted with rice on the Outer Islands where
there is reliable irrigation supplies. The present average yield of irrigated HYV rice in Indonesia is
about 4.0 ton/ha (dry unhusked rice), while the average yield on the Outer Islands is 3.0 to 3.5 ton/ha
for irrigated areas and 2.0 ton/ha for rainfed rice. The 'present' and 'future without' project yields are
based on limited information provided by the Department of Agriculture. in each province. The 'future
with' project yields are estimated on the basis of existing irrigated yields with a full irrigation service
and, where applicable, take into account soil fertility and other physical resource factors. Where
existing irrigated yields of rice exceed some 3.5 ton/ha, long-term average yields (assuming a 6 to 10
year build-up) of 4.5 to 5.0 ton/ha are anticipated. On schemes where there is little or no irrigation at
present, or soils are poor, 4.0 to 4.5 ton/ha are anticipated. On soils with known constraints (high
acidity) that can be overcome with additional inputs and a higher level of management, long term
average yields of 3.5 to 4.0 ton/ha are anticipated. Soils with known constraints which cannot be
overcome are excluded. Rice yield increases in areas already irrigated, and on which no further capital
expenditures are scheduled, would be restricted to less than 0.5 ton/ha to reflect the general
improvement in irrigation services.
6.04    Diversification to palawija (non rice) crops is likely to be limited since the preferred cx-,, is
rice when irrigation is available. The yields of palawija crops using reliable surface irrigation supplies
are expected to increase significantly by full development: groundnuts from 0.6 to 1.2 ton/ba. maize
from 1.5 to 3.0 ton/ha and soybean from 0.6 to 1.5 ton/ha. Such yields are feasible for small, isolated
schemes which have been fully completed and developed, with improved O&M that ensures timely,
reliable and equitable water supplies, and with more efficient application of farm inputs through better
agricultural extension services.
6.05    The higher yields derive from the interaction of several factors including: (a) reliable and
timely irrigation service; (b) where applicable, improved drainage and flood control; (c) provision of
inputs (improved seeds, fertilizers, etc); (d) better access to markets; and (e) improvements to farm
practices through better extension and farner training. Of these factors, reliable irrigation supply is
critical because it reduces the risk of crop failures and induces farmers to apply other essential inputs.
Seeds, fertilizer and pesticides are generally readily available in the project areas and no supply
constraint is expected under the project.
6.06    Marketing. Estimated annual incremental production generated by the project at full
development is given in para 7.03. Additional rice producLion under this project is not expected to
cauns marketing problems. 'l he amount of crop surplus which could be sold and that consumed in the
producing areas would vary considerably between schemes. About 10 percent of the marketed rice is
acquired by Government and any marketable surplus of rice is expected to be sold as paddy by farmers
to private traders or millers. The numerous private traders, operating in villages close to most schemes
and village cooperative owned rice mills, would provide competition and offer services such as
collection and drying of produce. Milling capacity is expected to be adequate for incremental rice
production in most provinces. Marketing of palawija crops is handled almost entirely by private
traders and well established marketing channels exists, especially for soybean, maize and groundnuts.



- 29 -
6.07.   Financial Prices. Rice prices in Indonesia are largely determined by local supply and
demand. Although there are price fluctuations between locations, seasons and years, prices in the
open market are generally higher than Government's floor prices. The fertilizer subsidy, irrigation
investment and price policy through trade regulations have in the past been the major quantitative
instruments influencing rice production. However, fertilizer subsidies are now being phased down
and pesticide subsidies have been eliminated. Thus, financial prices for all commodities used in the
project analysis are based on farm surveys conducted by the M&E unit within DGWRD and on
information provided by MOA. Prices have been adjusted, as necessary, to the farmgate prices
observed during appraisal.
6.08    Farm Budgeta. Farm incomes in irrigated arcas where rice is the dominant crop are largely
determined by the farmgate price of rice relative to input costs. Off-farm income is generally needed to
supplement farm income because of small farrn size. Crop budgets were prepared for each of the
Outer Islands 1Q/ for different crop yields, varying from 1.5 ton/ha to 3.0 ton/ha for rainfed rice and
2.5 ton/ha to 5.0 ton/ha for HYV rice. The budgets cover a representative range of conditions and
have been adjusted for soil conditions of individual schemes. Gross margins and the costs of
production were estirated using 1990 prices. Detailed crop budgets are given in Attachment 1 of
Annex 2.
6.09    The payment capacity of irrigation beneficiaries would vary considerably through the project
provinces. To simplify the situation, it has been tested by the preparation of farm models with
holdings of 0.5 ha, 1.0 ha and 1.5 ha, considered to be representative of conditions on the Outer
Islands, and is summarized in Annex 1, Tables 5 and 6. Farm costs and returns were estimated at
current farmgate prices for these farm size holdings. In the farm models, it is assumed that the
minimum living cost for a farm family would be about Rp 600,000 per year. On this assumption, a
farm size of about one hectare would produce enough income to provide for the family's living costs
and have sufficient savings to pay for O&M costs. Farmers with less than one hectare would only
have sufficient income to maintain a minimum standard of living and pay for O&M if they earn extra
income from off-farm work.
6.10    O&M Funding. At present, the Government is faced with funding of O&M on about
4.8 million ha of irrigated lands and some 600,000 ha of reclaimed swamp lands. It is expected that
this area would be gradually reduced by the turnover of the smaller irrigation systems less than 500 ha
to Water Users Associations (WUAs). In the projections, it is assumed that some one million hectares
would be turned over to WUAs by about year 1998. By year 2000, the proposed irrigation service fee
(based on conservative estimates) should produce an annual revenue of about Rp 45.0 billion. For this
project, the underlying assumption is that only as areas are brought under EOM, would irrigated area
be considered ready for levying the irrigation service fee. On the Outer Islands, a prerequisite for this
to happen would be that all irrigation schemes are completed and the command areas fully developed
so that schemes can function effectively.
6.11    Cost Recovr. The Govemment's objective is to reduce and eventually eliminate central
government subsidies for efficient O&M. To achieve this objective, the Government would recover all
EOM costs through an irrigation service fee (ISF). In the meantime, the ISF would be supplemented
by provincial/local revenues (in lieu of central govemment budgetary support) through PBB (land and
property tax revenue). The levels of irrigation service fees and mechanisms for estimation, billing and
collection are being tested in the pilot areas under the ongoing ISSP (Loan 2880-IND) and such tests
would continue under a proposed follow-on project. Over time, as appropriate levels of irrigation
service fee are determined and institudonal mechanisms for its collection developed, it is expected that
all EOM costs could be recovered by irrigation service fee together with a portion of the capital costs.
However, this objective can only be achieved if schemes are completed and function effectively, and
farmers receive reliable and timely irrigation service.
lQ/ The assumptions on crop budgets used for Sulawesi have been used for Kalimantan.



- 30-
6.12    Cost recovery indices are estimated to: (a) indicate the extent to which an irrigation service
fee (starting from Rp 15,000/ha in year I to Rp 35,000/ha in year 6 and thereafter) would recover the
costs of EOM; and (b) show the extent to which this minimum service fee plus any reasonable
allocation of PBB for O&M expenditures would recover the cost of EOM and the investment cost of
special maintenance (over and above routine maintenance). The relevant data are given below:

6.13    In addition to the recovery of O&M costs for the primary and secondary systems (for
schemes larger than 500 ha) from the ultimate beneficiaries via the irrigation service fee, it should be
noted that Government's long-term policy is to turn over (privatize) all schemes less than 500 ha to the
WUAs, who will than own, manage, operate and maintain these systems. The costs of land clearance
and dcvelopment and other agricultural development activities that are implemented on individual
holdings would be recovered through the PPB which relates to land capability and use. The
appropriate level of PPB are to be determined once actual unit costs of the LC&D and other activities
for individual schemes are established.
VII. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
7.01    Project Benefits and Beneficiaries. This project complements previous Bank support to
irrigation development on the Outer Islands. Investments aimed at completion of ongoing projects and
improving efficiency of O&M of existing facilities would have higher economic returns than new
investments, since considerable investment costs have already been sunk. Furthermore, since most
schemes have been under implementation for an extended period of time, project benefits are likely to
be as anticipated. Where there is still uncertainty, schemes have been deferred until sufficient
information is available to prepare a practical and viable action plan.
7.02    The primary benefits of the project would be increased agricultural (rice) production. rhe
main beneficiaries would be farmers with land within the design command area of schemes, but which
has yet to be served by irrigation and fully developed. Farmers presently receiving some irrigation
would also benefit with the introduction of more timely and reliable water supply. Major quantifiable
benefits at full development would be incremental annual production of about 700,000 tons of wet



- 31 -
season paddy and some 400,()00 tons of dry season paddy. T'here would also be small increases in
productioni of other crops including about 12,000 tons of maize and somne increase in soybeans and
grounLdnLIts in areas not suitable for rice. About 127,000 rural families would benefit directly from the
new areas brought under irrigation with improvements for an additional 100,000 families presently
receiving an irrigation service. The project would settle some 1400 families on schemes in Sulawesi
and Kalirnantan. In addition, an equivalent of 90,000 full-time farm jobs are expected to be created as
a result of crop intensification. Since project schemes are spread throughout the provinces involved,
the project should result in significant indirect benefits in more isolated regions. In addition, the
project would generate important socio-economic benefits and alleviate poverty since about 30 percent
of the beneficiaries are expected to be existing transmigrants or local landless settlers. The project
would also alleviate poverty by generating additional labor demand, mainly benefitting the landless, for
on-farm activities and through labor intensive activities such as minor irrigation works and land
clearance and development.
7.03    The overall impact of the project in terms of increased agricultural production, employment
generated and farm families benefited at full development is summarized below:
Directly benefitting farm families (no.)               227,000
Families settled (no.)                                   1,400
Increase in irrigated area (ha)                        159,300
Area with improved irrigation service (ha)             113,500
Increased rice production (tons/year)                1,100,000
Increased of maize production (tons/year)               12,000
Generated farm employment (no. of jobs)                 90,000
7.04    Economic Justification. Economic evaluations have been carried out using both intemal
Economic Rate of Return (ERR) and Net Present Value (NPV) estimations. The assumptions and
calculations used in the economic justification are detailed in Annex 2. Economic analyses have been
calculated for all schemes under the project, with the results for typical schemes on Sumatra, Sulawesi
and Kalimantan given in Annex 1, Tables 7, 8 and 9. For 'deferred' schemes, cost estimates and
analyses assume that the schemes would be completed as appraised and also follow the
recommendations in the status reports and acdon plans proposed for ex-SPIDP schemes and the
scheme proposals on which ongoing schemes were justified for inclusion under the ISSP
(Loan 2880-IND).
7.05    Cost streams include all capital and operating costs, including physical contingencies, but
exclude price contingencies and transfer payments such as taxes and subsidies (Annex 3) and the sunk
costs to date. Cost estimates are summarized Annex 2, Attachment 2, which also gives the per hectare
cost for each scheme of sunk costs where available, the new costs, and the total unit costs to full
development. Agricultural benefits have been evaluated on a per hectare basis, making allowance for
the local physical determinants (such as rainfall and soils) in the provinces concerned. Benefits are
expressed in incremental crop yields and intensities and/or a prevention of decline in yields (resulting
from improved water distribution). Benefits from improved coordination of irrigated agricultural
development, technical assistance through the project supervision component and other institutional
support activities cannot be quantified directly to individual schemes, but would contribute to the
project impact. Hence the costs of these components have been included in the total economic cost of
the project.
7.06    An ERR for the nverall project has little significance since all 130 schemes are separate,
discrete investments. The objective of the economic analysis was to determine for each scheme,
whether proposed expenditures (excluding settlement costs) are economically viable and would
produce benefits sufficient to give an ERR of at least 12 percent. Schemes that are calculated to
produce an ERR of less than 15 percent have been considered marginal and have generally been
deferred (Annex b). For all schemes with low ERRs, proposed expenditures would be carefully
evaluated during the screening and review process (para 3.20) before schemes are approved for



- 32 -
implementation. If a candidate scheme still has an ERR of less than 12 percent, it would be excluded
from the project.
7.07    Economic indicators (ERRs and NPVs) for all ongoing and deferred schemes are
summarized in Annex 2, Tables 3 and 4 respectively, and detailed in Annex 2, Attachment 2. The
ERR for all schemes included under the project gives a weighted average of about 17.5 percent. For
'go-ahead' and 'deferred' schemes, the weighted average ERR wo'ild be about 20.1 percent and 16.6
percent respectively and ERRs of typical schemes are low given the large sunk costs to date (Annex 2,
Attachment 2). As proposed by Government, some 29 schemes have ERRs of less than 12 percent, as
detailed in Annex 2, Tables 3 and 4. Many of these schemes would however be economically viable if
unjustified expenditures that do not result in identifiable benefits are eliminated, and/or only those costs
that generate benefits inside the schemne comrnand area are included.
7.08    Sensitivitv Analysis. A sensitivity analysis has been undertaken for all schemes to test the
effects of variations in investment costs, variations in direct benefits and rice yields and the irigated
area to dry season rice and are summarized in Annex 2, Tables 3 and 4. Delays in implementation was
not tested since this is a negligible risk with all schemes substandally complete. The analysis has not
been undertaken for individual project components since all components are interdependent and
necessary to achieve tC.n scheme impact. Experience under previous Bank supported projects, in
particular, SPIDP, Loan 2375-IND, has shown that unless 41 activities are undertaken under a
coordinated program, irrigation schemes do not achieve projected benefits.
7.09    The analysis shows that schemes are relatively insensitive to cost overruns since most
schemes are substantially complete. An average 25 percent cost overrun on all project components
would lower the average ERR to about 14.7 percent. Schemes are more sensitive to reduced benefits
from both lower production, particularly in the dry season, and to a lesser extent crop prices.
A 20 percent reduction in benefits would lower the average ERR for all schemes from 17.5 percent to
about 9.3 percent. Thus, critical determinants in scheme viability are confirmation (through
hydrological measurements) of reliable water availability (para 5.13) and that completed schemnes are
sustained. The sensidvity of individual schemes varies widely as would be expected with the spread
of the project over 13 provinces. Some schemes, such as those in Kalimantan provinces would have a
relatively low ERR and thus be sensitive to changes in assumptions because the schemes are located in
remote areas with low potential. These schemes have been supported under the 'deferred' Sub-
category because of the important social and regional benefits.
7.10    Switching values have not been calculated for key parameters because of the number of
schemes indtuded under the project and lack of reliable data. However, based on some selected cases,
it is estimated that: (a) if the projected rice price during the 1990-2000 period were to decline by
20 percent, the ERR would on average fall by about four percentage points. However, if off-farm rice
prices were to drop substantially, farmers would probably diversify into other more profitable crops.
If the recurrent O&M expenditures on com 'leted schemes were reduced by 50 percent, with a
corresponding reduction in economic life, s . weighted average project ERR would fall from about
17.5 percent to less than 12.0 percent. This demonstrates the importance of sustaining schemes once
they are completed and fully developed. A similar negative impact would be expected on some
schemes if environmental aspects of the project are not adequately addressed.
Risks and Uncertainties
7.11   The risk of delayed or incomplete agricultural impact would be limited since the project
supports all activides required to fully complete and develop an irrigation scheme and obtain project
benefits. In addition, policy reforms introduced under the ongoing ISSP (Loan 2880-IND) should
result in all project schemes being sustained when they are completed and fully developed. These
reforms, which are now being applied under ISSP include: (a) improved O&M funding and cost
recovery from beneficiaries; (b) strengthening of provincial irrigadon services; (c) improved
coordination between irrigation, agricultural and local government services; and (d) development of
staff skills. These subsector initiatives should lead to better resource mobilization and utilization;



- 33-
improved efficiency in selection, design and construction of irrigation systems; and improved
operation and maintenance to the farm level.
7.12    Considerable institutional risks can be expected given recent Bank experience in Indonesia.
These institutional risks include: (a) inability to keep in place the organizational arrangements;
(b) inadequate staffing; (c) reliance on technical assistance; (d) unpredictable funding; and
(e) ineffective coordination of irrigated agricultural development. In addition, technical agencies have
to date focused on expenditures on construction programs with little attention to achieving benefits.
The project solution would contain these risks by strict control of funding through disbursement
conditionalities. Schemes and works/activities financed under the project would be specific, restricted
to priority expenditures, and include support for all agencies involved. In addidon, the focus on and
implementation of coordination/management procedures would reduce these risks. There would be a
risk of undisbursed loan amounts, if deferred schemes have eventually to be deleted. However,the
multi-agency approach under the project, with a direct financial interest for all agencies involved,
should increase public accountability for poor perforna nce. All agencies involved in implementation
of a scheme would suffer, if one agency does not perfo,,n. In addition, use of procedures for scheme
approval and project monitoring under BAPPENAS and JAPPEDA should stop one technical agency
following its own agenda and deferring action on its own problems.
7.13    TechnicAl risks would be contained through: (a) setting standards and criteria related to
planning and design; (b) ensuring that all schemes are formulated on an adequate and measured data
base, (c) coordinated procedures for scheme selection and implementadon, (d) verification and
certification of scheme completion, and (e) the requirement to prepare annual work programs, budget
requests and funding provisions. In particular, any 'deferred' scheme would remain deferred until a
technical status report and appropriate action plan (based on physical resource data) are prepared,
satisf?ctory to the Bank (para 3.20). Specific agreement would also be reached on the definition of
what constitutes a completed scheme (para 5.15) to avoid extended implementation periods. Tle
provision of consultant services (para 3.18) should help reduce risks related to the technical
supervision of schemes spread over 13 provinces.
7.14    Environmental risks are small since most project schemes are substantially complete and parts
of the command areas have been under irrigation for many years. Completion and full development of
schemes is not expected to produce a significant change in environmental conditions. However, many
schemes are located in remote parts of the Outer Islands and surrounded by environmentally sensitive
areas. The provision of a specific envixonmental protection component under the Ministry of Forests
would ensure that there is an agency, independent of the main works programs, evaluating
environmental impact. In this respect, specific provisions have been made to survey and plan for
induced environmental impact in areas surrounding scheme commands. Special attention would be
given to upper catchment impact and status of land bordering irrigated areas once schemes are fully
developed. The project would also: (a) exclude from land development any areas under reserve or
protected forest; (b) require an environmental assessment be made for deferred schemes (as required)
before the schemes are cleared for implementation; and (c) undertake special studies for schemes with
flood control and/or drainage problems and where downstream water use is sensitive to water quality.
A basic project assumption, agreed by Government, is that good environmental practices in the
irrigated agricultural subsector are nearly always synonymous to good irrigation, agricultuml and
forestry practices.



- 34 -
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
Assurances were obtained that Govemrnment would:
(a)  by August 15, 1991, employ consultants administered by DGWRD to assist with
project implementation and supervision, under terms of reference satisfactory to the
Bank (para 3.18);
(b) screen, select and commission project schemes in accordance to procedures agreed with
the Bank (para 3.21);
(c)  implement the project in accordance to technical, financial, administrative, agricultural
and environmental practices, standards and criteria satisfactory to the Bank, and
provincial work programs to be agreed annually with the Bank, by June 30 each year
(para 3.22);
(d)  (i) undertake detailed environmental assessments on deferred schemes satisfactory to
the Bank as part of the project screening and selection process; and (ii) cause
MOF/DGWRD to classify catchment areas of schemes as Protection Forests
(para 3.25);
(e) cause each project executing agency, at both national and provincial levels, to establish
and maintain adequate and separate accounts from the beginning of project
implementation and to maintain such accounts in accordance with the sound and
generally recognized accounting principles and practices satisfactory to the Bank, so as
to enable these agencies to provide annual financial statements to reflect the financial
performance and status of project components (para 4.15);
(f)  (i) keep all project related contracts, documents related to the SOEs, and orders and
receipts for at least one year after the Bank has received the audit report for the fiscal
year in which the last withdrawal from the loan account was made; (ii) maintain the
Special Account, including records and documentation of receipts and withdrawals, as
deemed satisfactory by the Bank; (iii) complete annual consolidated financial statements
of the project for audit within six months of the close of each Government fiscal year
for independent audit; and (iv) undertake independent audits of each year's consolidated
project accounts, including the audits of SOEs and the Special Account, and submit
such audit reports to the Bank within nine months of the close of each Govemrnment
fiscal year (para 4.15);
(g) cause MOA/DGFCA to provide appropriate staffing, facilities, services and the other
resources required to carry out effective agricultural extension satisfactory to the Bank
throughout all the proposed project districts (para 5.05);
(h) cause BAPPENAS and each provincial BAPPEDA respectively to undertake project
coordination at national and provincial levels respectively, in a manner satisfactory to
the Bank (para 5.10);
(i)  submit to the Bank for its review and comments, for each province under the project:
(i) not later than June 30 each year, a coordinated work program and cost estimates for
the following fiscal yeaw, and (ii), by September 30 each year, coordinated budget
proposals for the agreed provincial work programs (para 5.10);



- 35 -
(j) cause DGWRD/PRIS to: (i) collect and analyze regular and sufficient hydrologic and
climatic data to provide a continuous and reliable basis for assessing water availability
and allocation for each scheme under the project; (ii) implement all works required to
fully complete and develop each scheme under the project, as defined and agreed when
the scheme is approved for implementation by the Bank, before any additional
development to a scheme or any expansion to the scheme command area is undertaken
(paras 5.13 and 5.15);
(k) cause DGWRD/PRIS to certify and verify that such schemes are complete and can
function as designed before handing over project schemes to O&M authorities within
each PRIS or to the farmers' groups, as appropriate (para 5.16); and
(1)  cause all project agencies to monitor and evaluate project activities and performance
according to standards satisfactory to the Bank (para 5.17).
8.02    An understanding was obtained from Government that:
(a) vehicles required for the project would be purchased from Government budgetary
sources under a schedule agreed with the Bank (para 4.06).
8.03    Assurances were obtained that, as condidons of loan disbursement, Government would take
the following actions:
(a) for schemes in the deferred category, including any additional schemes financed under
the project, except for the SID component, obtain prior approval by the Bank to scheme
design, scope and estimated costs, and confirm that all agencies involved have cleared
the scheme for implementation under the project (para 4.11); and
(b)  (i) for all schemes, except for the SID component, in each of the project provinces,
Government would make a budget allocation to implement the agreed annual provincial
work program for each such province; and (ii) for irrigation worcs in each of the
project provinces, Government would make annual budgetary aliocations (and fumish
evidence that funds were expended in the previous year) for the fiscal year 1992/93,
and each subsequent fiscal year thereafter until project completion, which at least
maintain the fiscal year 1990/91 level of total nation-wide irrigation O&M financing
under the Central Government budget in real terms and, if required, provide any
incremental funding need for efficient O&M (para 4.11).
8.04    With the above assurances and understandings, the project would be suitable for a Bank loan
of US$ 125.0 million equivalent with a 20 year maturity at the Bank's standard variable rate, including
a grace period of 5 years. The Borrower would be the Republic of Indonesia.
