- 46 -                                  Annex 2
INDONESIA
PROVINCIAL IRRIGATED AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT
Assumptions for Project Analysis
I.    Introduction. The project area (spread over 13 provinces) has substantial differences in
climatic conditions, water availability, soils and farmer capability. The project analysis has thus
been limited to a single analysis of each scheme (Appendix 2 to this Annex) in which only minor
modifications can be incorporated. Refornulating schemes as a result of the analysis has not been
done since the data base, from which the costs and benefits are derived, was insufficiently detailed
to make an optimization exercise either reliable or worthwhile (Annex 5). An analysis of impact
has been made for each candidate scherme using a conservative estimate of prsiect impact based on
crop intensity and yields collected on simnilar irrigated areas in each province by the Ministry of
Agriculture . This data has been checked and adjusted (as necessary) to take account of the analysis
given in the Status Reports and Action Plans prepared under the SPII)P and ISSP (Loan Nos. 2375
and 2880-IND).
2.    Construction Conversion Factors. The construction of project civil works requires the use
of a mixture of equipment, materials, and skilled and unskilled labor. The Uded comQlgnet
includes capital-intensive works that require imported materials and hence a conversion factor of
1.00 has been used. It is estimated that about 55 per cernt of the construction costs fall in this
category. The non-traded component includes works that require skilled labor and locally
manufactured materials and thus 0.90 has been used. These works include about 45 per cent of
construction costs. For unskilled labor, a ratio of economic tc, financial wages equal to that of
agricultural labor (67 per cent) has been used for unskilled construction labor. Unskilled labor is
estimated to constitute about 20 per cent of the total construction costs. Thus, the following
conversion factors were used to convert financial to economic prices: (i) 1.0 for designs of
irrigation civil works, land clearance and development, settlement, agricultural extension, and
preparation for O&M; (ii) 0.9 for construction of irrigation civil works, land development, and
annual O&M. For all other goods, domestic prices were converted to economic prices using a
standard conversion factor (0.8).
3.    Economic Prices of Commodities. The economic prices .or traded commodities such as
rice, groundnuts, sugarcane, and cottor were derived by using border prices in UJS dollars
according to IBRD commoditv projections and were converted to local currency values at the
exchange rate assu1iaed under the project (Tables 1 and 2). The domestic cost compor;ents (local
transport, handling, and marketing charges) were adjusted by a SCF of 0.80. Analysis of price
trends for major non-traded foodgrains indicates that prices in local markets are close to parity with
prices in the world market, and therefore no adjustments have been made. The assumptions used in
the calculation of economic prices and crop budgets are rice, maize, soybeans and groundnuts take
into account assumed crop balances past and future for Indonesia as follows:
1985          1990           1995         2000
---------------------------('000 tons)-------------------------
Rice (milled)                -126          +720           +480          +82
Maize                         -1           -271            -78          +25
Soybeans                     -302          -493           -562         -623
Groundnuts                    -16            -2             -4           -2
N=:   (-) denotes import requirements; (+) denotes potential exports or reserves.
Siource: Supply and demand for food crops in Indonesia, 1988, Director General Foodcrops
Economics (DGFE) within the Ministry of Agriculture.



- 47 -                               Annex 2
Page 2
On the basis of this data and other considerations, it has been decided to price rice, maize and
groundnuts as if Indonesia were self-sufficient (in the long term) and soybeans as an import
substitute.
4.    Coffee, rubber and copra have been priced as export crops. Quality discounts for Indonesia
rubber and coffee have been calculated as the ratio between Bank's current price projections for
rubber and coffee prices CIF New York and prices published by the Bank Indonesia. The
percentage of the coffee crop suitable for export has been obtalik 1 from other Governmens reports.
5.    Prices of Agricultural Inputs. Indonesia exports substantial quantities of urea. Phosphate
and potasaic fertilizer are either imported or mnanufactured from imported raw materials. Therefore,
fertilizers have been valued at projected world market prices. Except in Kalimantan, lime is
generally widely available and is usually obtained locally. Economic pesticide prices have been
obtained from a list of pnces of a major local supplier. The econormic price of seed and
miscellaneous charges has been assumed to approximate their financial price. The economic price
of farm power has been derived from current daily rental rates adjusted by a conversion factor. A
summary of economic prices is presented in Table 1 of this annex.
6.    Opportunity Cost of Labor. Monthly farm labor wage rates vary throughout Indonesia,
depending on the opportunities for work, and are notably related to the extent of irrigation
development in any particular area. Also taken into consideration is that almost all project schemes
are located in remote and isolLted areas. Thus labor is priced at 80 per cent of the mnarket rate of
unskilled labor (men and women), in line with usual Bank practice outside Java, Bali and Lombok.
A market rate of Rp. 2750 per day (average for men and women), has been assumed in Sulawesi
and Kalimantan and Rp. 2500 per day for Sumatra. The Sumatra rate is affected by its proximity
and access to Java.
7.    Investment C . The capital costs of al! works and activities, including the charges for
project administration (and overheads), and physical contingencies were based on 1990 contract
rates adjusted by the SCF and are detailed in Annex 3. Works under each component to be carried
out under the project are assumed to fall equally in each construction year of each scheme unless
there are specific reasons to vary from this assumption. Civil works include additions for
supervision of construcdon (10 per cent), physical contingencies (a weighted average of 10 per
cent) and, where appropeiate, administration (up to 4 per cent). Value added tax (10 per cent) is
levied on all contracted works. In additdon, five schemes, either presently or previously financed
by the Bank have been included under the deferred sub-category RC (Annex 1, Table 2). The
scope and thus costs to complete and develop these schemes (and additional schemes that may be
included), cannot be decided at this time. Thus a cost provision equivalent to 10 per cent has been
added to the annual base costs of all works components to allow for financing such scheres under
the project.
8.    Operation and Maintenance Costs. The costs of preparation for O&M (PROM) are based on
(i) equipment and transport for the provincial irrigation service at the provincial, district and scheme
levels; (ii) preparation of a plan for operation and maintenance which includes trial operational runs,
analysis of how to run the system, O&M documentation and preparation of an O&M manual, and
(iii) some field staff training. The costs for item (i) will vary between provinces depending on their
individual situations, whereas costs for items (ii) and (iii) are more or less standard, and are directly
applicable to each scheme. Costs for item (i) above are assigned to each scheme on a total scheme
area basis, which would tend to overestimate PROM costs as only parts of the total command areas
are to be completed and developed under the project. Th.s is justified since the PROM component
would apply to the overall scheme management and most schemes at present have no formal
arrangements for O&M. The average cost for EOM after scheme completion has been estimated at
about Rp. 28,000 per lia per year (in 1990 prices), based on a survey of schemes supported under
the ISSP (Loan 2880-IND), as detailed in Annex 3, Table 15.



-48-                                     Annex 2
page 3
9.    Other Components. Land clearance and development costs have been estimated from unit
rates provided by DAAD, based on ongoing land clearance and development programs in all
provinces under the project (Annex 6). The (downstream) agricultural development costs have also
been generated on a scheme by scheme basis using current unit rates for the various activities
(Annex 7). The KUD facilities (provision of inputs and a guaranteed market for selected farm
products), village improvements (provision of market facilities and other related community
infrastructure) and forest protection costs have not been included within the economic analysis.
KUD services are to some extent accounted for in the calculadon of farm prices. However, as any
failure to provide such facilides would have serious effects on agricultural production, each
provincial BAPPEDA has been advised of their importance and the need to make provision for them
through normal budgetary channels. Unit costs for settlement, provided by the Ministry of
Transmigration, are estimated at about Rp 6.0 to 7.0 million per settled family depending on
location. Unit costs were estimated by BPN (in Jakarta) based on for land titling and certification
after land development was completed. Additional costs for other BPN activities have been include,
inter alia. as required on an individual scheme basis for: (i) investigation, inventory and cadastral
survey; (ii) preliminary certificate (Hak Tanah); (iii) land certification; and (iv) tax.
10.   Crop Yields. The assessment of incremental benefits is based on estimating the difference
between the agricultural production in the 'future with' and 'future without' project situations. The
'future without' yields are based on information provided by the Department of Agriculture and
reflect present rainfed yields. The 'future with' yields are also estimated on the basis of existing
yields under reliable irrigation service and, where applicable, take into account soil fertility and
other factors. Where existing yields on irrigated land exceed 3.5 tons per hectare, a long term
average (with a 6 to 10 year build-up) to 4.5 - 5.0 tons per hectare are anticipated. Some 4.0 - 4.5
tons per hectare are anticipated where existing yields are low, or not known (little or no irrigation at
present), or where some soils may be infertile, but with no other serious constraints On soils with
known constraints such as high acidity but which can be overcome with additional inputs and
higher levels of management long teim average yields of 3.5 - 4.0 tons per hectare are anticipated.
Soils with known constraints which cannot be overcome are excluded. The justification of higher
yields is the interaction of improved inputs (improved seeds, fertilizers, crop protection) and a
sufficiently reliable water supply to encourage their use. Good drainage, markets, storage,
extension and training have also been taken into account. Incremental benefits on areas already
irrigated (and on which no further capital expenditure is scheduled) have been conservatively
restricted to between zero and 0.5 tons per hectare depending on the local circumstances.
11.   Crp Budgets. To simplify the analysis of a large number of individual schemes and to
reflect the quality of the data base, typical crop budgets (and thus benefit streams) were generated
for all schemes within Sumatra and Sulawesi X pectively. The assumptions used for Sulawesi
were applied to schemes in Kalimantan. Similarly, although experience with irrigation schemes in
Indonesia by and large suggests that increases in cropping intensities to full development are
achieved more rapidly than increases in yields, it is assumed that this is applicable to new rather
than to existing (ongoing) schemes.
12.   Crop budgets for Sulawesi and Sumatra schemes are presented in Attachment 1,
Tables 1 and 2 respectively. Some reductions in returns to rice cultivation have been incorporated
into the budgets because of increases in fertilizer requirements over the yield range of 3.8 to 4.3
tons per hectare, increase in labor requirements and input price increases. The budgets only cover a
representative range of conditions and are occasionally adjusted for individual scheme conditions
(such as soil or water constraints). It is assumed that the farmer objective on each scheme would be
to grow two crops of rice provided there is sufficient water, since rice produces the highest returns
to irrigation and the crop is less affected by acidity in puddled condidons. However the
combination of high fertilizer requirements (on isolated schemes), even for relatively modest yield
levels, and the virtually constant labor requirements per hectare, produces a plateau of gross
margins over the yield range of between 3.0 and 4.0 tons per hectare. This plateau occurs
especially in Sumatra where average long term yield projections have seldom exceeded 4.0 tons per



-49 -                                 Annex 2
Page 4
hectare. This situation, allowed for in the analysis, also illustrates why schemes are relatively
insensitive to shortfalls in yields below 4.0 tons/ha and highly sensitive to increases in yields over
4.0 tons per hectare.
Financial Anaysis
13.   Earm Budgets. Farm incomes in the irrigated areas, where paddy is the dominant crop, often
planted in two seasons and sometimes even the third season, are largely determined by the farmgate
price of rice relative to input costs. Farm budgets were prepared for each of the Outer Islands for
different crop yields (budgets) varying from 1.5 tons/ha to 3.0 tons/ha for rainfed rice and 2.5 tons/ha
to 5.0 tons/ha for HYV rice. The budgets only cover a representative range of conditions and have
been occasionally adjusted for individual scheme conditions. Gross margins and the costs of
production were estimated using 1990 prices.
14.   The payment capacity of irrigation beneficiaries would vary considerably through the project
provinces. To simplify the situation, it has been tested by the preparation of farmn models with
holdings of 1.5 ha, 1.0 ha and 0.5 ha were considered to be representative of conditions on the Outer
Islands for 3 size groups summarized below Farm costs and returns were estimated at current
farmgate prices for these farn size holdings. In the farm models, it is assumed that the minimum
living cost for a farm family would be about Rp 50,000 per month, or some Rp 600,000 per year.
On this assumption, a farm size of about one hectare would produce enough income to provide for the
family's living costs and have sufficient savings to pay additional O&M costs. Farmers with less
than one hectare would only have sufficient income to maintain a minimum standard of living if they
eamed extra income from off-farm work. Thus for small farms, off-farm income would be necessary
to generate sufficient savings to cover an irrigation service fee.

15.   As can be seen from the above, farmers with less than about one hectare would only acquire
payment capacity by working off-farm to help defray their minimum living costs. Even if farmgate
rice prices of all production would be raised to the Government price support levels, it would still not
enable the farmer with a 0.5 ha holding to meet minimum living costs and taxes and other obligatory
expenses. There would be no surplus of earnings to pay irrigation service fees unless his farm
income is supplemented with off-farm eamings.



- 50 -                                 Annex 2
Economic Analysis.
16.   Backgrund. Economic evaluation of the project has been carried out using both internal
Economic Rate of Return (ERR) and Net Present Value (NPV) estimations. The analysis has not
been calculated for individual project components, since all components are interdependent and
necessary to achieve a scheme impact. Experience under previous Bank supported projects (such as
SPI)P, Loan 2375-IND) has shown that unless all activities are undertaken under a coordinated
program, irrigation schemes do not achieve projected benefits. Furthermore, an overall project
ERR has little significance since all 130 schemes are separate, discrete investments. Thus the
analysis has been used primarily to assess whether individual schemes proposed by Government
should be financed. The results for typical schemes on Sumatra, Sulawesi and Kalimantan are
given in Annex 1, Tables 7, 8 and 911. In the case of deferred schemes, the cost estimates and
analyses assume that schemes would be completed as presented in the revised Project (Post
Appraisal) Report (September 1990) and that the implementing agencies would also follow the
recommendations in the status reports and action plans proposed for ex-SPIDP schemes and the
scheme proposals on which ongoing schemes were justified for inclusion under the ISSP (Loan
2880-IND).
17.   Cost Streams. Cost streams include all capital and O&M costs, including physical
contingencies, but excluding price contingencies and transfer payments such as taxes and subsidies
(Annex 3). Cost estimates do not include the substantial sunk costs to date, detailed for each
scheme in Annex 2, Attachment 2. The estimated cost of roudne O&M (EOM) per unit area is given
in Annex 3, Table 16. Benefits from improved coordination of irrigated agricultural development,
technical assistance through the consultant services and other institutional support activities cannot
quantified directly to individual schemes, but would accrue to the project. Hence the costs of these
components have been included in the total economic cost of the project.
18.   Benefit Streams. The assessment of economic benefits is based on estimating the difference
between the agricultural production in the 'future with' and 'future without' project situations. The
production objective for each scheme is to grow two crops of rice (as the preferred crop), provided
there is sufficient water. This assumption can be justified because most schemes are located in
traditional rice growing areas, rice produces the highest returns to irrigation and the crop is less
affected by acidity in puddled conditions.
19.   Economic Rates of Return. The ERRs and NPVs for the project provinces are summarized
in Tables 3 and 4 of this annex for ongoing and deferred schemes respectively. The ERRs for
individual schemes are summarized in Attachment 2, Tables 1 to 5. The analysis shows that the
ERR for all schemes under the project have a weighted average of about 17.5 per cent with the
range of ERRs varying from 7.4 to 40.0 per cent for 'go-ahead' schemes and from 1.2 to 32.4 per
cent for 'deferred' schemes. The ERRs of typical schemes are low given the large sunk costs to
date. For many schemes, the ERRs calculated for this project are lower than when the schemes
were submitted to the Bank under other operations (SPIDP and ISSP) with the investments under
previous Bank financing excluded as sunk costs. It is therefore concluded that for some schemes,
the main implementing agencies have proposed expenditures for many components that cannot be
justified in terms of incremental benefits. All such schemes that fit the project concept and are
potentially viable have been placed in the 'deferred' category and would be subject to careful review
and evaluation.
20.   Tables 3 and 4 of this annex show that, as proposed at appraisal, some 25 'deferred'
schemes and 4 'go-ahead' schemes have ERRs of less than 12 percent. Most of these schemes
1/ The ERRs and NPVs for each scheme included under the project are detailed in Annexes 1 to 13
of the Project (Post Appraisal) Preparation Report, DGWRE, September 1990 and are available in
the Project File.



51i-                                    Annex 2
Page
would probably be economically viable if unjustified expenditures (i.e. expenditures that do not
result in identifiable benefits) would be eliminated. For all deferred schemes with low ERRs,
proposed expenditures would be carefully evaluated during the screening and review process (para
3.03, main report) before schemes are approved for implementadon. If a candidate scheme still an
ERR of less than 12 percent, it would be excluded from the project. Of the four 'go-ahead'
schemes with ERRs less than 12 percent, three have command areas of less 900 ha and their cosis
appear to have included items, particularly agricultural development, that would produce benefits
from outside the scheme command area and have not been included. The analysis of these schemes
would be reviewed to establish the cause of low ERR and appropriate acdon taken before
implementation.
Sensitivity Analysis.
21.   A sensitivity analysis has been undertaken on all schemes to test the effects of variations in
investment costs and direct benefits (variations in rice yields and area to dry season rice) and are
summarized in Tables 3 and 4 of this annex. Delay in implementation was not tested since this is
considered to be a small risk with almost all schemes substantially complete.
22.   Schemes are relatively insensitive to cost overruns since all schemes are on-going and
substantially complete. The analysis shows that a 25 percent cost overrun on all project
components would lower the weighted average ERR to about 14.7 percent. Schemes are more
sensitive to reduced benefits from both lower production, particularly in the dry season, and to a
lesser extent crop prices. A 20 percent reducdon in benefits would lower the weighted average
ERR for all schemes from 17.5 percent to about 9.3 percent. Thus a critical determinant in scheme
viability would be to confirm availability of reliable water supplies through hydrological
measurements and the land capability through soil surveys, where applicable (para 5.13).
23.   The sensitivity of individual schemes varies widely, as would be expected, with the spread
of the project over 13 provinces. Some schemes, such as those in Kalimantan provinces, would
have a relatively low ERR, and thus be sensitive to changes in assumptions, because the schemes
are located in areas with low potential. These schemes have been included under the project
because of the important social and regional benefits.
24.   Switching values have not been calculated for key parameters because of the complexity of
the calculation with the large number of individual schemes included and lack of reliable data.
However, based on some selected cases, it is estimated that: (i) if the projected rice price during the
1990-2000 period were to decline by 20 per cent, the ERR would, on average, fall by about four
percentage points. If rice prices were to drop substantially, market forces would come into play and
many farmers would probably diversify into other more profitable crops. If the recurrent O&M
expenditures on completed schemes were reduced by 50 per cent, with a coriesponding reduction in
economic life, the weighted average project ERR would fall from about 17.5 per cent to less than
12.0 per cent. This demonstrate the importance of sustaining all irrigation schemes on the Outer
Islands once they are completed and fully developed.

Generl Notes:
Al    Estimated costs are based on the scheme proposals as submitted in June/July 1990 which
take into account the tindings of the appraisal mission. The scope and estimated costs of all
'deferred' schemes are subject to change after further survey, investigation and design.
The costs would be updated at each Annual Implementation Review, and/or when deferred
schemes are approved by the Bank for implementation.
h/    The nature of the proposed project is such that an implementation schedule in terms for
works/components cannot be usefully identified in summary tables or charts. The detailed
implementation schedule in physical terms for each scheme is available in the Project File.
The rate of implementation in terms of estimated expenditures per year is shown on
Tables 11, 12, 13 and 14 for the various project components.

*                      ~~~~~~~~Page I
IWNDONSIA
PROVINCIAL IRRIGATED2 AGRICULTURE DEVELOPNMENTfPROJECr
Poject Coordination
Present Situat
1.     Lack of coordination in irrigated agricultural development between the Ministries and
agencies at national and provincial levels has been a serious shortcoming, particularly with respect
to irrigation development on the Outer Islands. One of the main objectives of the proposed project
is to put in place procedures and a process to improve coordinadon and management of (and thus
accountability for) project implementation. An important start has been made during project
preparation at national and provincial levels with BAPPENAS and the provincial BAPPEDA
undertaking project coordination.
2.      One Ministry or agency cannot deliver the benefits from either its own work program or
irrigated agricultural development without interacting and coordinating with the other agencies
involved. Thus the development impact of the proposed project would depend on effective
cooperation and coordination between the Ministries of Public Works, Agriculture and
Transmigration (where applicable), the National Land Agency (BPN) and the respective provincial
agencies. Until recently, each of these agencies have been primarily concerned with its own
activity, with limited focus in the overall performance of an irrigation scheme. Since there is little
accountability for performance, work programs of each agency have continued on separate parallel
tracks. Each organization tends to set its own work program, follow its own budget provision and
set priorities and functions as if it was a self-contained entity. In most cases, once an agency has
secured its budget provision for a year, the overriding aim is to fully utilize the allocated funds in
the fiscal year irrespective of the progress in parallel, inter-related activities. When program
changes are necessary, individual agencies are generally unwilling (or unable for budgetary
reasons) to modify their own programs. This makes voluntary and informal arrangements for
coordination of irrigated agricultural development difficult to implement. Finally, there has been a
focus on expenditure, rather than an interest in whether the expenditures result in a measurable
impact. It is essential that the coordination problems be addressed if irrigated agriculture is to
deliver predicted benefits in a timely manner.
3.      Thus, the challenge with respect to inter-agency coordination and cooperation in irrigated
agricultural development relates, inte , to the following main issues: (i) the quality of the
planning and design with special attention to project and diagnostic analysis; (ii) the timely
completion of ongoing schemes; (iii) timely provision of local funding and coordination of
budgetary provisions; (iv) data collecdon, monitoring and evaluadon and reporting; and (v) reliance
on technical assistance and training. A basic issue is the lack of coordinated and reliable data to
enable management to monitor project pcrformance. Thus, the Government at national and
provincial levels has difficulty in defining the status of implementation of a scheme, particula:ly
when the scheme is small and isolated (as on the Outer Islands). The institutional support, assisted
by project consultant services through the projec. coordination working groups, and the
comprehensive scope of the proposed project would help address all of these issues.
4.    National Level Coordination. Experience on other Bank supported projects and preparation
of the proposed project has shown that the coordination required cannot be made the responsibility
of one of the technical Ministries or agencies. To resolve this practical issue at national level,
BAPPENAS would undertake coordinatica. A project national coordinadon working group
consisting of representatives of the project Ministries/agencies, chaired by BAPPENAS with a
Coordinating Secretary, and assisted by consultant services undertook project preparation.



- 88 -                       Annex 4
Page 2
5.    The main coordinating function at national level has been to provide technical assistance and
guidance, and generally review and coordinate provincial programs to ensure uniformity of
planning and design standards, criteria and cost estimates. In addition, it has started to address
some major policy (general and technical) issues that require special attention. These issues are
generally inter-agency in nature and have been deferred or not addressed because there is no
effective forum to discuss and formulate an inter-agency acdon plan that takes into accounts the
needs (and constraints) of each agency involved. Another important function has been to undertake
overall scheme supervision and scheme/project analysis and some diagnostic analysis. Most
provinces have little or no previous experience in these tasks which are essential if schemes are to
be implemented on schedule, unjustified expenditure programs are to be contained and priority is to
be given to achieving benefits from investments as quickly as possible.
6     Provincial Level Coordination. The BAPPEDA of each project province have coordinated
project preparation through provincial coordinating working groups chaired by BAPPEDA and
comprised of representatives from the provincial technical agencies involved in the project (public
works, agriculture, BPN, transmigration and in some provinces, forestry). The provincial
coordination process has started to work and there is a growing realization in most provinces of
present shortcomings and the substantial additional costs and loss of benefits through piecemeal
irrigated agricultural development. The main tasks for provincial coordination have been to review
candidate schemes, prepare coordinated work proposals and programs for selected schemes, and
identify provincial policy issues associated with scheme completion. An important step forward
under the j   t preparation has been that the provinces have taken increased responsibility for
planning, es aiuation and analysis of their own work programs.
7.    District level Coordination. Overseeing of project implementation is under the district
Bappedas through the Irrigation Committee, which is represented by Cabang Dinas irrigation,
Kabupaten agricultural service and other related services, and headed by the local Bupati. rhe
Bupati, as chairman of this committee, obtains technical guidance from the project implementation
units (i.e. PRIS, PRAS, transmigration and BPN), and the district services of the concerned
agencies. Under the guidance of the district level Irrigation Committee, a project implementation
unit has been established in each of the pilot areas for irrigation service fees (to be continued and
expanded under the proposed ISSP 2) operating at provincial/scheme level. These arrangements
work if schemes are properly prepared and funding is adequate.
Project Coordination
8.    Project success would depend on BAPPENAS and each provincial BAPPEDA respective!y,
maintaining effective project coo.-dination working units at national and provincial levels. Thus the
coordination arrangements used during project preparation (paras 4, 5 and 6 above) would be
maintained and strengthened during project implementation. A project objective for each province
would be to develop and strengthen the capability to undertake technical analysis and cost/benefit
evaluations for schemes, in particular, the deferred schemes included under the project. The
procedures and process for coordination of project implementation would be based on those used
during project preparation. These arrangements would be tested, standardized and strengthened as
described in Attachment 2 of Annex 5. To ensure that project coordination works and can be
monitored, each province under the project would prepare coordinated work programs and cost
estimates each year. The annual work programs would be reviewed early enough to enable such
programs to be changed if they are incomplete or uncoordinated. Thus, by the end of September
each year, coordinated budget proposals for the agreed provincial work programs should be
prepared. If issues related to the work programs cannot be resolved by Government within the time
frame of the budgetary process, the Bank may take appropriate action under the conditions of
disbursement.



- 89-                            An;
PAgc 3
9.      Consultant Services. To assist in implementation and supervision, project consultant
services, consisting of a joint venture of foreign and local consultants, would be provided at the
national level, responsible to BAPPENAS and administered by DGWRD. The consultant's main
tasks would be to assist in the: (i) supervision of coordinated project implementation for both
irrigation and agricultural activities; (ii) review and analysis of schemes; and (iii) verification and
certification process to confirm that schemes are complete and ready to be handed over for O&M.
The consultants team would include thre intemational consultants (specialized in general iirigation
development, irrigated agriculture and agro-economics) together with national consultant staff to
assist and monitor the provinicial programs. Draft terms of reference are given in Attachment 1 to
this Annex. Selection of consultants with extensive experience of irrigated agricultural development
in all of the project provinces is critical to project success.
10.   Institutional Support. The process of coordinating project activities at provincial levels has
already identified areas where institutional support is required. The project would provide support
to each province through financing facilities, services, vehicles and equipment, and limited project
specific training for BAPPEDA staff so that the PPCWG can function effectively. The objective
would be to strengthen the capability in each province as described in para 6 of this annex. A
tentative list of items to be financed and estimated costs are detailed in Annex 3, Table 13. The
NPCWG would monitor the institudonal performance of each province and take appropriate action
if additional support in required. An additional support would be reviewed by the Bank during the
annual implementation review. In respect to training, the project would support overseas training in
project and diagnostic analysis for two BAPPETA officials from each province. General training
and technical institutional strengthening are covered under many other projects supported by the
Bank and other donors and thus would not be duplicated under this project.
Coordination and Maiiagement Issues
11.    The following sections of the annex outline some important issues related to coordination
and the general management of irrigated agricultural development that should be given special
attention through project implementation .
12.    Farmer Participation. Irrigation schemes would benefit from an active and strong
beneficiary involvement in the planning, implementation and the O&M of completed schemes. In
this respect, water user associations (WUAs) should be set up on all schemes under the preparation
for O&M (PROM) component and the WUAs invited to participate in the verification process when
a scheme is to be formally certified as complete (Annex 5). Special attention would be given with
respect to schemes which would be candidates for handing over to farmer management.
13.    Monitoring and Evaluation. Monitoring and evaluation (M&E) of both project
implementation and performance has been generally poor on previous Bank supported projects. It
has often not been possible to define with certainty the physical status of schemes evei. when a
scheme is designated as complete. Thus information management should be given special attention
under the project, since each project agencies responsible for monitoring and evaluating the physical
and financial progress of their own programs. An important task would be to ccllect relevant
project data on implementation and performance of individual components and schemes and then
process and collate this data at provincial and central level to enable efficient project management.
There would be considerable advantages in standardizing the formats of the project reporting and
M&E so that each province collects and presents informration in the same way. Table 1 of this
annex shows a suitable format that could be useful for the efficient management of a typical scheme.
If such a form was kept up to date, it would provide implementing agencies at scheme, provincial
and national levels current status of the project. Project reporting and M&E would be processed
through the respective coordination working groups.



90-                             Annex 4
Page 4
14.    project Supervision. A major shortcomning during project preparation and urder previous
Bank supported operations has been the limited capability of Ministries/agencies involved in
irrigated agricultural development to supervise their activities. The Bank cannot undertake an
effective supervision role of these activities with Ats limited resources available (Annex 10), unless
Govemment and the agencies involved keep up-to-date, reliable and comprehensive records.
Considerable progress has been made through the setting up of national and provincial working
groups under project preparation in identifying processes and procedures to collect and collate
infornation. However, project preparation has depended heavily on support of consultants to
advise on technical issues, analyze provincial preparation proposals for consistency and viability
and to collate the various informnadon from the provinces. The consultant services during project
implementation will be considerably reduced from the preparation level. In this respect, Table 1 to
this annex gives a tentative check list of main activities that should be supervised on a regular basis,
and for wlich full documentation should be kept.
15.    Funding and Budget Arrangements. Coordination of funding and procedures for
channeling funds for development of irrigated agriculture clearly need to be considerably
strengthened. While funds are allocated by individual agency, inter-agency coordination will
continue to be difficult to achieve. Since the budgetary procedure cannon. be changed for a si)ecific
Bank financed project, the project procedure to review and agree annual work programs and budget
provisions (para 5.1 1, main text) would be used to address the shortcomings inherent in existing
budget procedures. Each year, each provincial BAPPEDA would prepare a coordinated work
program and budget proposal for each scheme in the Province through the provincial coordination
process. The NPCWG (assisted by Cie consultants) would collate the provincial proposals and
prepare the coordinated project proposal for BAPPENAS review and processing. The BAPPENAS
review of the project and provincial proposals would ensure that the work programs are coordinated
and funded.
16.    Project Accounting and Auditing. An important requirement under the project is the
preparation of accounts and audits satisfactory to the Bank. This task is made complex by the
number of components and spread of the project over 13 provinces. It is important that the process
is efficiently managed since each executing agency would have to prepare and process separate
financial accounts. These accounts would have to be collated and audited at national level,
following standard GOI procedures. In addition, the accounts would have to be prepared in such a
way to enable project activities to be separately identified. A provision is made for consultants to
advise on the procedures to process accounts and audits and, in particular, recommend how to
simplify the accounting process to satisfy the time frame set by the Bank for submission of financial
accounts and audits.
17.    Scheme Reference Numbers. To assist in the management (particularly accounting and
disbursement) of the project, all schemes have been given a reference number which identifies the
status of the scheme and the provincial location as shown on Tables 1 and 2, Annex 1. Each
scheme has a 7-digit reference number. The first digit is '1' for go-ahead schemes and '2' for
deferred schemes. When a scheme moves fron 'deferred' to 'go-ahead' the first digit would
change from '2' to 'l'. The next four digits define the province (i.e 'SSUM' for South Sumatra
with the other provinces defined as shown in Table 1, Annex 1). The last two digits identify the
specific scheme by a number (Oi, etc.). Sample reference numbers for a typical go-ahead and
deferred scheme are as follows: (i) for 'goahead' S , the Kaiti Samo scheme in Riau
Province would be L for go-ahead; 'RIAU' for Riau Province; and a number aQL to define the
scheme (i.e the scheme reference number is IRAIUO01); and (ii) for 'deferred' Schemes, the Alun
Dua scheme in South Sumatra would be Z for deferred; M1j for South Sumatra Province;
and a number E to define the scheme (i.e the reference number is 2SSUM05).

IND20NESI
PROVINCIAL IRRIGATED AGRICULTRE DEVELQPMEN717 PROJEC
Teryns of Reference - Project Consultant Services
1.     A major challenge identified during project preparation has been the need to strengthen the
capability of Ministries/agencies involved in irrigated agricultural development to coordinate and
supervise their activities. Thus, the BAPPENAS coordination arrangements, set up for preparation
consisting of representatives of the mninistries/agencies and chaired by BAPPENAS, would be
maintained through project implementation. These would support the provincial BAPPEDA
coordination arrangements and would be assisted by consulting services comprising a composite
team of foreign and local consultants, retained and administered through DGWRD.
Duties of the Consultants
2.      The mnain objective of the consulting services would be to assist the project authorities so
that the project is implemented in accordance with the technical, economic and environmental
standards, procedures and criteria agreed by the Bank and Government, detailed in Attachments 2
and 3, Annex 5. A check list of main project acdvities to be supervised on a regular basis, and for
which full documentation would be kept, is sumrarized in Table 1, Annex 4. The consultants
would fulfill the project objective by assisting in: (i) providing technical assistance and guidance to
provincial Government/agencies; (ii) generally reviewing and coordinating provincial programs to
ensure uniformity of planning and design standards, criteria and cost estimates; and (iii) advise on
the general management of the project, including accounting and audits procedures. With respect to
item (iii), an important issue for project management is the spread of the project over 13 provinces
and the large number of schemes and agencies involved.
3.     The consultants would thus assist in the following main tasks: (i) formulating
recommendations with respect to any policy (general and technical) issues that may arise; (ii)
implementation of all project activities; (iii) routine reporting, monitoring and evaluation procedures;
(iv) review the preparation and analysis of deferred and any additional schemes; (v) the setting up
and introduction of the verification and cerification process to confirm that schemes are ready for
turn over to routine O&M; and (vi) identify and fonrmulate recommendations of any generic technical
issues that should be addressed by Government. With respect to item (vi), the consultants would
continue with the work started under the project preparation and presented in a series of Discussion
Papers listed in Annex I1l. An important function would also be to advise and train Government
staff in project and diagnostic analysis to ensure priority is given to achieving benefits from
investments as quickly as possible and limit work programs to justified expenditures.
4.    Deferred and Additional Schemes. Th%; consultants would give special attention to assisting
Government in the preparation and processing of 'deferred' schemes so that the y ran be put up to
the Bank for implementation clearance. All schemes included under the project whiTh have been
supported under either the SPIDP (Loan 2375 - IND) or under the ISSP (Loan 2880 - IND) have
explicit planning and design criteria, satisfactory to the Bank and agreed by Government, which
were used to screen and select the schemes for inclusion under the respective Loans. Thus, with
respect to 'deferred' schernes, the consultants would assist the responsible implementing agencies
to ensure that scheme proposals, inter alia: (i) take into account the planning and design standards
and criteria agreed when the schemes were originally approved for financing by the Bank; (ii)
comply with specific criteria agreed under the proposed project; and (iii) take into account the



- 94 -                            Anne 4
Att,acwh,ment I
page 2
recommendations related to technicatl and economic viability in the project preparation documents,
the status reports and the action plans prepared for schemes financed under the SP!DP 1/ and
ISSP 2/. These planning and design standards and criteria would also be applied to 'deferred'
schemes included under the project that have not been previously fin.anced under either the SPIDP
or ISSP (and to any additional schemes that may be proposed by Government for inclusion under
the project). Within the scope of the tasks described above, the consultants would give special
attention to the following activities Y:
(a)  S                                    The consultants would advise and assist in the
introduction and application of procedures for evaluating and screening deferred (and/or
additional) schemes and for the implementation of the project at national and provincial
level. The procedures are outlined in Attachment 2 of Annex 5.
(b)  Planning and Design. The consultants would advise on the collection and evaluation of
physical resource data, including hydrology, climate, topography, soils and land
capability and land ownership such that it is sufficient (in terms of quality and quantity)
for scheme planning and design.. They would assist in the review of the scheme design
to confrmn the schemes conform to the design objectives and criteria given in Attachment
3, Annex 5. They would assist in the scheme analysis (and any diagnostic analysis, as
required) to ensure that schemes are technically and economically viable and would thus
able to achieve the predicted agricultural impact in terms of cropping area and crop
intensities and yields. In addition, the consultants would advise on irrigation operational
principles and O&M plans for project schemnes, and assist in the formulation of practical
procedures to initiate scheme operation through the PROM activity and after a scheme is
handed over for routine O&M.
(c)  Verification and Certification of Scheme Completion. An important step in irrigation
development is the transfer of a scheme from the implementation phase to the O&M stage.
The objective would be to undertake equivalent to a technical audit to test that a scheme is
complete and can function as designed in accordance with procedures and criteria,
satisfactory to the Bank, before handing over project schemes to O&M authorities. To
achieve this objective, the consultants would assist in the formuiation and introduction of
detailed procedures to: (i) formally test and verify the satisfactory functioning of the
completed irrigation system; and (ii) confirm that the command area is fully developed
and to certify that schemes are complete and ready to be turned over for routine O&M.

all project agencies at provincial level, and collating this information at national level so that the

INDONESIA
PROVINCLAL IRRIIATED AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT (PIADP)
Irrigated Agxiculture Development
1.     The essential PLADP concept would be to focus specifically on completing schemes to
achieve full benefits from irrigation through full agricultural development. Within this concept, the
project would only support on-going irrigation schemes where at least the diversion structure and
main canal distribution system are complete. The project would not support new sc..emes, schemes
which require major rehabilitation or modernization, or schemes which are complete and identified as
ready for hand over to efficient operation and maintenance (EOM). The main project objectives related
to irrigation development would be to: (i) complete irrigation schemes and achieve full potential
benefits from irrigated agricultural development; and (ii) benefit the largest number of farmers with the
mninimum expenditures. This annex describes in more detail, the status of project preparation, lessons
learnt during preparation, the process and procedures to screen, select and categorize project schemes
and the criteria used in the screening process.
Status of Project Preparation
2.     The main preparation task was to review and evaluate the provincial proposals to eliminate
schemes that did not fit the project concept (para 1), or were basically flawed, and to elimina.e
unnecessary expenditure items from included schemes. Some 170 schemes in 13 provinces were
reviewed and about 48 schemes excluded at appraisal (Table 1). These schemes either: (i) did not fit
the project concept; (ii) were essentially complete, but included in the hope of continuing expenditures;
or (iii) were unacceptable because of basic technical difficulties or unjustified expenditures. Some 35
of the remaining 130 included schemes (some 10 percent of estimated project costs) were judged
ready for immediate implementation (the 'go-ahead' category). The remaining 95 schemes were
included, but deferred pending further technical and economic evaluation (para 19).
3.     A basic shortcoming through the project preparation process has been lack of reliable
information on project status, even though all schemes had been under implementation for many
years. The following paragraphs summarize why many schemes have been deferred, related to three
financing sources: (i) schemes supported under SPIDP (Loan 2375-IND) which closed in 1988; (ii)
on-going schemes under ISSP (Loan 2880-IND) expected to close in March 1991; and (iii) schemes
financed from Government or sources other than the Bank.
4.      Ex-SPIDP Schemes. Technical status repr were prepared for all schemes financed under
SPIDP, primarily because in the year before SPIDP Loan closure, project status could not be defined
with certainty. As a result of this study, physical resources and technical constraints were identified
and recommendations made on existing scheme status and what actions should be taken. The findings
are summarized in Attachment 1 to this annex. SPIDP schemes were defered when the status reports
(and action plans) concluded that basic data sources, such as soils and land use maps, have not been
adequately prepared or evaluated in relation to design commnand area or layout of the irrigation system
(major parts of which have been completed for several years). Because of inadequate planning, for
many schemes, there is no supportable match between areas physically commanded by the irrigation
system and water availability or land areas of suitable for agricultural development (land capability).
In addition, the concurrence of farmers generally has not been obtained where tree crops have been
scheduled for clearance and the land developed for rice cultivation. For all schemes, construction
generally has been started before a complete socio-economic and environmental impact evaluation has
been made. In some cases, the designated scheme command areas may encroach on reserve forest
lands.
5.      For some deferred ex-SPIDP schemes, there are action plans that provide sufficient soil and
land use data to relate physical resource availability to command areas proposed for irrigation service.
However, for these schemes, the recommendations in the action plans (prepared by the consultants)



- 99 -                            Annex 5
Page 2
generally have not been followed. For many schemes, expensive work programs have been included
in scheme proposals under the project with little justification in terms of incremental benefits. A
condition of reinstatement of deferred ex-SPIDP schemes would be to confirm and prepare revised
proposals that would implement the specific recommendations in the SPIDP status reports and action
plans. In particular, prior to review of the status of any deferred scheme, the responsible agencies
would collect additional data and undertake any additional studies required, prepare revised design
modifications and update cost estimates, and support the revised scheme proposal with a financial and
economic analysis.
6.       Ex-ISSP Schem . The present status of most schemes on-going under ISSP cannot be
defined with certainty. Consequently all ex-ISSP schemes included under the project have been put in
the 'deferred' category. In this respect, Planiiing reports were prepared as a condition of inclusion for
all schemes under ISSP. Planning reports were also prepared for some schemes for consideration
under ISSP, though not finally accepted for inclusion under ISSP. For most schemes, there is some
presentation of availablc hydrological data ard an attempt to show a water balance, but in general,
insufficient information on soils and land use. Thus, to reinstate ex-ISSP schemes, it would be
necessary to undertake the basic data mapping (soils and land use) where applicable, and to
demonstrate a relationship between areas physically commanded by the irrigation system and soils
suitable for development. Recently collected hydrologic data should be used to calculate water
availability and confirm the empirical forrnulae used in planning and design of most schemes.
7.       'Other' Schgme. No recent status or planning reports have been made available for any
existing schemes financed by Government from sources other than the Bank. Generally, the original
planning and design of proposed schemes appears to be of poor quality. However some schemes
with large sunk costs app-  to warrant completion and full development. For 'deferred' schemes not
previously financed by the bsank, where planning cr feasibility studies are said to have been
undertaken, these studies would need to be updated to permit evaluation as described above for the ex-
SPIDP and ex-ISSP schemes (paras 4 to 6). Where no such previous studies have been undertaken,
the 'deferred' schemes would need complete irrigation planning exercises, including preparation of all
maps, collection and analysis of hydrologic data, involvement of beneficiaries, preparation of work
programs, detailed estimates of costs and economic analysis before the schemes can be being
considered for upgrading to 'go-ahead' status under the project.
Lessons Learnt during Preparation
8.      General. Most candidate schemes proposed for the project are technically simple and
straightforward. Most have had a substantial consultancy input during the original planning and
design phase and the implementation phase to date. This consultancy input has varied greatly in
quality, but an important lesson learnt is that often the findings and recommendations of the
consultants are ignored to avoid any delay to construction or because they exclude expensive works
programs. The consultants' reports are used to secure financing and then often ignored. The result
has been technical shortcomings (in terms of irrigation performance) tnd delayed impact from irrigated
agricultural development on the Outer Islands. The main project lessons learnt are: (i) commitment to
schemes without collecting or evaluating physical resource data; (ii) entering the implementation phase
before proper planning and design; (iii) lack of attention to operating principles in the design phase;
(iv) construction of schemes in a piecemeal manner with uncoordinated financing; and (v) treatment of
irrigated agricultural development as a civil works activity with no coordination with other agencies
involved. The following sections describe some of these issues.
9.       Inadequate Data Collection. The present DGWRD/PRIS practice, as evidenced by findings
of the technical audit on 84 SPIDP schemes and other schemes proposed for PIADP, has been to enter
the planning and design and construction stages with inadequate collection physical resource data
(water availability and soil suitability). Hydrological and climatic data collection in all project
provinces is given low priority and is subject to unpredictable budget allocations. In the original
planning documentation for most PIADP schemes, the consultants have qualified their
recommendations by stating that the data base was inadequate, and then generally resort to using



- 100-                          Annex 5
Page 3
empirical fonnulae to design the schemes. The assumptions in the empirical formulae are not tlsen
confirmed by subsequent collection of data, eveii though this could easily be done by permanent field
staff at the scheme site. For all schemes under the project, it would simply mean routine reading of a
staff gauge installed (and calib.'ated) on the river diversion structure and inscallation and reading a rain
gauge located at the weir site, Specific examples of this problem are the floc a control and drainage
proposals for the Sadang and Maloso schemes in South Sulawesi 1/. With respect to completed
schemes, river flow data is essential to allocate water supplies and thus to undertake efficient O&M.
As a result of inadequate data base, many substantially co.mplete schemes proposed for the project
have had to be deferred, pending confirmation of reliable water resources (and suitable soils). It is
recommende that a condition for presentation of deferred projects for clearance by the Bank is that
sufficient hydrological data be collected and analyzed to provide a reliable basis for estimates of water
availability.
10.      ata Proessg. A shortcoming in all provinces is lack of reliable data on individual
scheme status. It is therefore recommended that the Geographical Information Systems (LI= which
are being established in each province, be used as the fourndation for monitoring and evaluation of
PIADP schemes. All relevant data would be assembled and collated onto the base maps, using the
computer equipment recently installed in most BAPPEDA. The tertiary block would become the basic
planning unit. Physical information (soils and land use) and economic parameters (costs and returns)
would be assembled by tertiary block, and entered in the GIS for analysis and presentation. This
inforrnation would then be used to formulate realistic action plans. For this purpose, simple and
standardized formats should be adopted to elicit inter-agency coordination. Introduction of GIS based
planning would be supported by other project activities particularly: (i) developmnent of diagnostic
analytical capabilities in national/provincial agencies; and (ii) appropriate training for selected
BAPPEDA staff.
11.    Data Transfer and Communications. The project schemes are located in remote and isolated
areas throughout the Outer Islands. In addition, there is an increasing need for the central Ministries
to improve ,;ommunication links if the necessary progress is to be made under the Government
decentralization policy. At present, communications both from schemes to provinrial headquarters
and between the provinces and central Ministries and agencies is poor and slow. Communications
depends on inefficient telephone connecnions and postage deliveries. With the introduction of
computers for much of the project processing and management activides, more attention should be
given to modern communications technology on a trail basis. Once the technology has been test-I
under field condidons and is proven to be satisfactory, it can then be introduced on a wider scalt.
12.    To address the communication issue, it is recommended that DGWRD test Very Small
Aperture Terminal (VSAT) communication technology which uses standard satellite links. The VSAT
equipment is now practical because standard satellite technology has made important strides in
operational efficiency in Indonesia in the last 2-3 years. A part of the Indonesia Regional Network
(IRN) is provided by the Palapa satellite, which also supports a number of international commercial
and private entity "communications links" such as off-shore oil and gas drilling. Depending on the
geographical location relative to the domestic satellite "footprints", the antennas are small in size and
electronic packages are micro-miniatured and power requirement have been substantially reduced.
Assuming that the IRN process is put in place, scheduled by the end of year 1990, the VSAT could be
used for simultaneous voice/data transmission without involving the local telephone network. The
project could thus provide for reliable links between DGWRD headquarters in Jakarta and scheme
1/    A detailed flood control and drainage report was prepared by consultants (Halcrows &
Partners) in 1980. This report acknowledged that there was insufficient reliable data for the
study which was completed on empirical assumptions. Since that date, no data has been
regularly collected to evaluate these assumptions, although measuring stations have been
installed under previous Bank supported operations. Thus the proposed flood control and
drainage works to the Rappang river under Sadang scheme (at an estimated base cost of about
(US$ 23.0 million) and have been excluded from the project.

sites. Thrme schemes implemented as "special projects" directly bv,' DOI-II within DGWRD would be
selected in coasultation with the Bank after further evaluation of 'v 'AT technology. The objective
would be to demonstrate effective communication links to meet the needs for isolated schemes on the
Outer Islands. The performance of the VSAT equipment would be monitored and evaluated by
DGWRD assisting by the consultants from the project consultant services.
13.     Planning and Design. The quality of planning and design for small and medium irrigation
schemes on the Outer Islands needs to be substantially improved. Few schemes are examined for
basic feasibility, primarily because of inadequate data and also to avoid delay in moving to the
construction stage. The main shortcoming is the lack of project and diagnostic analysis of design
quality because of nominal monitoring and evaluation of project performance. Thus consultants use
(and are repeatedly paid for) the same standard desigas which imply opeational principles that cannot
be successfully demonstrated and often appear not to be appropriate for small, isolated schemes on the
Outer islands. Many proposed schemes have flood control and/or drainage components which
generally are not supported by measured data and which at present cannot be evaluated in terns of
impact. In all such schemes, the proposed flood and drainage programs need to be physically and
technically justified on an adequate data base. An example of this issue is the expensive flood control
and drainage programs proposed for the Sadang and Maloso schemes in South Sulawesi. Because of
the uncertainty, these programs were eliminated from the project at appraisal. In this respect, it is
essential to calculate incremental benefits from the proposed flood control and drainage works to
provide specific economic justification for components that are expensive to implement and sustain.
14.    Scheme Comledtion. Most provincial proposals included schemes that have be-en previously
financed under either SPIDP (Loan 2375-ND) or the on-going ISSP (Loan 2880-IND). In many
cases, the estimated costs to completion of these schemes under thz project has been substantially
greater than the proposals (generally prepared by consultants) put forward when the schemes were
approved for Bank financing. Major expenditure items (canal lining) have been added, often without
technical justification, in anticipation of additional financing, whilst essential works are deferred (such
as completion of tertiaries). As a consequence, most schemes are lett incomplete for an extended
period of time aid deteriorate before the scheme (or in some cases, even part of a scheme) is
functiona'. Priority is given to expenditure (major work programs) and not essential works necessary
to achieve potential benefits as so .a as possible. The implementing agencies (DGWRD/PRIS) appear
to take completion of a scheme to be a moving target which is then allowed to slip if there is any
chance of further financing of civil works. A condition of project implementation would be that: (i)
all works required to fully complete and develop each scheme under the project would be defined and
agreed whcn the scheme is approved for implementation by the Bank; and (ii) before any additional
development to a scheme or any expansion to the scheme command area is undertaken, the scheme
would be completed as defined under project (para 5.15).
15.     Scheme Opeiron. Little attention has been given to operation of schemes. On the
information available to the Bank, there is no scheme on the Outer Islands that is at present effectively
functioning as designed. There is virtually no monitoring and evaluation of irrigation performance
(including Bank financed projects). During the preparation of the project, PRIS officials in the proj,ct
provinces (or DGWRD staff) were not able to explain how small and medium schemes are, or should
be, effectively operated The shortcoming can be partly explained in that few schemes are ever fully
completed and made , function as designed before they start to deteriorate. The problem is not just
lack of reliable or predictable funding, but what often appears to be basic flaws in the scheme planning
and design (para 13). This situation presents a technical risk to the project, but resolving the problem
(i.e. making schemes functional) is a basic project objective. In this respect, the Government and the
Bank are addressing the O&M related issues, including institutionalizing processes on a subsector
basis to ensure that: (i) plans for O&M are in place, (ii) operational procedures are exemplified; and
(iii) scheme, are verified and certified that they operate as designed. The project activides for PROM
would include preparing the plans of operations (and maintenance), developing operating procedures
as appropriate to local or scheme conditions, and reflecdng these procedures in the system designs.



- 102 -                         Annex S
16.     Clearly, until schemes are fully completed, efficient scheme operation is impossiele and the
full potential benefits from EOM cannot be achieved. At present, under O&M programs on the Outer
Islands, the main focus is on maintenance since this resulted in civil work programs. The preferred
use of O&M funding and resources appear to be: (i) sustaining the establishment; and (ii) maintenance
contracts - often canal lining - whicl. is undertaken on the unproven belief that lining mduces the need
for maintenance. The projoct specifically addresses these shortcomings.
17.   Verification and Certification of Scheme ComPl   An important step in inigation
development is the transfer of a scheme from the implemnentadon to the routine O&M stage. At
present, the transfer process and procedure is unsatisfactory and often incomplete schemes are
prematurely handed over for O&M. To address this problem, when a scheme is consideied complete,
there would be a formal test to verify the satisfactory funcdoning of the completed irrigation system
and confm that the command area is fully developed. 'te test would be undeaken by experienced
staff fror. DGWRD/MOA together with representatives from BAPPEDA, PRIS, PRAS, other
prvincial agencies involved in scheme implementadon, and the farmers to be smrved through the
WUAs. The project consultants would assist in this process as required. The verification process
would consist of a technical audit to confurm complktion of all civiT works and land clearance and
development as agreed when the scheme was approved for implementation rnd also completion of the
preparation for O&M (PROM) activities. The scheme would then be testeed to confirm that it can
function as designed through the full opcrational range of discharges and deliver the design irrigation
service to match the agricultural objectlve. The operating principles and plan would thus be
confirmed as efficient and pracdcal. If the scheme cannot function as designed, remedial works
would be undertaken and the scheme re-tested after such works are completed. Once the various
representatives involved in the verification process agree that a schemne is ready to transfer from the
construction phase to routine O&M, a Certificate of Corletion would be issued and schemes
formally handed ever for O&M. Under the proposed project, the Govemment would cause
WGWRD/PRIS to certify and verify that such schemes are complete and can function as designed
before handing over prolject schemes to O&M authorities (para 5.16).
18.    Monitoring and Evaluation. Monitoring and evaluation (M&E,) has been poor on all
previous Bank supported projects and it is often not possible to define with certainty the physical
status of schemes during implementation or those designated as complete. Each project agencies
should systematically monitor and evaluate physical and financial progress of their own programs.
Implementation performance on individual components should be collated at provincial and central
level through the respective coordination working groups. To confirm that investments in irrigation
works are justified, socio-economic impacts, crop incomes and margins, crop production and support
services and water management practices must be routirnely monitored by DGWRD, based on methods
and criteria previously agreed with the Bank. In particular, water management practices must be
monitored and evaluated to confirm design criteria such as water use efficiencies and also to confirm
that field staff understand (and are foilowing) rou ine O&M procedures, such as opcra"or. Of gates,
water measurement and maintenance of canal systems. There is also an urgent need to evaluate the
technical performance of irrigadion schemes, such as the percentage of the water demand that was met,
fluctuations in water use efficiencies and farmers' contributions (in cav3h or labor) to tertiary systems
management. Under the project, all project agencies should monitor and evaluate their project
activities and performance according to standards and detail satisfactory to the Bank. Without
effective M&E, diagnostic analysis of project performance is not possible.
Scheme Selection and Screening Process and Criteria
19.    Under project preparation, a screening and selection process has been used to id.entify
schemes, or parts of schemes, that would provide the best returns on investment und also to evaluate
the potential risks. The process consists of a sequential procedure to ensure that each step of the
selection and scr,.ening process is undertaken in a logical order. Standard criteria have been set for
each determinant to evaluate the technical and economical viability of a candidate scheme. The
determinants, essential to establish the technical viability of any irrigation scheme include, inter alia,
location and designated command area, soils and water availability, topography, land use, present and



- 103 -
Page 6
future crop pattems, land ownership and settlement. Also taken into consideration is the present
status of the scheme in terms of quality of original planning and design, sunk investment costs and
any other socio-economic factors that would affect the project risk. Thus a screening and selection
process has been used to decide what schemes proposed by the provinces should be included under
PLADP and then to decide the category status of the included schemes. 'GQahead' schemes are those
that have sufficient planning and enough data to confirmn (with acceptable risk) that they would
produce worthwhile retums on further proposed investments. 'Defenred schemes have sufficient
potential to be included, but insufficient data or inadequate planning to mnake imrediate
implementation an acceptable risk. Table 2 summarizes .he status and main determinants used in the
categorization process.
20.    The deferred schemes have been further subdivided into sub-categories [I A, II B and II C
(see main Table 2). The 43 schemes placed in the Category II B would need special attention since, as
presented, they have serious problems that related to their physical, technical and economic viability.
For example, some 25 schemes under sub-category II B, as proposed at appraisal, have ERRs of less
than 12 percent These schemes would need special review to explain this lack of economic viability
since all of the schemes are supposed to be substantially complete. Five deferred schemes (Sitiung II
in West Sumatra, Kaleana Kanan, Kaleana Kiri and Sanrego in South Sulawesi and Gumbasa in
Central Sulawesi), have been designated sub-category II C. They have been placed in category II C
because they include unrealistic expansion areas, unjustified costs and/or insufficient information to
justify the proposed work programs. AU have all been previously financed by the Bank and are
included under the project because they could be reformulated (to reinstate the design proposed when
the schemes were approved by the Bank) and are likely to be viable. To cover the financing of these
schemes (if they can be reinstated) and any additional schemes that may be proposed by Govemment
that satisfy the project objectives, some 10 percent of total base costs of all schemes have been added
to total project costs. It is esdimated that an additional 45,000 ha would be developed under additional
and sub-category II C schemes.
21.    Scheme Selecticn and Screening Procedures. The project schemes would be screened,
selected and commissioned in accoriance to a process and procedures satisfactory to the Bank
(para 3.21). Appendix I summarizes the process and procedures that would be followed through the
implementation and commissioning phases for a selected scheme. The sequential steps from pre-
screening through implementation to scheme completion are shown schematically in Figure 1 of the
appendix and are as follows:
Sheet 1    Screening - to confirm fit with the project concept and objectives.
Sheet 2    Scheme Analysis - test physical and technical viability.
Sheet 3    Involvement of Beneficiaries and Approval by Provincial Government.
Sheet 4    Review by Central Working Group and Approval by BAPPENAS.
Sheet 5    Implementadon.
Sheet 6    Verification and Certificadon of Scheme Completion.
These schematic flow charts define in a simple manner the complex technical planning and
coordination required to effectively and efficiently process an irrigation scheme from initial selection
through to certification of completion. An essential post construction activity would be to monitor and
evaluate the performance of a scheme to confirn that it continues to function as designed and to record
any lessons learnt.
22.    Planning and Design Criteria. The Bank would review the reinstatement of deferred
schemes to confirm that a scheme is physically, technically and economically viable. In this respect,
the objective would be to implement the project schemes in accordance to technical, financial,
administrative, agricultural and environmental practices, standards and criteria satisfactory to the
Bank, and to an implementation schedule to be agreed annually with the Bank. Appendix 2
summarizes the criteria and conditions that would be followed through the screening and subsequent
implementation and commissioning phases for a selected scheme.
