INDONESIA
PROVINCLkL IRRIGATED AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Procedures for Scheme Selection. Screening and Implementatioi
1.    This attachment summarizes the sequential steps in the process used to screen and select
schemes for inclusion in the project at appraisal and would be followed through the project
implementation. The screening process would be used for evaluating deferred and additional
schemes before they are submitted to the Bank for approval prior to implementation. The steps
related to implementation and the commissioning phases would apply to all schemes included under
the project. The sequential steps through a selected scheme cycle are shown in the six sheets under
the Schematic Flow Chart (attached as Figure 1).
The Screening and Selection Process - (Sheet 1)
2.    The screening and selection process is to determine whether a candidate scheme would
satisfy the pject objective, namely of fully completing and developing the command area of
irrigation schemes to obtain full potential benefits from irrigated agriculture. The proposed project
would be limited to completing on-going small and medium scale irrigation schemes, where at least
the weir and the main canal distribution system are substantially complete. Thus, new schemes or
schemes that require replacement and/or major reconstruction, rehabilitation or modernization would
be excluded.
Scheme Evaluation and Analysis - (Sheet 2)
3.    Status of Preparation Check. The schemes which satisfy the screening and selection process
would be evaluated to establish whether they are ready and sufficiently prepared for agreed activities
to proceed to implementation. The conditions and criteria to be used in scheme preparation are
summarized in Attachment 3 to this Annex. The initial process would be to check that sufficient
physical and other resource data is available (such as mapping and hydrologic data collection, etc.)
both in terms of quantity and quality. The analysis would then proceed by sequentially to assess the
physical, technical and economic viability (Sheet 2). The first steps would be to confirm that a
scheme is physically feasible by checking topography, environmental status, water availability and
coher physical resources (such as soil suitability and the general topography) essential for a scheme
to deliver potential agricultural benefits at acceptable costs. Schemes which have passed through
these steps would then be subjected to technical analysis to confirm that all needed activities have
been fully specified. At this stage, proposed works which cannot be proved to contribute to potential
benefits are excluded (in particular, expensive items such as canal lining are evaluated to confirm that
they are justified). For schemes with flood control and/or major drainage problems, the flood
control and drainage components would be evaluated separately, where applicable, following the
same sequential steps as for general scheme analysis.
4.    Deferred Schemes. Schemes included under the proposed project that cannot be processed
for lack of data would remain deferred and specific reasons for the deferment would be identified.
Schemes that remain deferred would be provisionally phased into the provincial work program with
tentative cost estimates and phasing. These deferred schemes would be revisited and reviewed by
each Bank supervision mission, and the work programs and costs adjusted at each Bank-GOI
Annual Implementation Review. The national and provincial coordination working groups would
then incorporate any reinstated schemes into the respective provincial programs, or when applicable,
delete the schemes from the project.. Adherence to the agreed annual work programs would be the



- 110 -                         Annex S
Attachrnent 2
responsibility of the provincial government, with the program coordinated by the BAPPEDA in each
province.
5.    Costing. Phasing and Economic Analysis. The last stage of the Scheme Analysis would be
to estimate costs and check economic viability. Quantity and costs of all components would be
calculated ising current unit rates (for similar work contracts) and potendal benefits would be
estimated from the best local crop producdon data available. Sunk and social costs would be
excluded and a rate of return of at least 12.0 percent would be achieved, since all schemes are
supposed to be "substantially complete". If a satisfactory ERR is not achieved, the scheme would be
excluded, or deferred until the proposed works, costs and potential benefits can be further examined
to establish the reason for the low ERR.
Involvement of the Beneficiaries & Provincial Approval - {Shet 3)
6.    When scheme has passed the process described above and thus can be realistically defined by
the responsible agencies, the social viability would be tested by confirming that the potential
beneficiaries want the scheme. If the affected community does not want all or part of a scheme, it
would be either excluded from the project, or the scheme command area would be adjusted to serve
only areas with community approval. Reformulated schemes would then be re-evaluated (as
required) through the scheme evaluation and analysis shown on Sheet 2.
Review by Proiect National Coordination Working Group - (Sheet 4)
7 .    The next step would be to prepare (or periodically update) a work program for each province
specifying the agreed activities and proposed phasing of schemes cleared for implementation and
those still deferred. A coordinated provincial work program with estimated annual costs would be
prepared for submission and budget approval to the center. A final review of the provincial
programs (by components) would then be undertaken by the agencies involved at the national level to
check for inter-provincial consistency. The provincial programs and individual schemes (including
cost estimates) would be reviewed and formally approved at the national level and then subject to
budgetary approval by BAPPENAS.
Implementation and Commissioning. - (Sheets S and-6
8.    The various steps in the implementation and commissioning process need to be carefully
coordinated to ensure that any changes or delays are reflected in the revision to the work programs of
all agencies. An essential last step in scheme implementation would be to verify and certify that a
scheme is fully complete and function as planned and designed, before it is handed over for routine
O&M. The verification and certification process is summarized in para 17 of Annex 5 and detailed in
a Discussion Paper produced by the project preparation consultants1/.
Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E)
9.    The M&E procedures would be designed so that key steps in the process described above can
be clearly identifiable and monitored. Each scheme would have to be processed and cleared through
all phases, and in particular, the f nal verification and certification of completion, before the scheme
is designated as complete under the project.
1J    Discussion Paper on Verification and Certification Procedures to Confirm Completion
Status, DWRGD (prepared by Sir M. MacDonald and Partners Asia and PT Indah Karya,
Consultants), September 1989.

INDONESIA
PROVINCIA', IRRIGATED AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Criteria for Scheme Selection and Screening
1.     Of the 130 schemes included under the project, some 99 schemes have been supported
under either the SPIDP (Loan 2375 - IND) or under the ISSP (Loan 2880 - IND). Both these Bank
operations have explicit planning and design criteria, satisfactory to the Bank and agreed by
Government, which were used to screen and select the schemes for inclusion under the respective
Loan operations. A general criteria under the proposed project for schemes financed under previous
Bank operations would be for all works, to take into account the planning and design standards and
criteria agreed when these schemes were originally approved for financing by the Bank and, in
particular, the recommendations related to technical and economic viability in the project preparation
documents, in status reports and action plans prepared for schemes financed under the SPIIP I/ and
ISSP 2/. These planning and design standards and criteria would also be applied to those schemes
included under the project that have not been previously financed under either SPIDP or ISSP.
2.     Except for SID and land titling and certification, prior to submitting a scheme to the Bank
for approval for implementation under the project, Government would confirm that the agencies
involved have approved the design, scope, estimated costs and coordinated implemnentation program
of the porposed scheme.
3 .    Without limitation to the provisions in para I and 2 above and unless agreed by the Bank,
the following conditions and criteria, inter alia, would be met before a deferred scheme included
under the project (or an additional scheme that Government may propose for h iclusion under the
project), other than 'go-ahead' schemes, is submitted to Bank the approval for i;. plernentation under
the project.
A Project Scope and Irrigation Works.
(a)   Sufficient physical resource data, including hydrology, clinmate, ;opography, soils
and land capability and use would b-, collected and evaluated to, ..onfirm that the
proposed scheme is technically viab.e and would be able to ach' ~. the predicted
agricultural impact in terms of croppirg area and crop intensitie. z .d yields;
(b)   presentation of hydrologic and climatic records sufficient to evaluut. any empirical
assumption used in the scheme planning design and confirmation 4na this data is
being collected and evlauated for the scheme on a continuous basis,;
(c)   that the proposed scheme has sufficient water resources to provide, on an 80 per
cent dependable basis, the annual design irrigation requirements for ihe designated
command area;
Status Reports and Action Plans for the Second Provinc;al Irrigation Development Project
(SPIDP), GOI, DGWRD (assisted by Sir M. MacDonald & Partners in association with PT
Indah Karya, Consulting Engineers), March 1989 (M.ain Volume and 24 Annex Reports).
2/    Review Reports, ISSP Preparatory Designs, and Review of 22 On-going Projects, GOI,

INDONESIA
PROVINCIAL IRRIGATED AGRICULTURE DE-VELOPMENT PROJECr
Land Clearance and Development
Background
1.    General. The Directorate of Agricultural Area Development (DAAD) in the Directorate
General of Food Crop Agriculture (DGFCA) in the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) is charged with
supporting smallholders in land clearing and land development (LC&D) for the cultivation of food
crops. This support comprises technical advice and some financial assistance towards land clearing
and land levelling. DAAD is solely a LC&D contract administering agency and it is providing
training and extension to smallholders regarding land clearing, land development and sawah
formation. All land development is done by contract.
2.    Each province retains its own contractors to do the work (KEPPRES 29 and 30, 1984).
Contractors are prequalified in classes, as shown below:
c1as                                                Contract Pric (Rp M)
C3           Call bids from 3 contractors             0 - 20
C2           Call bids from 3 contractors             20- 50
Ci           Limited tender-minimum 3 contractors    50 - 100
B2           Limited tender-minimum 3 contractors    100 - 200
B 1          Limited tender-minimum 3 contractors    200 - 500
A           General tender: mainly Java               > 500
3.    LC&D used to be based on a credit scheme. However, to become eligible for credit from
Bank Rakyat Indonesia (BRI), smallholders had to have title to their land. Since only a very small
proportion of farmers possess titles to the land, DAAD's development program was seriously
delayed. In September 1989, GOI decided to provide financial support to smallholders on a grant
basis to accelerate land development. Funding is provided through the Government (APBN)
budget and a large program from supported OECF funds. Under the proposed PIADP, adequate
funding would be provided in participating irrigation schemes to finalize land clearing and LC&D
operations in the command area of PIADP schemes.
4.    There has been insufficient progress under Government funding LC&D programs to fully
assess the impact. However, most on-going LC&D programs are reported to be underfunded. As
a result, the land is not fully cleared or developed, placing a future burden on the farmers An
Integrated Command Area Development Project (ICADP), funded by ADB, is under
implementation in Lampung and Bengkulu provinces. One objective is to ascertain technical
parameters to adequate land development at reasonable cost. The lessons learned are:
(i) land clearing and land level of the ecologically fragile red-yellow prodzolics should
preferably be done manually;
(ii) contractors should employ participating smallholders/beneficiaries; and
(iii) training of and extension smallholders is essential with respect to adequate development
methods, the role and importance of the topsoil, the choice of cropping pattems etc.
5.    DAAD Staffing. The DAAD is organized in four sections of which land development is
one. DAAD is represented at the provincial and the district levels. DAAD staffing in the six project
provinces is as follows:



- 120 -                                   Page 2
Number of
Provine       Technical         Admin.    Technical      Admin  DAAD Units
Riau             13                2           0            0          0
W. Sumatra        6 (land dev.)    2          18            2          2
Aceh              4                3          32            16         8
Lampung           3                1          90           20          5
S. E. Sulawesi    6                ?          32            8          2
S. Sulawesi      18                1          33            2          2
6.    Provincial DAAD staff are not geared in number and in quality to adequately administer the
immense LC&D programs in the provinces. DAAD relies on consultants (survey, investigation,
design, and supervision) and contractors to implement the program. In some provinces with
modest land development programs, DAAD considers it would be able to deliver good quality
development. In others provinces, DAAD realizes that it will not be able to cope with present
staffing resources.
Description of LC&D Works
7.    The LC&D activity consists of: (i) survey, investigation and design (SID) to prepare
contracts; (ii) clearing the land (cutding trees and destumping); and (iii) developing the cleared land
into sawah (fields suitable for rice cultivation). The technical details are described below and the
basis for establishing LC&D costs given in Section C of this annex.
8.    Land Clearing. The land clearance operadons comprise the following activities. First an
inventory of existing vegetation is made since the costs for clearing are mainly determined by the
size and density of the vegetation. Land clearance is classified as follows:
(i)  Heavy forest (HF)         -  over 600 trees per ha with a diameter of
over 30 cm at breast height;
(ii)  Light forest (LF)        -  thick cover of small trees and bushes;
(iii) Secondary bush (SB)      -  thin cover of small trees and bushes; and
(iv) Grass (GA)                -  few trees and bushes.
Land clearance of heavy forest includes: (i) selection and marking of valuable trees for timber, (ii)
underbrushing; (iii) cutting and felling; (iv) stacking and windrowing; (v) burning; (vi) destumping
and reburning; and (vii) construction of ditches and planting of cover crops to prevent erosion.After
land clearing is completed, more accurate topographic maps can be prepared which serve as the base
for tertiary layout and land development designs.
9.    Land develpment. The land development (LD) operations include the following activities.
Land is levelled to produce a flat area suitable for bunded irrigation, preferably in plots of about
0.25 ha. Land levelling requirements are dictated by slopes:
Slopes              Developmeat
(i)      0 - 1.5%      -     little or none needed;
(ii)      1.5 - 3%     -     levelling needed; and
over 3%      -     substantial levelling needed.
During the LD operation, final destumping is undertaken, if needed. Following completion of LD
work in-field irrigation development is completed (tertiary irrigation/drainage canals; construction of
bunds/dikes along the contour, bund preparation for sawah formation).



- 121 -                                  Annex6
page 3
10.   To minimize the quantity of earthworks to be moved, (and thus costs) during land levelling
the following steps are taken:
Stage I   Divide the future terrace in three parts; scrape topsoil from the upper and parts to
the middle of the proposed terrace.
Stage  2   Move the cut of the top part of the terrace to the bottom part (fill) of the above
lying terrace.
Swagm 3   Construct dike or bund.
Stage 4   Replace topsoil back over disturbed subsoil.
The process is illustrated in Figure 1. The quantity of soil to be moved by slope class is given in
Table 1. Under APBN and OECF funded programs, land appears to be levelled by scraping soil
(topsoil and subsoil) from the top of the terrace to the bottom part of the same terrace. This can
result in topsoil being covered by subsoil and serious long term reduction in land fertility.
11.   Contracting. DAAD contracts out LC&D operations. So far, DAAD has combined both
land clearance and development operations in one contract, often with disastrous results since
adequate topographic mapping is impossible for heavily vegetated areas. Contractors tend to
concentrate on land clearing and underestimate iarid development requirements. Contracts are often
left incomplete or abandoned when the land clearance operation is finished. Under many contracts,
destumping is not adequately completed. DAAD is aware of the need of separating land clearance
from land development, but fears that contractors would not be interested in bidding for these
separate operations. Most would concentrate on land clearing only.
12.   Backgroun  Only scant data are available on LC&D costs in most provinces, mainly
because Government funded programs have only recently started. The unit costs for LC&D
activities used to estimate the project costs have been based on data collected by DAAD from recent
contract bids, in particular, unit costs in Bengkulu and South and Southeast Sulewasi, detailed
below.
13.   Bengkulu. Land development contract for land clearing and levelling are all undertaken
manually with no destumping. Only terrace bunds are provided ,. ith no compaction. The cover is
reported as: 72%, bush/grass; 14% light forest and 14% heavy forest. Land slopes are in the range
5-10%.

has been funded by about Rp 1.90 billion averaging about Rp 320,000/ha. DAAD expects that an
unsatisfactory job will be done and it estimates that at least double the amount would be required to
achieve satisfactory results.

19.   Project Cost Estimates. Cost estimates for the "Go Ahead" and the "Deferred" schemes are
given in Tables 2 and 3, respectively. Existing tree crop stands in command areas have been
excluded, pending economic justification and agreement format by participating smallholders. All
areas proposed to be cleared falling in a slope class over 8% have been excluded since technical
parameters to guarantee satisfactory LC&D are not known, and since costs involved are expected to
be prohibitive, the activity would not be economically viable.
Conclusions
20.   DAAD at the provincial and district levels does not have the required manpower in number
and in technical quality, and funding to deliver adequate land development, over the short to
medium term. DAAD only administers contracts of consultants and land clearing/land levelling
contractors. Consequently, field supervisio.a is minimal and unsatisfactory performance is a
substantial risk.
21.   Underfunding of LC&D operations as under the OECF program may result in unsatisfactory
land development, resulting in lower than expected yields over the first four years. DAAD is letting
contracts for land clearing and land development combined. However, these two activities should
be separated in two discrete contracts. Land clearing should be followed by topographic mapping
as a basis for planning tertiary design and land development, which includes amongst others land
levelling.
22.   It is recommended that to minimize the risk of unsatisfactory LC&D, the final contract
payment (say 20-25% of the contract price) should not be transferred to the contractor until after
participating smallholders have agreed that the works have been completed to a satisfactory (to be
formally confirmed during the verification and certification procedures of individual schemes,
described in Annex 5.

INDONERSIA
PROVINCIAL IRRIGATED AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Land Titling and Certification
1.    General. The agency, previously known as Agraria in the Ministry of Home Affairs, was
reorganized recently into the National Land Agency or Badan Pertanahan Nasional (BPN) reporting
directly to the President of the Republic of Indonesia. This reorganization was needed due to
Agraria's unsatisfactory performance in its land titling/certification operations in the rural sector, for
instance in the Bank financed transmigration program and the tree crop subsector (NES, SRDP, and
SCDP).
2.    BPN - is a revenue generating agency and is supposed to support itself from fees charged to
its clients (as was Agraria). Typical BPN activities include land registration, cadastral survey, land
titling and the issuing of land certificates. Land consolidation also forms part of BPN's mandate.
Until recently, farmers and smallholders wishing to expand or intensify their farning operations
required credit for land clearing and land development. To be eligible for credit from the rural
banking system, (mainly Bank Rakyat Indonesia), farmers needed title to their land.
3.    In addition, GOI considers that by issuing individual land titles, farmers would obtain
security and farmers would be more inclined and wi;lling to follow advice of the extension service
and to apply appropriate husbandry practices. Individual land titles will be issued to local farmers
and transmigrants, except in those provinces where land is held in clan ownership; the clan will be
issued a title.
4.    BPN Program. Under the proposed PIADP, BPN, for the first time would become an actiye
participant in a program of the irrigated agricultural development. This should enable the more rapid
development of the small and medium scale irrigation schemes under the proposed project and thus
enable benefits to participating farmers from irrigated agriculture to be achieved in a more timely
manner. The BPN would undertake the survey and field work as soon as the land is sufficiently
cleared to enable the the process to be undertaken effectively. The land certificates would generally
be issued once the cleared land has been developed into irrigated sawah. Thus of the project
participating agencies, BPN would generally be the last in line to complete its duties. Since BPN
would obtain specific support under the project, it would no longer depend on funding by or through
other participating agencies such as DGWRD and DAAD.
5.    BPN Staffing. BPN is a well-established agency and that it is adequately represented -
technically as well as administratively - at the provincial and district levels. Land titling and the
issuance of land certificates is a function of BPN at the district level. However, BPN would provide
assistance at the provincial level, if so needed.
6.    Staffing levels at provincial and district levels in 5 sample provinces are summarized below:

BPN budgets for the complete land titling/certification process about 0.5 ha per staff member per
day. Thus, a scheme commanding 500 ha would be processed by about 10 staff and take some three
months to complete the certification process.
7.    Co. The provincial preparation of the proposed project showed a confusing array of BPN
activities in the 13 provinces, as well as widely varying unit costs for these activities between
provinces (as well as between schemes in a province). For example, land titling and certificaticn
costs varied from about some Rp 60,000 per ha in Sumatra to Rp 300,000 per ha in Kalimantan.
8.    BPN Cost Estimates for Land Certification. BPN would only perform its functions after
sawah formation has been completed, and then undertake only relatively simple titling and
certification activides. These tasks would be similar to all schemes under PIADP, and agreement
has been reached with BPN on activities to be performed and the unit cost for these activities to be
applied uniformly to all project schemes/provinces. Agreed land titling/certification unit costs, are
presented below:

IND)ONESIA
PROVINCIAL iRRIGATED AGRICULTURE DEVELOPMENT PROJECT (PIADP)
Agricultural Aspects
1.    Background. Irrigated crop production on the proposed schemes is almost exclusively for
rice. Palawija (annual food crops other than rice) crops would only be grown when there is
insufficient water for the dry season rice crop and where soil conditions necessitate growing
rotational crops for soil reclamation/amelioration purposes. Otherwise there would be both wet
and dry season rice crops. Climatic conditions in all thirteen provinces are suitable for double
cropping rice and/or palawija crops when timely and appropriate cultural practices are followed.
There is a distinct need for careful water management to allow timely land preparation for seedbeds
and main production fields, as well as timely drainage for harvesting evenly maturing (for
improved grain quality) crops. Drying of paddy is required after harvest (when grain moisture
reaches 18-22 percent ) to afford safe storage at a grain moisture content below 14 percent.
2.    There are substantial differences in soil, climate, farming population, water availability,
agricultural support services (extension, credit, input supplies, post harvest facilities) between
schemes within the 13 project provinces and it is impossible to generalize on crop production
practices and methodologies. It is therefore proposed that a fairly detailed Agricultural
Development Plan (ADP) is prepared for each of the schemes to be financed under the project.
ADP's would be prepared under the overall supervision of the Project Provincial Coordination
Working Groups (PPCWG ) in each of the 13 BAPPEDA's and would be used for seasonal
monitoring of progress with scheme implementation. Actual data collection, analyses of
constraints and proposed solutions, required support services, credit, inputs, farmer organization
strengthening, etc., would be done by the Provincial Agricultural Service (PRAS) with substantial
input from its programming division, Subject Matter Specialists (PPS) and heads of the relevant
REC(s) in coordination with KUD and/or other credit and input (fertilizers, agro-chemical)
suppliers. Some survey work is required to establish a reliable profile of the farming community.
On many of the existing schemes, where either irrigation improvements or further land clearing and
leveling is to be done, there exists no data on the farm size distribution and the crop production
arrangements (owner-operators, tenants, share cropping, etc). On schemes which are or have been
developed for transmigrants the situation is simple; most have one hectare of land for irrigated rice.
On older transmigration schemes there are already complications due to land acquisitions,
sharecropping arrangements, etc. It is extremely important to establish if share croppers and
tenants (thus without land title) can obtain crop production credit at reasonable interest rates. If this
is not the case, or when there are shortfalls in inputs such as fertilizers and pesticides, potential
crop yields cannot be achieved and which thus restricts the production and income potential of
producers. A sample ADP is provided as Attachment 1 and should be prepared before incremental
scheme development commences (Condition of Approval for Scheme Financing). ADP's would
be seasonally updated and from a careful analysis of production results, revised production targets
and alternative crop production efforts can be proposed and produced.
3.    To achieve a fairly uniform preparation of ADP's and to ensure that appropriate measures
are being taken for the delivery of services and inputs an experienced (with rice growing in
particular) Agriculturalist/irrigation agronomist consultant would be appointed (Attachment 1,
Annex 4), for the duration of the project. The selected candidate would travel extensively to
irrigation schemes in the 13 project provinces. He would guide, assist and train (in the course of
his routine duties) on the land and agricultural development activities and work mainly through the
Directorate General for Food Crops Agriculture (DGFCA) and the respective PRAS. He would,
inter alia, assist with:
(i)   approving the liaison between PRIS and PRAS so that timing of completion of
irrigation facilities, land clearing and sawah development can be synchronized;



- 129 -                            Annex 8
,Page 2
(ii)   the timely preparation of Agricultural Development Plans (Table I) for each scheme
to be implemented;
(iii)   arranging for the preparation and timely execution of a program of research and
verification trials (mostly variety and fertilizer interaction) on schemes where soil
fertility problems are expected (such as acid podsols, sandy loams, peat or muck,
and severely disturbed soils) by research institutions, and provincial subject matter
specialists;
(iv)   ensuring that arrangements are made for an adequate and timely provision of crop
p,oduction credit and inputs such as improved seed, fertilizers, pesticides,
rodenticides, etc.;
(v)    ensuring the provision of adequate PPL's for scheme farmers, so that fanner
groups, in particular indigenous land owners, will be visited on a regular
fortnightly basis and that appropriate demonstration plots are established in a timely
manner each season;
(vi)   arranging the collection of yield estimates for each crop season (by both crop
cutting and farmner interviews);
(vii)  ensuring that Integrated Pest Management (IPM) technologies can be followed and
that appropriate pest threshold levels and control measures are established;
(viii)  prepare crop production guidelines for rice and the most popular rotational palawija
crops which can se-rve as a handy pocket size techno-guide for PPL's and contact
farmers if deemed necessary following review of available documentation for this
purpose; and
(ix)   prepare seasonal reports on the above acdvities and on overall scheme achievements
with distinct recommendations for improvements where needed.
4.    Soils and ToDographv. Most scheme soils are suitable for rice production when individual
fields can be shaped and leveled to retain fairly even water depth. There are, however, large
variadons in soil fertility and several schemes have distinct problem soils. Peat soils, except the
very shallow peat/muck soils, should be avoided due to soil shrinkage, drainage and
fertility/toxicity problems. Acid podsolic soils require a long period of amelioration and are
generally not desirable for rice development due to farmer disappointment in yields obtained from
using high levels of labor and inputs during initial years of crop producdon. Under dry-land
conditions, plants in these acid podsolics show symptoms of Al-toxicity, while under wet sawah
conditions rice plants present typical Fe-toxicity symptoms such as stunted growth, poor tillering
and severe infestation with Pericularia (blast) and Helminthosrorium diseases. To develop these
soils for irrigated rice a 3-5 year reclamation/ amelioration period is required. This consists of crop
rotation, incorporation of lime and organic matter in the top soil, and a careful water management
program. Wet season rice crops should be followed by dry-season legumes, such as cowpeas
(apparently best), mungbeans and groundnuts. To leach toxic iron ions out of the soil, intermittent
drainage (1 week) and irrigation (2 weeks) is recommended for at least the first two years after
sawah formation. To be effxtive, all surface water must be removed which thus requires
additional investments in tertiary drainage. Even drainage of puddles in low lying field areas is
necessary to obtain a plant stand. This intermittent drainage - irrigation regime is claimed to
increase yields by 40 percent, albeit from a low yield level. Lime should be applied at rate of 1-2
ton/ha, while potash leveis should be doubled (e.g., from 75 to 150 Kg/ha KCI if package B
fertilizer is used). Care should be taken with lime applicadon since a pH above 7 can be counter
productive. Doubling the potash rate in a fertilizer package may be useful, but the N-rate of 150
Kg/ha urea is excessive during the soil reclamation phase and this issue should be further



- 130 -                            Annex 8
Page 3
investigated and resolved. Peat soils show the same iron-toxicity symptoms on rice and similar
management recommendations - interTitted drainage of rice in the wet season, followed by a dry-
season legume crop apply.
5.    The introducdon of organic matter into the podsolic soil is greatly beneficial and all
available rice straw and stubble, as well as other crop residues, manure and compost, should be
used. Direct-seeding of rice is not recommended during early soil recl-madon years due to the
likely occurrence of severe seedling-toxicity. Seedbeds should thus be established on the least
toxic soil of the farm and careful water management is required with a possible fungicidal treatment
for seedling blight. The low-land rice varieties Batang Ombil and Kapuas have shown
considerable tolerance to iron toxicity and are therefore recommended for these soils. Reasonable
yield assumptions are: year 1 - from 0.2-0.5 ton/ha; year 2 - from 0.8-1.2 ton/ha; year 3 - from
2.0-2.5 ton/ha; and in year 4 - from 3.5-4.0 ton/ha with a possible increase to 4.5 to 5.0 ton/ha
later on. In the first 2-3 years only one rice crop and one legume crop are to be used. Using
results from the work of the Agricultural Research Institutes, demonstration plots should be
established as soon as possible in irrigation schemes where these soils occur. The soil and water
management regime must be clearly demonstrated to the farming community over a sustained
period of time. In general, acid podsolic soils, which are also frequently on undulating terrain,
should not be considered ior irrigation development and they should by preference be used for tree
crops.
6.    Most lowland alluvial soils and many of the hilly land soils of volcanic origin are highly
suitable for rice when infiltration rates are not excessive. Only normal nutritional amendments are
required to achieve satisfactory yields. Extreme care must be taken with land leveling and terracing
to ensure that the ferdle topsoil remains in place or is replaced after removal. Prevention of erosion
on hilly lands is essential by terracing, contour bund construction or by establishing vegetadve
contour bands (with Vetiveria zizanioides) in waste areas along and between rice fields. When the
introduction of direct seeding in considered, much emphasis must be given to land leveling and in-
field and tertiary drainage. Complete surface drainage must be achieved within 24-48 hours and
adequate water must be available for providing a quick irrigat.on during a dry period after direct
seeding into water or on mud. If sown in water, it must be completely drained off the field within
48 hours.
Farming Community
7.    Many of the irrigadon schemes in the more remote provincial districts have an indigenous
population, supplemented by transmigrants, while other schemes are only settled by locals or
transmigrants. Since Balinese, Javanese and Sundanese are mostly familiar with irrigated rice
production it is generally easier to achieve sadsfactory levels of production provided the soils are
suitable and adequate water is made available. Many indigenous farmers have a low standard of
education, a communication problem (local d: lects) with most extension workers and are difficult
to motivate for adoption of modern rice growi-j technologies. Additionally, many locals have
large farms which they are unable to cultivate fully and they are often reluctant to lease land or
arrange sharecropping in fear of losing land ownership riglhts. Many indigenous larger land
owners often find that it is easier to earn adequate income from even poorly maintained tree crops
(coffee, rubber, pepper, etc.) and some high value crops such as ginger (Bengkulu province) then
from rice which requires high labor and purchased inputs. Extension workers must therefore be
well trained to deal with this situation. Where possible small demoastration plots would be
established and large landowners should be persuaded to engage share farmers or to lease land,
preferably to experienced rice farmers. Double cropping of rice should always be aimed at to
achieve adequate productivity and incomes.
8.    Existing productive tree crop areas should not be converted to rice fields when satisfactory
yields are sustainable and owners desire to retain them. There are also, however, many
landowners who wish to reduce risk and want to develop part of their holding into rice fields
(sawah). Even this practice should he avoided since irrigation development costs per unit area



- 131 -                                  Annex 8
page 4
increase while rice crops become more vulnerable to pests and diseases. The decision to make
further investments should thus be based on a detailed economic evaluation based on realistic costs
and benefits. In Sulawesi there are many schemes with significant areas of coconuts, often owned
by absentee landlords. Where it is unlikely that these holdings will be intensively cropped, sawah
developmnent should not be encouraged. Rice can be grown successfully between rows of
coconuts on suitable soils (not too permeable) but coconuit yields decline and waterlogged palms
will eventually die.
9.    Transmigrants with familiarity of rice production make ideal settlers in newly developed
irrigation areas. However, also these settlers require substantial guidance from extension workers
and usually need crop production credit to purchase the necessary inputs. It is therefore essential
that extension workers (PPL's) adhere to their fortnightly schedule of visits to farmer groups
(kelompok), being well supervised by PPUP's and technicallv supported by PPS, and establish
adequate demonstration plots with appropriate varieties inputs, and cultural/water management
practices. Availability of credit and inputs through the V llage cooperatives is essential, but this
appears often not possible due to allotment constraints by central authorities or due to non-credit
worthiness as a result of delayed, or non-payment, of debts. During the preparation of ADP's the
services required by the farming community would be thoroughly reviewed and BAPPEDA's
would be asked to assist with making improvements where shortfalls have been identified.
Agicultureserzch
10.   The main crops on irrigation schemes supported under the project are rice and palawija
(non-rice food crops such as corn, sorghum, soybean, peanut, mungbean, cowpea, cassava and
sweet notato). Research for these crops is carried out through the Central Research Institute for
Food Crops (CRIFC) which is one of the major research institutions in the Agency for Agricultural
Research and Development (AARD) within the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA). CRIFC directs
and coordinates the planning, implementation and evaluation of research which is conducted at six
(6) research institutes, 15 research stations and 45 experiment farms. Project provinces in Sumatra
are served by the Sukarami Research Institute for Food Crops (SARIF), in Kalimantan by the
Banjarbaru Research Institute for Food Crops (BARIF); and in Sulawesi by the Maros Research
Institute for Food Crops (MORIF). All regional institutes receive technical and administrative
support from (CRL-C) at its Bogor headquarters. Based on the support of agricultural
development goals for Indonesia CRIFC established research programs in the following areas:
rice, palawija, problem soils, water management, post harvest technologies, agricultural
engineering/farm mechanizadon, farming systems, seed production and technologies, socio-
economics and environmental impacts, biotechnology and germplasm. Each research program has
a research coordinator, directly responsible to the Director of CRIFC, and each coordinator works
closely with the director and staff of the research institute. More fundamental research is now
carried out at the Bogor Research Institute for Food Crops (BRIFC). Many of its programs are in
support of regional research activities.
11.   SARIF, located near Sukarami near West Sumatra has the national mandate in food crops
research on drylands with a wet climate and on high elevation wetland rice. Of a total of 411 staff
some 193 are directly engaged in research programs (6 PhD, 34 MS, 34 IR/DRS and 5 BS). One
research station is located at Sitiung (W. Sumatra) where problems of acid podsolic soils are
analyzed and which are highly relevant to several proposed irrigation schemes under this project,
when further expanded for irrigated rice research. There are another 6 experimental farms
(4 in West Sumatra, one in Aceh and one in Lampung provinces. It is proposed that SARIF staff
will evaluate long term suitability for crop production and make crop production recommendations
for all irrigation schemes proposed for Sumatra provinces which have problem soils (acid podsols,
peat, muck, etc.). BARIF near Banjarbaru - South Kalimantan is mainly geared towards research
on food crops under t;da; swamp and swampy conditions. Most research aims to develop
technologies for rice growing on peat soil, soils with high salinity and acidity, aluminum toxicity,
poor drainage, etc. There are 253 staff of which 183 are directly involved in research programs
(2 PhD, 14 MS, 34 IR/DPS and 3 BS). There are two research stations (Handil Manaray and



- 132 -                            Annex 8
Page
Barabui) and nine experiment farms (5 - South Kalimantan, and one each in Central and East
Kalimantan, and South Sumatra). MORIF in Maros, South Sulawesi, is responsible for food
crops research on dryland areas with a dry climate (<2000 mm rain per year) and is the national
center for research on rice tungro virus. Of 429 staff, some 257 are directly engaged in research (9
PhD, 30 MS, 56 IR/DRS and 7 BS). There are 5 research stations and 10 experimental farms
strategically located throughout Sulawesi, the Maluka's and East Nusa Tengara.
12.   CRIFC maintains strong international relationships with IRRI in the Philippines (with
several key staff seconded to the Sukamandi and Bogor Institutes) and other CGIAR institutions.
The World Bank, FAO, USAID, CIDA, ACIAR, and Japanese and Dutch Governments have
assisted greatly in the development of CRIFC through financial and technical support since the
early 1970s.
13.   Under the project the Director of CRIFC in Bogor would initially dispatch a multi-
disciplinary team of scientists to the provinces of Riau, West Sumatra, Bengkulu, South Sumatra
and Lampung to review with the respective PPCWG in each province, the need for specific
agricultural research on problem soils on schemes which are to be completed under the project.
There are already a range of general recommendations available for ameliorating the acid podsolic
and shallow peat soils and these recommendations need to be verified on several schemes with
problem soils under the project. Schemes with known problem soils in Sumatra would be
evaluated during the second half of 1990, to establish an experimental program and to make
arrangements for actual trial execution over several seasons. The CRIFC agreed that the proposed
trial program could be accommodated within the established work programs if adequate funds
could be made available. If wa dnerefucie agreed that funds for the survey work by CRIFC
personnel in 1990 would be made available from Project Preparation Funds available to DGWRD.
It is estimated that trial programs are initially required in four provinces. Each site would require
three years of experiments, and due to the difficulty of the problems expected, about 5
complimentary trials on each site are needed during each season. Funding for some 240
experiments costing about Rp 1.0 million (US$ 555) each would then be made available from the
project. Total research costs are estimated at Rp 240 million or US$ 135,000 to be made available
to CRIFC on the basis of approved seasonal work programs, and detailed seasonal reports of
achievements. Resulting crop production recommendations would be made available to the various
PRAS for use and demonstration by provincial subject matter specialists (PPS) and extension
agents (PPL) on schemes. Where necessary, additional trials would be carried out within
Kalimantan and Sulawesi schemes, on advice of the Coordinating Agriculturist and following
review with the Director of CRIFC.
Agricultural Extension
14.   In early 1984 the Agency for Agricultural Education, Training, and Extension (AAETE)
was given overall responsibility for coordinating agricultural extension services at the national level
and for formulating extension policies and methodologies. Each of the four Directorates General
(Food Crops, Estate Crops, Animal Husbandry, and Fisheries) have a Directorate of Extension,
responsible for exerting technical and operational control over extension staff and in formulating
extcnsion programs to support the specific commodity production programs. The administration
of agricultural extension personnel for all subsectors, and for synchronizing the provision of
inputs, credit, marketing and cooperative development is the responsibility of the BIMAS Directing
Board Secretariat. BIMAS is not only responsible for personnel administration and salaries, etc.,
but also for the overall administration of the Rural Extension Centers (REC) constructed under the
Bank-financed extension projects (Loans 1267-IND, Credit 996-IND and Loan 2748-IND).
15.   At each of the REC's there are now a REC Head and 4 agricultural extension programmers
(supervisors or PPUP) usually one for each of the Directorates General (DINAS). All REC Heads
are responsible to the Bupati (District Administrator) through the BIMAS District Executive Unit
for carrying out the agreed extension programs. Technical support is provided by Subject Matter
Specialists (PPS) based at provincial or district level. PPS's are also mostly responsible for



- 133 -                            Annex 8
Page6
technical training of PPUP's and PPL's.

Irrigaj.on development would be completed on some 120 schemes over a 5-year period for a total
area of about 200,000 ha irrigable land. At a ratio of one PPL to 450 farm families and an average
irrigated farm size of 1.0 ha the number additional PPL's required is about 150 (in 1990 some 306
PPL's were already assigned to schemes). No incremental PPUP's or PPS's are needed since
these are already provided at RECs and in provinces respectively (some vacancies will be
filled when staff becomes available). In isolated areas, where no rental housing is available about
100 PPL's would be provided with a house which would also serve as his/her office and farmer
contact place. Where distances are excessive, about 150 PPL's would be provided with a
100 cc motorcycle or scooter. For supervision and technical support of PPL's in scheme areas,
each of the 13 PRAS's in project provinces would be provided with a 4-wheel drive vehicle (also
for use by the Coordinating Agriculturist and for delivering inputs for trials and demonstration
plots).
17.   Extension Methodology. Since 1985 the Training and Visit (T&V) extension methodology
was gradually introduced throughout all provinces, initially for food crop production only. Each
PPL was to establish 16 farmer groups based on field neighborhoods (in contrast to village
groups) which are visited one morning or afternoon per fortnight. During these field visits the
PPL meets with the selected contact farmers and as many progressive and ordinary farmers as
possible. Demonstration plots or farms were to be established were new technologies should be
introduced to increase productivity. Under the latest NAEP (Loan 2748-IND reformulated in
1988) it was agreed to appoint 1 PPL for 400 farm families in Outer Islands (thus all project
provinces), and that PPL's would be trained and work as multi-disciplinary agents, supported and
supervised by PPUP's in the various disciplines and PPS's in provinces and in some districts at
RECs. Budgeting constraints, however, make only a ratio of 1 PPL to 450 farmers possible. All
PPL's, assigned to 16 farmer groups each, would now deal with all aspects of crop, animal and
fishery production at the farm/enterprise unit.
18.   The basic T&V system appears to work satisfactorily in the mostly homogeneous food crop
(mainly rice) areas. PPL's are generally adequately trained and familiar with standard production
methodologies for rice and palawija crops. However, there remain too few demonstration plots,
and in schemes with problem soils PPL's are as yet not adequately familiar with soil amelioration



- 134 -                             Annex 8
Page 7
and crop rotation techniques which lead to satisfactory sustainable yields. Low salaries and low
staff morale mitigate against high work ethics and discipline. Many PPL's do not adhere to the set
schedule of visits to fanner groups. Therefore PPL's should be given incentives to allow them to
work full-time and efficiently. It may be possible to piovide them with some village land, which
they should develop as demonstration farms. Additionally, PPUP's must provide closer
supervision and greater support to the PPL's in their field work.
19.   To ensure that appropriate and cost effective crop production methodologies are generated
and then disseminated to the farming community, a research program to be executed on schemes
with problem soils, and by personnel of Research Institutes, would be introduced and funded
under the project (para. 13). Results from multi-seasonal trials would then be reviewed for
technical and economic viability and presented as a package of recommendations (variety, method,
and time of establishment, fertilizer levels and times of application, crop husbandry and water-
management practices, and possibly crop rotations) for demonstration by PPL's on 0.1 ha plots (2
per PPL) on farms within schemes or on a whole farm area of about 1.0 ha (1 per PPL). Costs for
inputs on demonstration plots and farms would be financed under the project. Demonstration plots
and farms would become focal points during fortnightly visits of PPL's to the area. A field-day
would be arranged close to crop maturity to demonstrate the benefits of the applied technologies, in
comparison with adjacent farmer managed crops, to the local farming community in the hope that
they would adopt the profitable technologies.
20.   Village Cooperatives or Koperasi Unit Desa (KUD) were in April 1989 proclaimed as the
sole distributors of inputs and are to play a key role in bolstering the village economy in the future.
The present situation is, however, far from satisfactory since most lack the capital basis to provide
inputs and loans (Kredit Usaha Tani (KUT) or Candak Kulak) for purchasing crop production
inputs and small implements and equipment. Many KUD's have defect hullers or rice mills and
there is often a significant debt repayment problem. Additionally, in the more isolated areas
KUD's are located too far from production areas for ease of acquiring critical inputs (e.g.,
insecticides and rodenticides) in a timely manner. It is therefore recommended that the
cooperatives in or near the irrigation schemes under the project are strengthened both managerially
and financially, and with possibly a depot system for outlying areas. The BAPPEDA working
groups in each province, with the assistance of the consultant agriculturist and the representative of
the Department of Cooperatives and Bank Rakyat Indonesia (BRI) would review the present status
of KUD's in project schemes and propose changes in operating procedures and financing during
the first project year. A report on the operation of KUD's and its associated credit system with
recommendations for improvements would be forwarded by each of Lie project provinces to the
national level, with copy to the Bank, for review and remedial actions by December 31, 1991. It
should be assured that adequate crop production credit and inputs are made available to eligible
farmers and that adequate and timely arrangements for acquiring inputs and/or services are made
for tenants and share farmers. If insufficient fertilizers and agro-chemicals are used production
targets could not be to achieved for economic sustainability of a number of schemes.
21.   Credit and Inp . BIMAS continues to make available crop production credit packages for
irrigated rice and also for a range of palawija crops. For the 1990 crop season, there are 4 INSUS
packages (A,B,C and D) available (Table 2) ranging in cost from RP 167,500 to RP 312,750 ha
for individual farmers. Package A provides for 250 Kg/ha fertilizer (150 Kg urea and 100 Kg/ha
TSP), while Packages C and D provide for 575 Kg/ha (250 Kg urea, 12 Kg triple super
phosphate, 100 Kg ammonium sulphate and 100 Kg/ha potassium chloride). For groups of
farmers with a contiguous area of at least 50 ha, group credit is available for the Supra Insus
Package, at rates equivalent to Package D. The economic justification for using these high fertilizer
rates must be clearly established through comparative trials before these can be recommended on
schemes. However, there are seasonal upper limits of crop production credit available to each of
the KUD's and there are many, apparently eligible, farmers (without a history of bad debts) whom
cannot avail of these inputs at subsidized prices through the credit program. In various areas there
are also sharecroppers or tenants without collateral whom are also unable to obtain credit. The
provision of groun credit facilities, with repayments in kind, should be explored under the project



- 135-                            Aex8
Page 8
for this category of producers. Credit eligibility can be recommended by the kepala desa (village
head) or by the leader of the kelompok tani (farmer group established for extension activities).
Interest rates charged by KUD's in 1990 amount to 16 percent over an 8 month period of which
the KUD repays 9 percent to BRI and retains a 7 percent mark-up for administering the loans.
Wealthier farmers purchase inputs for cash and there are usually various informal credit sources in
the community charging even higher interest rates, but which are not necessarily excessive for the
services and convenience provided.
22.   Seed Production. Rice, as a self-pollinating crop, can fairly easily maintain adequate purity
if farmers are advised to rogue their fields and to separately harvest, dry and store the seed
intended for planting the next season. Most varieties have little or no dormancy and can be planted
within a few weeks of harvesting and drying. The main reason for purchasing certified seed is to
either retain purity or to change variety. The latter reason has been very important in Indonesia due
to break-down in resistance to diseases, in particular vector-borne diseases such as tungro
(transmitted by brown plant hoppers) and also fungal diseases. Within the range of input packages
available for rice and also for palawija crops (Tables 2 and 3) a quantity of certified seed of a
recommended crop variety is included. There are adequate seed farms for foundation and certified
seed production in most areas. Under the project about 5 new seed farms in isolated provinces
would be established if, following detailed review by PRAS's in conjunction with the
Coordinating Agriculturists, these are deemed necessary. For all other schemes, contract growing
arrangements would be adopted to produce extension seed. Rice varieties on these contract farms
would be inspected for purity and freedom of harmful weeds by seed inspectors (BPSB) stationed
in districts. Rice seed certific ion costs in 1990 are RP 1500/ha and there is a RP 2/Kg fee for
laboratory tests. Certified set. is sold at a premium and this should be established at a level that
makes seed growing attractive to efficient farners, while still retaining its attractiveness to farmners
to replace their seed when needed for purity, or change of variety reasons. The seeds farms to be
established under the project should become self-supporting. Seed prices in 1990 are: stock seed
Rp 700/Kg, extension seed Rp 500/Kg, farmer seed (not certified) Rp 400/Kg and commercial
paddy Rp 275/Kg. There is usually inadequate seed or planting material available for palawija
crops. Where these crops become important on irrigation schemes supported under the project,
special arrangements for the production of high quality seed should be made through local efforts,
initiated by PRAS with the assistance of the Coordinating Agriculturist.
23.   Integrted Pest Management (IPM). It is more and more realized that relying entirely on
chemicals for control of insects in crops results in the evolution of pesticide-resistant insects, while
these new pest bio-types speed the breakdown of plant resistance. 'ndonesia, with IRRI technical
assistance and supported under USAID funding, has initiated a significant effort in training staff,
farmer leaders and farmers in the use of appropriate cultural practices and biological control
methods. In each provincial BIMAS office there are now IPM specialists, and pest observers
based in districts or at REC's (PPHamat) for training extension staff and for establishing/verifying
insect threshold levels, above which spraying with Govemment-approved pesticides should be
initiated (Indonesia banned in 1986 the use of 57 insecticides). Under the IPM concept,
management techniques are promoted to attract helpful insects, spiders, and pathogens. Under the
project all PPL's and at minimum also contact farmers would receive training in the recognition of
insect pests, diseases and vermin, the appropriate threshold levels for the stage of crop
development, above which control is necessary, and the appropriate approved chemical treatments.
24.   Post Harvest Operations. Losses during crop transport, threshing, winnowing, drying and
storage are generally high. Extension workers and fanners would be trained in adopting
appropriate methodologies for loss avoidance, in particular during threshing and storage (mostly
vernin and insects).
25.   Staff and Farmer Training. Staff training would be provided for all newly appointed and
existing PPL's working in irrigation schemes under the project. New appointees, mostly directly
from secondary high schools, require both induction/communication and skill training, apart from
the usual fortnightly training/discussion sessions in REC's. Both induction/communication and



- 136-                             Annex 8
Pagre 9
skill and water management trainings would be conducted at AETE's In-Service Training Centers
(ISTC) which are located in most provinces (except in Central and South-East Sulawesi, but for
which adequate capacity is available in North and South Sulawesi provinces). The
induction/communication training courses would be for 30 days, while skill and water management
training sessions would be for 14 days. Contact Farmers and Water User Association (WUA or
P3A) officials would be trained in all aspects of land clearing, land leveling and sawah
development in classes of 30 at REC's, while Water Masters (Ulu Ulu) and WUA officials would
also be trained in the O&M  of systems and water management on sawah fields. All courses would
be of one week duration. Contact farmers would also be trained at RECs for 3 days in crop
husbandry techniques, input requirements, integrated pest management (IPM), water management,
and post harvest operations.
Other Items 
26.   Estimated Cot. Table 4 of this annex summarizes agricultural requirements and the
estimated base costs at the time of appraisal. Since not all of these items would be provided under
the project in all provinces, the estimated costs of agricultural development by province to be
included under the project would be reduced as summarized in Annex 3.
27.   Rice Yields. Yield statistics are seasonally (twice a year) obtained from crop cuttings.
Extension agents receive every season instructions from Pusat Statistics in Jakarta, through
PRAS's, which farmer fields have been selected on a random basis from within each 500 ha crop
area. After crop establishment, the PPL is required to place a permanent marker in the field where
the crop cutting will be made at harvest time. An area of 2.5 x 2.5 meters is then harvested,
threshed, sun dried and weighed. Plot yields in grams are communicated through PRAS to Pusat
Statistics where results are summarized by sub-district and expressed in quintals per ha (after using
an unknown correction factor). Since the multiplication factor for yields per ha is 1,600, highly
inflated yields are usually reported at RECs, in districts and provinces. However, the corrected
Pusat Statistics data appear realistic. Crop budgets were prepared for each scheme using adjusted
yield data. Present rainfed rice (unhulled or paddy gabah) yields range from about 1.5 to 2.5 t/ha,
while irrigated rice yields range from about 2.5 to 4.5 t/ha depending mostly on natural soil fertility
and input usage. Estimated future yields under the project for fully irrigated areas range from
about 3.5 t/ha (for podsols and peat soils and insufficient use of ameliorants and crop/water
management technologies) to some 5.0 t/ha. Many of the more experienced and financially strong
farmers on good soils should, however, obtain yields of about 6.0 to 6.5 t/ha. Averages are
substantially lower, mostly due to poorer farmers not using the required levels of inputs.
28.   RiLks. Scheme viability may become constrained due to continuing investments for
irrigation/agricultural development on basically unsound schemes (due to soil constraints or the
continued presence of tree crops and/or large landholdings) and because of large prior investments or
commitments made before/during initial construction. The BAPPEDA and line agencies at present
do not have sufficient leverage to achieve the required development targets to satisfactory standards,
and in a timely manner, but not to facilitate the necessary credit and agricultural inputs to achieve
high levels of production. Such inputs include the quality control of sawah development with top
soil retained, soil amelioration where necessary, and arranging group and individual credit for
adequate and timely supplies of fertilizers and other agro-chemicals. At present, extension personnel
are not being adequately supervised and rewarded to establish demonstration plots in a timely manner
to sound technical standards, and to visit farmer groups fortnightly with technically sound and
profitable crop production informnation.
