INDONESIA
PROVINCIAL IRRIGATED AGRICULTUR DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Environmental As,octs
I.     Intoduction. This annex summarizes the findings and recommendations of the appraisal
environmental review of the above mentioned Project. The full report is available in the Project File.
The project as a whole has been assessed as having low environmental impact potential. The main
finding is that the Bank's classification of the project as a Type B project is valid and justifiable. The
project poses minimal environmental risks.
2.    The schemes originally proposed for inclusion under the project were categorized as 'go-ahead'
and 'deferred' and a substantial number of schemes deleted from the project for various reasons
(Annex 5). All schemes have been environmentally screened using Government Department of Public
Works environmental screening guidelines (described below). The principal criterion under these
guidelines is taken as the change in irrigable area which would occur if the proposed development was
to proceed.
A. Environmental Assessment Procedures
3.    The Government of Indonesia (GOI) has made legal provision for environmental impact
assessment, management and monitoring of relevant private and public sector developments through
the AMDAL process. This process is targeted primarily at the identification of potential environmental
effects of projects and the formulation of environmental monitoring and management plans to
ameliorate these potentlal ffects. For the assessment of existing (as opposed to planned) projects, the
regulations make provision for two types of AMDAL report: (i) a PEL (Preliminary Environmental
Evaluation Report or Penyajian Evaluasi Lingkungan); and (ii) an SEL (Environmental Evaluation
Study of Studi Evaluasi Linkungan), which is a more detailed assessment report which is the
equivalent of an AMDAL report for proposed projects.
4.    The Ministerial Decrees which provide guidelines for preparation of PELs and SELs to evaluate
oresent impact of an existing project and identify actions which might be taken to mitigate that impact.
Although not explicitly stated, the most obvious application of these procedures is for periodic review
of environmentall significant projects, in much the same way as technical audits are carried out.
Further, there is no prescribed automatic trigger which implements these studies. The PEL/SEL
approach is not entirely consistent with the Bank's interest which is to evaluate potential impact of
future works which could be financed under a proposed loan programme. The PEL/SEL procedure
assesses effects of events to the present time, while the Bank's interest is in identifying the effect of
planned future activities. In addition, whether a PEL or SEL would be required on an irrigation
scheme is greatly influenced by the Ia1 area of the scheme. The environmental significance of project
is thus a function primarily of the change in irrigable area attributable to the development programmne
and not the total command area of a scheme/project..
5.    Potential environmental effects of an ongoing irrigation scheme would be a function of the way
in which the total scheme may change existing environmental effects. Under present EA procedures,
the total command area of scheme is not been subject to any forn of environmental screening as a
whole by the Government for a variety of reasons. Firstly, there is no procedural requirement to
undertake such a review. Secondly, the AMDAL administration system does not readily apply to
projects like PLADP which require inter-agency coordination. At present, the line agencies involved
function separately at both national and provincial levels, each having their separate budgets. For a
total scheme EA review to take place, at present, each line agency would have to undertake separate



- 141 -                                 Annex 9
Page 2
AMDAL assessments of their particular components of the programme. This does not happen and is
clearly an untenable situation. Thirdly, where a new environmental assessment system is being started
from nothing on a country-wide basis, the highest priority is given to new projects with substantial
environmental impact potential. The proposed project does not fit into this category.
6.    Review of "Go-ahead" Schemes. The 'go-ahead' schemes are listed in Table I in descending
order of change in irrigable area attributable to project development. None of the schemes involves an
increase in functional area greater than 2000 ha. and following Government guidelines, no detailed
EIA would be recommended. Only two schemes, Indrapura and Suliti in West Sumatra, involve
changes in irrigable area of 1600 and 1400 ha. respectively and might be regarded as marginal cases.
All other schemes involve changes in irrigable area of less than 900 ha. Table 1 also specifies which
schemes require clearing of medium or heavy forest, the areas involved and whether nature reserves,
protection forest or production forest might be affected. Only four schemes require forest clearing,
may be located wholly or partly within protection forest and man require some clearing of that forest.
These schemes are Air Eenkenang, Air Nasal Kiri and Air Kedurang (Bengkulu); and Andowengga
(South East Sulawesi) with some 437 ha. of clearing required. The areas involved are not significant
in themselves, but important principles of land management are involved which may become more
apparent under the project when the deferred schemes are also considered.
7 .   In addition, 16 go-ahead schemes require land clearing with about 3000 ha. presently classified
as conversion forest. The need for these schemes is to ensure that these forests are subject of
commercial assessment by the MOF prior to clearing and that measures are taken to recover and sell all
merchantable timber prior to (or during) clearing operations so that the economic benefits of the
proposed schemes are maximized.
8. Review of "Deferred" Schemes. Some 85 of the 'deferred' schemnes are listed in Table 2 in
descending order of marginal increase in irrigable area. The table shows the originally proposed
command areas and modifications (agreed at appraisal) which have reduced the aggregate functional
area by about 20 percent. Some 13 schemes involve an increase in irrigable area of greater than 2000
ha.and, consistent with the Government guidelines (para 4), require specific EIA consideration (as
detailed in the full EA report). Only two of these scheres (Sadang and Maloso) require special
attention (as detailed in Attachment 1 of the full project environment report). Of the remaining deferred
schemes, some 40 involve some potential conflict with forest management or conservation land uses.
The areas involved include: (i) 9500 ha. of clearing of convertible forest for which no apparent
provision has been made for recovery of merchantable timber, (ii) 2000 ha. of clearing (potentially)
within protection forest; (iii) 1500 ha. of clearing potendally within production forest; and (iv) 3500
ha. of forest clearing where the status could not be determined. Thus potential clearing for these
schemes is estimated at about 16000 ha. or 80% of total clearing or all deferred schemes.
9.    The schemes with large command areas are relatively unimportant in terms of these issues.
This highlights the potential cumulative effects of numerous small developments which separately may
have little negative impact, but collectively may be significant. This issue reinforces the need for an
integrated land use planning approach for these types of development in preference to the present EIA
approach for separate schemes.
10.  Overall Environmental Chamcterizadon. There are few schemes which individually have
significant environmental impact potential. A recurring issue relates to the implications of some
schemes for management of the national forest estate, but this can be dealt with through existing
administrative mechanisms. Provision should be made to cover costs incurred by the MOF in
undertaking forest inventories and providing guidance to the Provincial (and National) Coordinating
Working Groups on how to deal with any identified EIA issues.



- 142 -                               Annex 9
page 3
11.   Small-scale irrigation schemes generally have limited environmental impact potential
individually. However, the real problem can be their potential cumulative and induced effect. This
issue cannot be adequately evaluated through a conventional EIA process. Specific environmental
problems raised by small scale irrigation schemes are generally simple and can be dealt with effectively
by specifying and implementing simple mnanagement guidelines. Some simple generic safeguards
could be adopted. For example, no scheme should be approved for funding unless it satisfies
DGWRD's guidelines for establishing an irrigation scheme. Land clearing for irrigation development
should not take place unless the necessary formal clearances are given by MIOF. All construction
contracts should include a soil erosion control specification approved by MOF staff. Extension
programmes should ensure that farmers are adequately informed about safe management procedures
for agricultural chemicals. Finally, the Government should continue its present pesticide and herbicide
control policies and closely monitor the use of agricultural chemicals within irrigation scheme
commnand areas.
12.   A preventative approach based on simple management guidelines is likely to be much more
effective at controlling environmental impacts than the use of expensive consultants to produce
extensive EIA reports. However, comprehensive ETA analyses would be useful for schemes with
special EA problems or in sectorial and regional level studies. Results of such studies can be
converted into practical actions through modification of management guidelines and procedures and
promotion of relevant institutional level responses. Thus the recommended study on the Sadang
Scheme would sponsor such a broader scale environmental analysis to deal with a specific local issue
(downstream fish farming) and in addition lead to useful generic management guidelines.
B. General Environmental Issues
13.   The main generic environmental issues in the irrigation subsector relate to land use management
issues and environmental control. This section describes these issues as they relate to the project and
to provides some recommendations which should be taken into account in the planning and
development of future irrigation subsector developments.
14.   Land Use Management. Indonesia, like virtually every other country, faces a growing and
major problem in equitably distributing its national land resources between the four main competing
land uses - agriculture, forestry and forest based industries, urban/industrial and conservation. In this
respect, the Outer Islands are under substantially less land use pressure than Java, but pressure is
growing and is likely to increase substantially in the future. One shortcoming at present is that the
country does not have an effective land use management control system with a range of disparate
approaches being formulated within various ministries. Government should take appropriate action
since as land use pressures increase, the need for effective land use management is bound to increase.
In this respect, inter-departmental cooperation being encouraged. Under the project, the national and
provincial working groups should include specialists from the MOF. These specialists could then
provide advice on potential forest management and conservation conflicts and on soil erosion control.
15.  Eos Conseryation  d Magmna Isiiue. Direct forest conservation conflicts with land
development where land scheduled for clearing or development lies within forested land gazetted, or
when recommended to be gazetted for conservation purposes by the Directorate of Forest Protection
and Conservation (PHPA) within the Ministry of Forestry. For the project assessment, a forest was
assumed to be a conservation forest if designated as such on relevant land use and land system maps of
Indonesia (the "RePPProT" maps, see 1/ as either an actual or planned reserve. The three categories of
such land defined in the RePPProT study are:
I/    Land Resources Department/Bina Program (1988) "Review of Phase 1 Results, Sulawesi"
from: Physical Planning PnraoM     fx Tranimigmtiga fMSftI. Land Resources



- 143 -                                 Annex 9
Page 4
(i)  land which has been officialy gazetted as reserves either for conservation (national
parks, nature reserves, game reserves, recreation reserves, hunting reserves) or for
watershed protection;
(ii)  land which had been aged interally by the PHPA and MOF to gazette the land and,
at the time of preparation of the RePPProT maps, was awaiting official ratification; and
(iii) land which was oposed for reserve status but was still under consideration within
PHPA and may or may not have gone forward for eventual ratification.
However, there appears to be numerous uncertainties regarding the boundaries of these reserves and
the provinces, uncertainties as to their location and what they actually signify. The only arbiter on the
issue can be MOF which has the final responsibility in this area. Thus the project supports an
environmertal protection component (para 3.14, Main Report), to be implemented through Tthe
Directorate of Reforestation and Land Rehalibilitation (DGRLR).
16.   The potential for indirec foe conservation conflicts arises where marginally economic
schemes, particularly those in remote areas, are located near forest reserves. Several provincial
foresters reported the spill-over effects of remote schemes (and other remote area population
development programmes) - i.e. where farmers cannot support their families from an irrigation scheme
and turn to the adjacent forests for supplementary income, either through timber poaching or slash and
burn agriculture. Clearly, controlling such incursions is virtually impossible where supervisory
manpower is limited and access is difficult. One possible solution would be only to develop economic
and agricultural viable schemes only where farmers want to farm and can meet their family needs from
within the scheme command area. A supplementary control would be to maintain a suitable buffer
zone of production forest between the schieme and nearest forest reserve.
18.   With regard to foe  management, the project review raises two issues: (i) potential for direct
conflicts between irrigation development and management of production forests; and (ii) wider need to
optimize recovery of commercial forest resources when approved forest clearing is to occur (para 19).
Potential for production forest management conflicts arises where schemes or parts of schemes
requiring land clearance may fall within areas which are designated by MOF as limited or normal
production forests. The only permissible land use within these areas is selective logging under the
direction of MOF which would preclude clearance of these forests to create irrigable land. However, it
is possible to vary production forest boundaries based on land swops. This may offer a potential
solution to this form of potential conflict, but would a substantial amount of time since it is a
complicated process. It would be easier to avoid the problem in the first plare by resolving the issue at
the project feasibility stage.
19.   Resource Cost Recovery. The issue arises where, it is proposed to clear "convertible" forest
as part of an irrigation scheme (i.e. forest which has been classified as being suitable for clearance for
non-forest uses). There is a commonly held view that because a forest was classified as convertible, it
contained no commercial timber. In many cases, this is not the case. It appears that many convertible
forests are only so classified because they had been identified as having potential for irrigation or other
development when the land classification boundaries (the so-called "consensus boundaries") were
drawn up in the early 1980s. However, several convertible forests within project scheme commands
appear to contain substantial quantities of merchantable timber. For instance, provincial forestry
officers estimated that conversion forest yields in several schemes in three Sumatra provinces ranged
from 10 to 70 cubic meters per hectare. Depending on species involved, recovery costs and markets,
Department, ODNRI, Overseas Development Administration, London, U.K.; and, Direktorat Bina
Program, DG Penyiapan Pemukian, Depn. Transmigrasi, Jakarta, Indonesia.



-144-                                   Annex 2
,Page S
the gross value of this timber could far outweigh land clearing and development costs. It appeared that
no attempt is being made to recover this merchantable timber prior to or during clearing. This
represents a triple loss to Indonesia since irrigation development costs are inflated, MOF loses royalty
payments; and pressure increases on resources in gazetted production forests. I'his is an not a
desirable situation and greater involvement of MOF would alleviate the problem.
20.   Soil Erogion. Soil erosion is not normally a problem with irrigated agriculture, since it is a
conservative land use practice. However, associated civil works can often cause localized problems
particularly on schemes where soils were marginal. For example, some schemes have major erosion
of weir abutments and widespread gully and sheet erosion associated with the major civil works (main
and secondary canals and borrow pits). The erosion products frequently end up in the canals,
reducing their capacity and increasing maintenance costs. Most erosion problems on project schemes
would have been avoided if suitable erosion control procedures been included in the design and
implemented in construction and clearing contracts.
21.   Catchnent ManagemenL Where a significant investment is to be made in an irrigation
development, all reasonable steps should be taken to protect the basic resource base, i.e. to maintain
the quality and qu:antity of catchment yields. The most effective way to do this is to dedicate the direct
catchment of the scheme, wherever possible, as protection forest (Hutan Lindung) or an equally
conservative use. This may not always be possible due to existing upstream land use pattems. In this
respect, the Governor of Bengkulu Province is expected to issue a decree to this effect for all
provincial irrigation schemes. Other Provinces could usefully follow this lead.
22.   Downstream Effects. Potential downstream effects for run-of-river schemes include periodic
loss of minimum flows for downstream consumers (either human or plants/animals) and chemical
pollution. The potential for chemical pollution due to herbicides/pesticides or fertilizers in tail waters is
the more critical. In this respect, most project schemes are believed to pose little or no threat. The
Sadang and Maloso schemes however illustrate the potentially important nature of this type of problem
under certain circumstances. In both schemes, there is a close and dependant relationship between two
different water uses. The suture financial viability of the downstreamn fish farmers depend on the
quality of their water supply. A different, but equally significant situation could arise where the
proportion of catchment dedicated to irrigation is high and the recipient is a completely or
comiparatively closed hydrological system. It is understood that this 4ituation applies with the Bolango
Bionga Irrigation System in North Sulawesi. The recipient is Limt o Lake, which drains into the
Bone River. These and similar situations can be avoided if due camr is taken in system planning.
However, this requires the active participation of relevant environmental specialists during scheme
formulation rather than later, when the task- is to prepare an AMDAL report on an already designed
(and partly implement,l) project.
23.   On the more general question of levels of pesticide and herbicide residues in the environment as
they relate to irrigation projects, the only practical approach at present is avoidance. Government
should approve "environmentally safe" chemicals (comparatively low toxicity, short environmental
residence times and lack of persistent degradation products) and extension workers should encourage
farners to use only prescribed application rates. Attachmnent 5 of the full EA report (project file)
provides a review of the toxicological properties of these chemicals. All of but one were from the
carbamate family of chemicals which are widely considered as being environmentally safe. The
exception was an insectistatic - an anti-moulting or growth inhibiting agent which is also
environmentally benign. Monitoring of residues is not practical at present due to a lack of laboratories
in Indonesia who can do repeatable determinations of trace contaminations and sample collection
difficulties.
24.   Environmental Institutional Capabilities. Substantial progress had been made in managing the
AMDAL process to date. Technical teams have been formed in the main ministries, AMDAL



-145 -                                Annex9
Page 6
Comrnissions are in place, sorne staff at least have attended AMDAL A and/or B courses and, in
several cases, people with post graduate qualifications in environmental studies are involved.
However, where project management and administration needs to be inter-agency, the AMDAL
process is not effective because it is almost entirely an intra-departmental process. In addition, an EIA
approach may not be effective in dealing with the types of problems raised by small scale irrigation
sector developments. An "environmental planning" approach would produce greater dividends for
small to medium scale irrigation schemes. Technical specialists involved in scheme formulation should
ensure that major land use conflicts of the type highlighted in this Annex are avoided, and during
project preparation ensure that potential environmental problems su,. as soil erosion are anticipated.
25.    The immediate need under the project is for participadon of a forestry officer or officers on
the national and provincial coordinating working groups from DGRLR and the Provincial Forestry
Services respectively. The responsibilities would be to locate boundaries of reserved land or
production forest in the vicinitv of proposed and schemes and identify whether schemes intrude into
these areas. The forestry officer would assist in redesign of schemes to avoid such intrusions or
advise whether the MOF would countenance them. In addition, he would organize changing of
boundaries as may be required and agreed upon by the Department including idendfication of possible
land swops, resurvey of boundaries and all associated administrative procedures since gazetted forests
can only be de-gazetted by authority of the MOF. Where any forest is to be cleared, the forester would
organize timber inventories and prepare contract documentation covering the harvesting and sale of this
timber and payment of relevant royalties to MOF. Finally, he would review soil reports on the project,
identify potential for erosion problems and, as required, to ensure that adequate soil erosion control
provisions are included in contract documents and applied in the field.

Annex I
INDONESIA
PROVINCL4L IRRIGATED AGRICULTUJRE DEVELOPME-NT PROJEC
Project Suoervision Requirements
1.    Pject Supcnrsion. The Bank staff resources to supervise the project would be greater than
supervision resources (say 10 staff weeks per year) for a typical specific investment operation. The
supervision arrangements for the proposed project would be complex for both Government and the
Bank because of the geographical spread, the number of schemes and components, and the range of
challenges being addressed and the need to provide project implementation and performance data
essential to the supervision process. In this respect, an important function of the consultant services
described in Annex 4 would be to guide and assist Govemment in project supervision and to
strengthen the capability of the individual agencies involved to monitor and evaluate project
implementation. However even with the consultant's input, the project could require above average
Bank supervision resources and that it may be more effective to supervise the proposed project through
RSI, (Jakarta Resident Mission) The recommended supervision requirements are summarized below.
2.    Suirmision Arrangcnie. The recommended arrangement (after the first supervision
mission) would be for the Bank to supervise the project through the resident mission staff with two
missions each year. The main mission would be May/June each year and constitute the annual
implementation review. During this mission, appropriate action would be taken to review and aniend
the annual work program for the following fiscal year (to match the budgetary process). The second
inisinn would take p'lace about in November/December each year. An important task would be to
review the budgetary request based on the work program agreed during the previous mission.
Between the two supervision missions, the day-to-day supervision would be undertaken by RSI staff
based in Jakarta. The first supervision mission (Juine/July 1991) would require extra staff resources to
cover the start-up of the project and because all deferred schemes will have to be reviewed. Thus the
first supervision mission should, scheduling permitted, include headquarters staff who have been
involved in the project preparation and preferably who took part in the appraisal mission.
3.    ProposedSupervision Resources Required.
Activity                 Staffing                       Staff Weeks
